1. **The "halfway" attractor:** The answer that results from doing half the work. If the problem asks for $2x$ and you solve for $x$, the value of $x$ will appear as an attractor.

2. **The "sign error" attractor:** The answer that results from getting a sign wrong. If the correct answer is $-5$, the attractor might be 5.

3. **The "reversal" attractor:** The answer that results from reversing the order of a subtraction or division. If the problem asks for "5 less than $x$" and you compute $5 - x$ instead of $x - 5$, the result will appear as an attractor.

4. **The "unit confusion" attractor:** The answer that results from using the wrong units. If the problem gives you measurements in inches but asks for the answer in feet, the un-converted value will appear as an attractor.

5. **The "successive change" attractor:** For problems involving successive percent changes, the attractor is often the simple sum or difference of the percents (e.g., "20% increase followed by 20% decrease = no change" — the attractor is 100%).

6. **The "partial information" attractor:** The answer that results from using only some of the given information. If a problem gives you two conditions and you only use one, the answer you get will appear as an attractor.

**The defense against attractors is simple but requires discipline: always re-read the question stem after solving, before selecting your answer.** Verify that you are answering the question that was actually asked, not a related question that would be easier to answer.

### The "Answer the Question Asked" Protocol

This deserves its own section because it is the single most common source of lost points on the ACT Math section. The protocol is:

1. **Before solving:** Identify exactly what the question is asking. Underline it if necessary.
2. **After solving:** Re-read the question stem and verify that your answer matches what was asked.
3. **Before bubbling:** Check one final time that you are selecting the answer choice that corresponds to the quantity the question asks for.

This takes 3–5 seconds and can save you from the most frustrating type of error: getting the right mathematical result but selecting the wrong answer because you solved for the wrong variable.

### The "Error Log" System

While not a test-day strategy, maintaining an error log during your preparation is one of the most effective ways to improve your score. For every question you miss on a practice test or problem set, record:

1. **The question topic and type** (e.g., "quadratic equations — finding the vertex")
2. **What you did wrong** (e.g., "used the wrong formula for the vertex; used $x = b/2a$ instead of $x = -b/2a$")
3. **The correct approach** (e.g., "the $x$-coordinate of the vertex is $-b/2a$, not $b/2a$")
4. **A short fix** (e.g., "remember: the vertex formula has a negative sign")

Review your error log regularly. The goal is to **eliminate repeat mistakes** — these are the easiest points to gain because you already have the knowledge; you just need to fix the execution.

### The "Stamina and Focus" Factor

The ACT Math section requires sustained concentration for 50–60 minutes of intense cognitive work. Mental fatigue is real, and it leads to careless errors, slower processing, and poor decision-making in the latter part of the section.

To build stamina:
- **Take full-length practice tests** under realistic conditions (timed, no breaks during the section, no phone, no snacks)
- **Practice at the same time of day** as your actual test
- **Develop a pre-test routine** that you follow consistently (specific warm-up exercises, mental preparation, breathing techniques)
- **Sleep well** in the days leading up to the test (not just the night before)
- **Eat a balanced meal** before the test — not too heavy (which causes drowsiness) and not too light (which causes energy crashes)

### The "Mindset" Framework

Your mental state during the test has a measurable impact on your performance. The optimal mindset is:

- **Confident but not overconfident.** You know the material, you have a system, and you trust it. But you are not so overconfident that you rush through problems without reading carefully.
- **Calm but not complacent.** You are alert and focused, but not anxious. A moderate level of arousal actually improves performance (the Yerkes-Dodson law), but excessive anxiety impairs working memory and decision-making.
- **Flexible but not scattered.** You are willing to abandon a strategy that is not working and try a different approach, but you are not constantly second-guessing yourself or switching strategies mid-problem.
- **Process-oriented, not outcome-oriented.** During the test, your focus should be on executing your system correctly for each problem, not on worrying about your final score. The score takes care of itself if the process is right.

---

## Integrating the Systems: A Comprehensive Walkthrough

To illustrate how all of these strategies work together, let's walk through a hypothetical section using the complete system.

**Question 1:** *What is the value of $3(4 + 2)^2 - 5$?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — straightforward arithmetic
- **Strategy:** Direct computation
- **Execution:** $3(6)^2 - 5 = 3(36) - 5 = 108 - 5 = 103$
- **Time:** 15 seconds

**Question 2:** *A shirt is on sale for 25% off its original price of $48. What is the sale price?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — basic percent problem
- **Strategy:** PIN or direct computation
- **Execution:** 25% of 48 = 12. Sale price = $48 - 12 = \$36$. (Or: 75% of 48 = 0.75 \times 48 = 36$.)
- **Time:** 20 seconds

**Question 3:** *If the points $(2, 5)$ and $(6, y)$ lie on a line with slope $\frac{3}{2}$, what is the value of $y$?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — slope formula
- **Strategy:** Direct computation
- **Execution:** $\frac{y - 5}{6 - 2} = \frac{3}{2} \Rightarrow \frac{y - 5}{4} = \frac{3}{2} \Rightarrow y - 5 = 6 \Rightarrow y = 11$
- **Time:** 30 seconds

**Question 4:** *A circle has a radius of 5. What is the length of an arc that subtends a central angle of 72°?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — circle geometry
- **Strategy:** Proportional reasoning
- **Execution:** $\frac{72°}{360°} = \frac{1}{5}$ of the circle. Circumference = $2\pi(5) = 10\pi$. Arc length = $\frac{10\pi}{5} = 2\pi$.
- **Time:** 25 seconds

**Question 5:** *Which of the following is the solution to the inequality $-3x + 7 \geq 22$?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — basic inequality
- **Strategy:** Direct computation, with attention to sign reversal
- **Execution:** $-3x \geq 15 \Rightarrow x \leq -5$ (remember to flip the inequality when dividing by a negative)
- **Time:** 20 seconds

**Question 6:** *A rectangular garden has a length that is 3 feet more than twice its width. If the perimeter is 66 feet, what is the area of the garden?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — word problem, linear equation
- **Strategy:** Translation Protocol
- **Execution:** Let $w$ = width. Length = $2w + 3$. Perimeter: $2w + 2(2w + 3) = 66 \Rightarrow 2w + 4w + 6 = 66 \Rightarrow 6w = 60 \Rightarrow w = 10$. Length = 23. Area = $10 \times 23 = 230$.
- **Time:** 45 seconds

**Question 7:** *If $f(x) = 2x^2 - 3x + 1$, what is $f(-2)$?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — function evaluation
- **Strategy:** Direct substitution
- **Execution:** $f(-2) = 2(-2)^2 - 3(-2) + 1 = 2(4) + 6 + 1 = 8 + 6 + 1 = 15$
- **Time:** 20 seconds

**Question 8:** *In triangle ABC, angle A = 50° and angle B = 70°. What is the measure of the exterior angle at C?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — triangle exterior angle
- **Strategy:** Exterior angle theorem
- **Execution:** Interior angle C = $180° - 50° - 70° = 60°$. Exterior angle at C = $180° - 60° = 120°$. (Or: exterior angle = sum of remote interior angles = $50° + 70° = 120°$.)
- **Time:** 15 seconds

**Question 9:** *A bag contains 4 red marbles, 3 blue marbles, and 5 green marbles. If one marble is selected at random, what is the probability that it is NOT blue?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — basic probability
- **Strategy:** Complement rule
- **Execution:** Total marbles = 12. P(blue) = $\frac{3}{12} = \frac{1}{4}$. P(not blue) = $1 - \frac{1}{4} = \frac{3}{4}$.
- **Time:** 15 seconds

**Question 10:** *The population of a town increased from 12,000 to 15,000 over 5 years. What was the average rate of increase per year?*

- **Categorization:** "Now" — rate problem
- **Strategy:** Direct computation
- **Execution:** Total increase = 3,000. Per year = $\frac{3,000}{5} = 600$ people per year.
- **Time:** 15 seconds

After these 10 questions, you have spent approximately 3–4 minutes and have "banked" 1–2 minutes compared to the average pace. This banked time is now available for the harder questions ahead.

Now consider a harder question:

**Question 25:** *If $x^2 - 5x - 14 = 0$, which of the following is a possible value of $x$?*
>
> A) $-7$
> B) $-2$
> C) $2$
> D) $5$

- **Categorization:** "Now" — quadratic equation
- **Strategy:** Back-solving (PITA)
- **Execution:** Test B) $-2$: $(-2)^2 - 5(-2) - 14 = 4 + 10 - 14 = 0$. It works.
- **Time:** 15 seconds (faster than factoring or using the quadratic formula)

Or consider a question that requires PIN:

**Question 30:** *If $a$ is an odd integer and $b$ is an even integer, which of the following must be odd?*
>
> A) $a + b$
> B) $ab$
> C) $a + 2b$
> D) $2a + b$

- **Categorization:** "Later" — abstract number theory
- **Strategy:** PIN
- **Execution:** Let $a = 3$ (odd), $b = 4$ (even).
  - A) $3 + 4 = 7$ (odd) ✓
  - B) $3 \times 4 = 12$ (even) ✗
  - C) $3 + 8 = 11$ (odd) ✓
  - D) $6 + 4 = 10$ (even) ✗

  Both A and C work. Let's try $a = 5$, $b = 2$:
  - A) $5 + 2 = 7$ (odd) ✓
  - C) $5 + 4 = 9$ (odd) ✓

  Both still work. Let's try $a = 1$, $b = 2$:
  - A) $1 + 2 = 3$ (odd) ✓
  - C) $1 + 4 = 5$ (odd) ✓

  Both A and C are always odd. This suggests we need to look more carefully. Actually, $a + b$ = odd + even = odd (always), and $a + 2b$ = odd + even = odd (always). So both A and C are always odd. In a real test scenario, if PIN produces two matches, you should re-examine your number choices or look for a subtlety in the expressions. This particular example illustrates that **no strategy is infallible** — PIN can occasionally produce ambiguous results, and you must be prepared to try different numbers or switch to a different strategy. The key is to have multiple strategies in your toolkit and to be flexible in deploying them.

---

## Summary: The Strategic Solver's Checklist

For every problem on the ACT Math section, the strategic solver's mind works through the following checklist:

1. **Read the problem** (using the Translation Protocol — read twice, underline key information)
2. **Categorize** (Now / Later / Never)
3. **If Now:** Identify the most efficient strategy (direct computation, back-solving, PIN, estimation, etc.)
4. **Execute** the strategy
5. **Verify** that you are answering the question actually asked
6. **Check** for attractor answers
7. **Bubble** the answer
8. **Move on** (without lingering)

For the section as a whole:

1. **Pass 1:** Answer all "Now" questions, flag "Later" and "Never" questions
2. **Pass 2:** Return to "Later" questions, prioritized by confidence and tractability
3. **Pass 3:** Guess strategically on "Never" questions, verify no blanks remain
4. **Throughout:** Monitor time, maintain composure, trust the system

This system is not something you read about once and master. It is something you practice, refine, and internalize over weeks and months of preparation. Every practice test is an opportunity to practice the system. Every missed question is an opportunity to improve the system. By test day, the system should feel like second nature — a set of automatic habits that free your conscious mind to focus on the mathematics.

The students who score 30+ on the ACT Math section are not necessarily the ones who know the most math. They are the ones who have the best systems, the most disciplined habits, and the most efficient use of their time. This chapter has given you those systems. The rest is practice.

---

*In the next chapter, we will apply these strategic systems to each major content area — Algebra, Geometry, Trigonometry, Statistics & Probability, and Functions — showing how the general strategies from this chapter are adapted and optimized for specific types of problems.*

---


# Chapter 3: Algebra and Linear Functions Master Reference — Formulas, Properties, and Graph Analysis

---

## 3.0 — Overview and Strategic Importance

Algebra and Linear Functions constitute the single largest reporting category on the ACT Mathematics section, accounting for approximately **12–15%** of all questions directly, and an even larger share indirectly — since linear reasoning undergirds problems in geometry, functions, data analysis, and modeling. When combined with the "Functions" reporting category (another 12–15%), linear and algebraic concepts collectively represent roughly **one-third** of the entire exam.

This chapter serves as the definitive, exhaustive reference for every algebraic and linear concept the ACT tests. Every formula, property, graph interpretation technique, common trap, and strategic shortcut is catalogued here. Mastery of this chapter is non-negotiable for any score above 23, and deep fluency here is what separates 28+ scorers from the rest.

The ACT tests algebra and linear functions not merely as computational exercises but as reasoning tools. You will be asked to interpret slopes in context, translate word problems into systems of equations, analyze graphs, manipulate algebraic expressions, and solve for unknowns under time pressure. The test rewards both **procedural fluency** (can you execute the steps?) and **conceptual understanding** (do you know *why* those steps work and *when* to apply them?).

---

## 3.1 — The Language of Algebra: Expressions, Equations, and Inequalities

### 3.1.1 — Algebraic Expressions vs. Equations

The ACT frequently tests whether you can distinguish between an **expression** and an **equation**, and this distinction carries strategic weight.

An **algebraic expression** is a combination of numbers, variables, and operation symbols (addition, subtraction, multiplication, division, exponentiation, roots) that represents a value. It has **no equality sign**. Examples:

- $3x + 7$
- $\dfrac{2x^2 - 5x + 1}{x - 3}$
- $\sqrt{x^2 + 4}$

An **equation** is a statement that two expressions are equal, connected by an **equality sign** ($=$). Examples:

- $3x + 7 = 22$
- $\dfrac{2x^2 - 5x + 1}{x - 3} = 0$
- $\sqrt{x^2 + 4} = 5$

**Why this matters for the ACT:** You can **simplify** or **evaluate** an expression, but you **solve** an equation. The ACT frequently presents problems where you must first recognize whether you are being asked to simplify (expression) or solve (equation). Misidentifying which you are dealing with leads to wrong answers.

### 3.1.2 — The Equal Sign and Equation Properties

The equality sign establishes a balance. Whatever operation you perform on one side, you **must** perform on the other to maintain equality. This is the foundational principle of equation solving.

**Properties of Equality:**

| Property | Statement | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Addition Property** | If $a = b$, then $a + c = b + c$ | $x - 4 = 7 \Rightarrow x = 11$ |
| **Subtraction Property** | If $a = b$, then $a - c = b - c$ | $x + 4 = 7 \Rightarrow x = 3$ |
| **Multiplication Property** | If $a = b$, then $a \cdot c = b \cdot c$ | $\dfrac{x}{4} = 7 \Rightarrow x = 28$ |
| **Division Property** | If $a = b$ and $c \neq 0$, then $\dfrac{a}{c} = \dfrac{b}{c}$ | $4x = 7 \Rightarrow x = \dfrac{7}{4}$ |
| **Substitution Property** | If $a = b$, then $a$ can replace $b$ anywhere | If $y = 2x$ and $x = 3$, then $y = 6$ |
| **Reflexive Property** | $a = a$ | Any number equals itself |
| **Symmetric Property** | If $a = b$, then $b = a$ | If $3 = x$, then $x = 3$ |
| **Transitive Property** | If $a = b$ and $b = c$, then $a = c$ | Chain reasoning |

### 3.1.3 — Inequalities: The Critical Differences

Inequalities use the symbols $<$, $>$, $\le$, and $\ge$ instead of $=$. While most equation-solving techniques apply, there is **one critical exception** that the ACT exploits relentlessly:

> **CRITICAL RULE:** When you multiply or divide both sides of an inequality by a **negative number**, you **must reverse** the direction of the inequality symbol.

**Example:**
$$-3x > 12$$
$$x < -4 \quad \text{(Divided by } -3\text{, symbol reversed)}$$

If you forget to reverse the symbol, you get $x > -4$, which is wrong. This is one of the most common trap answers on the entire ACT.

**Inequality vocabulary the ACT uses:**

| Word/Symbol | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| $x > a$ | $x$ is greater than $a$ | $x$ is more than 5 |
| $x < a$ | $x$ is less than $a$ | $x$ is fewer than 5 |
| $x \ge a$ | $x$ is at least $a$ | $x$ is at least 5 |
| $x \le a$ | $x$ is at most / no more than $a$ | $x$ is at most 5 |
| $a < x < b$ | $x$ is between $a$ and $b$ (exclusive) | $x$ is between 3 and 7 |
| $a \le x \le b$ | $x$ is between $a$ and $b$ (inclusive) | $x$ is from 3 to 7, inclusive |

**Graphing inequalities on a number line:**
- **Open circle** at the boundary means the boundary is NOT included ($<$ or $>$)
- **Closed (filled) circle** at the boundary means the boundary IS included ($\le$ or $\ge$)
- **Arrow** points in the direction of the solution set

**Compound inequalities joined by "and":**
$$x > 2 \text{ AND } x < 5$$
This means $x$ must satisfy **both** conditions simultaneously. The solution is the **intersection**: $2 < x < 5$.

**Compound inequalities joined by "or":**
$$x < 2 \text{ OR } x > 5$$
This means $x$ must satisfy **at least one** condition. The solution is the **union**: two separate intervals.

The ACT rarely tests compound inequalities directly, but the logic of "and" vs. "or" appears in domain problems, range problems, and word problems involving constraints.

---

## 3.2 — Linear Equations: The Four Essential Forms

Every line in the coordinate plane can be expressed in multiple algebraic forms. Each form reveals different information at a glance and is optimal for different tasks. The ACT tests your ability to **select the right form** for a given problem and **convert between forms fluently**.

### 3.2.1 — Slope-Intercept Form: $y = mx + b$

This is the **single most important form** on the ACT. If you master only one form, master this one.

**Structure:**
$$y = mx + b$$

- $m$ = **slope** of the line (rate of change, steepness, direction)
- $b$ = **y-intercept** (the y-value where the line crosses the y-axis; this occurs when $x = 0$)

**Why it is the best form for graphing:**
You can immediately identify the y-intercept and plot the point $(0, b)$. Then use the slope to find additional points by rising and running.

**Why it is the best form for interpretation:**
In word problems, the slope represents the **rate of change** (how fast $y$ changes per unit change in $x$), and the y-intercept represents the **starting value** (the value of $y$ when $x = 0$).

**Example interpretation:**
> "A plumber charges a $\$50$ visit fee plus $\$30$ per hour."

$$C = 30h + 50$$

- Slope ($m = 30$): $\$30$ per hour (the rate)
- y-intercept ($b = 50$): $\$50$ visit fee (the starting cost when $h = 0$)

**What the ACT tests with this form:**
- Given a graph, find the slope and y-intercept and write the equation
- Given an equation, identify the slope and y-intercept
- Given a word problem, write the linear model and interpret $m$ and $b$ in context
- Given two points, find the equation of the line

### 3.2.2 — Point-Slope Form: $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$

**Structure:**
$$y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$$

- $m$ = **slope**
- $(x_1, y_1)$ = **any specific point on the line**

**When to use it:**
This form is optimal when you know the **slope** and **one point** on the line — which is exactly what many ACT problems give you. Two points? Find the slope first, then use point-slope form with either point.

**Why it is the best form for modeling:**
When a problem says "a line passes through $(3, 7)$ with slope $2$," you can immediately write:
$$y - 7 = 2(x - 3)$$

Then convert to slope-intercept form if needed:
$$y - 7 = 2x - 6$$
$$y = 2x + 1$$

**Critical nuance:** The point $(x_1, y_1)$ can be **any** point on the line. If you are given two points, either one works. Both will produce equivalent equations (after simplification).

### 3.2.3 — Standard Form: $Ax + By = C$

**Structure:**
$$Ax + By = C$$

- $A$, $B$, and $C$ are typically integers (in "proper" standard form, $A$ is non-negative)
- $A$ and $B$ are **not both zero**

**When the ACT uses this form:**
Standard form appears frequently in word problems involving two quantities that sum to a fixed total, and in systems of equations.

**Example:**
> "Tickets cost $\$8$ for adults and $\$5$ for children. A total of $\$200$ was collected."

$$8a + 5c = 200$$

**Finding the slope from Standard Form:**
The slope of $Ax + By = C$ is:
$$m = -\dfrac{A}{B}$$

This is a **must-know shortcut**. The ACT frequently gives you an equation in standard form and asks for the slope. Rather than converting to slope-intercept form, simply compute $-\dfrac{A}{B}$.

**Derivation (for understanding):**
$$Ax + By = C$$
$$By = -Ax + C$$
$$y = -\dfrac{A}{B}x + \dfrac{C}{B}$$

So $m = -\dfrac{A}{B}$ and the y-intercept is $\dfrac{C}{B}$.

**Finding intercepts from Standard Form:**
- **x-intercept:** Set $y = 0$: $Ax = C \Rightarrow x = \dfrac{C}{A}$
- **y-intercept:** Set $x = 0$: $By = C \Rightarrow y = \dfrac{C}{B}$

### 3.2.4 — Summary: When to Use Each Form

| Form | Best For | Key Information Revealed |
|---|---|---|
| $y = mx + b$ | Graphing, interpretation, quick analysis | Slope ($m$), y-intercept ($b$) |
| $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$ | Writing equations given a point and slope | Point $(x_1, y_1)$, slope ($m$) |
| $Ax + By = C$ | Word problems with two quantities, systems | x- and y-intercepts, slope = $-A/B$ |

### 3.2.5 — Converting Between Forms

**Standard Form → Slope-Intercept Form:**
Solve for $y$.
$$6x + 3y = 18$$
$$3y = -6x + 18$$
$$y = -2x + 6$$

**Slope-Intercept Form → Standard Form:**
Move all variable terms to one side.
$$y = \dfrac{3}{4}x - 5$$
$$-\dfrac{3}{4}x + y = -5$$
Multiply through by 4 to clear fractions:
$$-3x + 4y = -20$$
Multiply by $-1$ to make $A$ positive:
$$3x - 4y = 20$$

**Point-Slope Form → Slope-Intercept Form:**
Distribute the slope and solve for $y$.
$$y - 5 = \dfrac{2}{3}(x + 6)$$
$$y - 5 = \dfrac{2}{3}x + 4$$
$$y = \dfrac{2}{3}x + 9$$

---

## 3.3 — Slope: The Defining Feature of a Line

### 3.3.1 — The Slope Formula

Given two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$:

$$m = \dfrac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1} = \dfrac{\text{rise}}{\text{run}} = \dfrac{\text{change in } y}{\text{change in } x}$$

**Critical details:**
- The order of the points does not matter, as long as you are **consistent**. If you use $y_2 - y_1$ in the numerator, you must use $x_2 - x_1$ in the denominator.
- Slope measures the **steepness and direction** of a line.
- The ACT frequently gives you two points and asks for the slope. This is a "gimme" — do not overthink it.

**Common error:** Swapping the numerator and denominator. Remember: slope is **rise over run**, or **vertical change over horizontal change**. Think of it as "how much does it go up/down for each step right?"

### 3.3.2 — Interpreting Slope Values

| Slope Value | Meaning | Line Direction |
|---|---|---|
| $m > 0$ | Positive | Rises from left to right (↗) |
| $m < 0$ | Negative | Falls from left to right (↘) |
| $m = 0$ | Zero | Horizontal line (→) |
| Undefined | Division by zero | Vertical line (↑) |
| $|m| > 1$ | Steep | Rise exceeds run |
| $|m| < 1$ | Shallow | Run exceeds rise |
| $|m| = 1$ | 45° diagonal | Rise equals run |

**The vertical line case:** A vertical line has the equation $x = k$ and its slope is **undefined** because the run (change in $x$) is zero, making the denominator of the slope formula zero. The ACT occasionally tests this. A vertical line is **not a function** (it fails the vertical line test).

**The horizontal line case:** A horizontal line has the equation $y = k$ and its slope is **0** because the rise (change in $y$) is zero.

### 3.3.3 — Slope in Context (Word Problems)

The ACT loves to test slope as a **rate of change** in real-world scenarios. When a word problem describes a linear relationship, the slope always represents:

$$m = \dfrac{\text{change in dependent variable}}{\text{change in independent variable}}$$

**Units of slope:** The units of slope are always the units of $y$ divided by the units of $x$. For example, if $y$ is cost in dollars and $x$ is time in hours, the slope has units of **dollars per hour**.

**Example:**
> "A candle is 12 inches tall. It burns at a constant rate. After 3 hours, it is 9 inches tall."

The slope is:
$$m = \dfrac{9 - 12}{3 - 0} = \dfrac{-3}{3} = -1 \text{ inch per hour}$$

The slope of $-1$ means the candle loses 1 inch of height per hour.

### 3.3.4 — Parallel and Perpendicular Lines

This is one of the most frequently tested concepts in all of ACT Mathematics.

**Parallel Lines:**
- Have the **same slope** ($m_1 = m_2$)
- Have **different y-intercepts** (otherwise they would be the same line)
- Never intersect

If a problem says "line $l$ is parallel to the line $y = 3x - 7$," you immediately know the slope of line $l$ is $3$.

**Perpendicular Lines:**
- Have slopes that are **negative reciprocals** of each other ($m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$)
- Intersect at a right angle (90°)

If a problem says "line $l$ is perpendicular to the line $y = 3x - 7$," the slope of line $l$ is $-\dfrac{1}{3}$.

**How to find the negative reciprocal:**
1. Flip the fraction (take the reciprocal)
2. Change the sign

| Original Slope | Negative Reciprocal |
|---|---|
| $3$ | $-\dfrac{1}{3}$ |
| $-\dfrac{2}{5}$ | $\dfrac{5}{2}$ |
| $\dfrac{1}{4}$ | $-4$ |
| $-7$ | $\dfrac{1}{7}$ |
| $1$ | $-1$ |

**Special case:** A horizontal line ($m = 0$) is perpendicular to a vertical line (undefined slope). These are the only pair where the product-of-slopes rule does not apply (since one slope is undefined).

**ACT trap:** The test frequently gives you an equation in standard form and asks for the slope of a perpendicular line. Remember: first find the slope of the given line ($m = -A/B$), then take the negative reciprocal.

---

## 3.4 — The Midpoint and Distance Formulas

### 3.4.1 — Midpoint Formula

The midpoint of the segment connecting $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is:

$$M = \left(\dfrac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \dfrac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$$

**Conceptual understanding:** The midpoint is simply the **average of the x-coordinates** and the **average of the y-coordinates**. This makes intuitive sense — the midpoint is "halfway" between each pair of coordinates.

**When the ACT uses this:**
- Finding the center of a circle given the endpoints of a diameter
- Finding the midpoint of a segment in the coordinate plane
- Determining a missing endpoint when given one endpoint and the midpoint

**Finding a missing endpoint:** If you know one endpoint $(x_1, y_1)$ and the midpoint $(x_m, y_m)$, the other endpoint is:
$$x_2 = 2x_m - x_1, \quad y_2 = 2y_m - y_1$$

This works because the midpoint formula gives $x_m = \dfrac{x_1 + x_2}{2}$, so $x_2 = 2x_m - x_1$.

### 3.4.2 — Distance Formula

The distance between $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is:

$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

**Conceptual understanding:** This is simply the Pythagorean theorem applied to the coordinate plane. The horizontal leg of the right triangle is $|x_2 - x_1|$ and the vertical leg is $|y_2 - y_1|$.

**When the ACT uses this:**
- Finding the length of a side of a triangle or other polygon in the coordinate plane
- Finding the radius of a circle given the center and a point on the circle
- Determining if a point is a given distance from another point

**Strategic note:** The ACT rarely requires you to compute the exact value of a distance that involves a square root. Usually, the answer choices will contain the square root expression, or the numbers will be chosen so that the distance is a whole number (Pythagorean triples).

---

## 3.5 — Systems of Linear Equations

A **system of linear equations** is a set of two (or more) linear equations that must be satisfied simultaneously. The solution to a system is the point(s) where the equations intersect.

### 3.5.1 — The Three Possible Outcomes

| Outcome | Geometric Interpretation | Algebraic Indicator |
|---|---|---|
| **Exactly 1 solution** | Two lines intersect at one point | Different slopes |
| **No solution** | Two parallel lines (never intersect) | Same slope, different y-intercepts |
| **Infinitely many solutions** | Two identical lines (every point overlaps) | Same slope, same y-intercept |

**The ACT frequently tests "no solution" and "infinitely many solutions" scenarios.** The key insight:

For the system:
$$a_1x + b_1y = c_1$$
$$a_2x + b_2y = c_2$$

- **No solution** when: $\dfrac{a_1}{a_2} = \dfrac{b_1}{b_2} \neq \dfrac{c_1}{c_2}$ (parallel lines)
- **Infinite solutions** when: $\dfrac{a_1}{a_2} = \dfrac{b_1}{b_2} = \dfrac{c_1}{c_2}$ (same line)
- **One solution** when: $\dfrac{a_1}{a_2} \neq \dfrac{b_1}{b_2}$ (intersecting lines)

**Example of "no solution":**
$$2x + 3y = 7$$
$$4x + 6y = 10$$

Here $\dfrac{2}{4} = \dfrac{3}{6} = \dfrac{1}{2}$, but $\dfrac{7}{10} \neq \dfrac{1}{2}$. The lines are parallel — no solution.

**Example of "infinite solutions":**
$$2x + 3y = 7$$
$$4x + 6y = 14$$

Here $\dfrac{2}{4} = \dfrac{3}{6} = \dfrac{7}{14} = \dfrac{1}{2}$. The second equation is just the first multiplied by 2 — they represent the same line.

### 3.5.2 — Solving by Substitution

**When to use:** When one of the equations already has a variable isolated (or can easily be isolated), especially if a variable has a coefficient of $\pm 1$.

**Procedure:**
1. Solve one equation for one variable
2. Substitute that expression into the other equation
3. Solve for the remaining variable
4. Substitute back to find the first variable

**Example:**
$$y = 2x + 1$$
$$3x + y = 11$$

Substitute: $3x + (2x + 1) = 11$
$5x + 1 = 11$
$5x = 10$
$x = 2$

Back-substitute: $y = 2(2) + 1 = 5$

Solution: $(2, 5)$

**ACT strategic note:** When a problem gives you an equation like $y = 3x - 4$ and another equation with both variables, substitution is almost always the fastest method. The variable is already isolated — don't waste time rearranging.

### 3.5.3 — Solving by Elimination (Linear Combination)

**When to use:** When both equations are in standard form and no variable is isolated. Also when the coefficients of one variable are opposites (or can easily be made opposites).

**Procedure:**
1. Multiply one or both equations by constants so that the coefficients of one variable are opposites
2. Add the two equations together (this eliminates one variable)
3. Solve for the remaining variable
4. Substitute back to find the other variable

**Example:**
$$3x + 2y = 12$$
$$5x - 2y = 4$$

Add directly (the $y$ terms cancel):
$$8x = 16$$
$$x = 2$$

Substitute: $3(2) + 2y = 12$
$6 + 2y = 12$
$2y = 6$
$y = 3$

Solution: $(2, 3)$

**When you need to multiply:**
$$2x + 3y = 13$$
$$5x + 4y = 21$$

To eliminate $x$: multiply the first equation by 5 and the second by −2:
$$10x + 15y = 65$$
$$-10x - 8y = -42$$

Add: $7y = 23 \Rightarrow y = \dfrac{23}{7}$

Then substitute back to find $x$.

### 3.5.4 — Choosing the Best Method

| Situation | Best Method |
|---|---|
| One variable is already isolated | Substitution |
| Both equations in standard form, coefficients are opposites | Elimination (direct) |
| Both equations in standard form, no easy cancellation | Elimination (multiply first) |
| One equation is $y = mx$ or $x = my$ | Substitution |
| The system has 3 variables | Usually elimination, working variable by variable |

### 3.5.5 — Systems in Word Problems

The ACT frequently presents systems in the context of real-world scenarios. Common types:

**Mixture/Coin problems:**
> "A jar contains nickels and dimes totaling $\$4.50$. There are 55 coins in total."

$$n + d = 55$$
$$0.05n + 0.10d = 4.50$$

**Ticket/Revenue problems:**
> "Adult tickets cost $\$12$ and child tickets cost $\$7$. A total of 200 tickets were sold for $\$1{,}900$."

$$a + c = 200$$
$$12a + 7c = 1900$$

**Rate problems:**
> "Two cars start from the same point. One travels east at 60 mph, the other west at 75 mph. After how many hours are they 270 miles apart?"

$$d_1 = 60t, \quad d_2 = 75t$$
$$d_1 + d_2 = 270$$
$$60t + 75t = 270$$
$$135t = 270$$
$$t = 2$$

**Strategy for word problem systems:**
1. Define your variables explicitly (write down what each variable represents)
2. Write one equation for each piece of information
3. Solve the system
4. Answer the specific question asked (not just "find $x$")

---

## 3.6 — Linear Inequalities and Their Graphs

### 3.6.1 — Graphing Linear Inequalities

The graph of a linear inequality is a **half-plane** bounded by a line.

**Procedure:**
1. Graph the boundary line (replace the inequality with $=$)
   - Use a **solid line** for $\le$ or $\ge$ (the boundary is included)
   - Use a **dashed line** for $<$ or $>$ (the boundary is NOT included)
2. Choose a test point (usually $(0,0)$ if the line does not pass through the origin)
3. If the test point satisfies the inequality, shade the half-plane containing it; otherwise, shade the other half

**Example:** Graph $y > 2x - 3$

1. Graph $y = 2x - 3$ with a **dashed line** (because $>$, not $\ge$)
2. Test $(0,0)$: $0 > 2(0) - 3 \Rightarrow 0 > -3$ ✓ TRUE
3. Shade the half-plane containing $(0,0)$ — which is above the line

### 3.6.2 — Systems of Linear Inequalities

The solution to a system of linear inequalities is the **region where all the shaded half-planes overlap**. This region is called the **feasible region**.

The ACT tests this concept occasionally, usually in the context of:
- Identifying which point lies in the feasible region
- Determining the number of corner points (vertices) of the feasible region
- Finding the maximum or minimum of a linear function over the feasible region (linear programming concept)

**Strategy:** To check if a point satisfies a system of inequalities, substitute the coordinates into **every** inequality. The point must satisfy **all** of them.

---

## 3.7 — Absolute Value Equations and Inequalities

### 3.7.1 — Understanding Absolute Value

The absolute value of a number represents its **distance from zero** on the number line. It is always non-negative.

$$|x| = \begin{cases} x & \text{if } x \ge 0 \\ -x & \text{if } x < 0 \end{cases}$$

Key properties:
- $|x| \ge 0$ for all real $x$
- $|x| = |-x|$
- $|x \cdot y| = |x| \cdot |y|$
- $\sqrt{x^2} = |x|$

### 3.7.2 — Solving Absolute Value Equations

The equation $|A| = b$ (where $b > 0$) splits into two cases:
$$A = b \quad \text{or} \quad A = -b$$

**Example:** $|2x - 3| = 7$
$$2x - 3 = 7 \quad \Rightarrow \quad x = 5$$
$$2x - 3 = -7 \quad \Rightarrow \quad x = -2$$

Solutions: $x = 5$ or $x = -2$

**Special case:** If $|A| = 0$, then $A = 0$ (only one solution).

**Special case:** If $|A| = b$ where $b < 0$, there is **no solution** (absolute value cannot be negative).

**ACT trap:** The test frequently includes the "no solution" case. If you get $|x + 4| = -3$, the answer is immediately "no solution" — do not try to solve it.

### 3.7.3 — Solving Absolute Value Inequalities

**For $|A| < b$ (or $\le b$):**
$$-b < A < b$$

This means $A$ is between $-b$ and $b$.

**For $|A| > b$ (or $\ge b$):**
$$A < -b \quad \text{or} \quad A > b$$

This means $A$ is outside the interval $[-b, b]$.

**Mnemonic:** "Less thAND" (the solution is an AND compound inequality — between the values). "GreatOR" (the solution is an OR compound inequality — outside the values).

**Example:** $|x - 2| < 5$
$$-5 < x - 2 < 5$$
$$-3 < x < 7$$

**Example:** $|3x + 1| \ge 4$
$$3x + 1 \le -4 \quad \text{or} \quad 3x + 1 \ge 4$$
$$3x \le -5 \quad \text{or} \quad 3x \ge 3$$
$$x \le -\dfrac{5}{3} \quad \text{or} \quad x \ge 1$$

**ACT trap:** When solving $|A| < b$, students often write $A < b$ and forget the $A > -b$ part. Always write the compound inequality.

---

## 3.8 — Exponent Rules and Algebraic Manipulation

### 3.8.1 — The Fundamental Exponent Rules

These rules appear constantly on the ACT, often in combination with each other.

| Rule | Expression | Result |
|---|---|---|
| **Product Rule** | $x^a \cdot x^b$ | $x^{a+b}$ |
| **Quotient Rule** | $\dfrac{x^a}{x^b}$ | $x^{a-b}$ |
| **Power Rule** | $(x^a)^b$ | $x^{a \cdot b}$ |
| **Zero Exponent** | $x^0$ (where $x \neq 0$) | $1$ |
| **Negative Exponent** | $x^{-a}$ | $\dfrac{1}{x^a}$ |
| **Fractional Exponent** | $x^{a/b}$ | $\sqrt[b]{x^a}$ or $(\sqrt[b]{x})^a$ |
| **Distributive (Power of Product)** | $(xy)^a$ | $x^a \cdot y^a$ |
| **Distributive (Power of Quotient)** | $\left(\dfrac{x}{y}\right)^a$ | $\dfrac{x^a}{y^a}$ |

**Examples:**
- $x^3 \cdot x^5 = x^8$
- $\dfrac{x^7}{x^2} = x^5$
- $(x^3)^4 = x^{12}$
- $x^{-3} = \dfrac{1}{x^3}$
- $x^{2/3} = \sqrt[3]{x^2}$

### 3.8.2 — Common Exponent Traps

**Trap 1: Negative base with even vs. odd exponents**
$$(-2)^3 = -8 \quad \text{(odd exponent → negative result)}$$
$$(-2)^4 = 16 \quad \text{(even exponent → positive result)}$$
$$-2^3 = -8 \quad \text{(the exponent applies only to the 2, not the negative sign)}$$
$$(-2)^3 = -8 \quad \text{(the exponent applies to } -2\text{)}$$

**Trap 2: Negative exponents do NOT change the sign**
$$3^{-2} = \dfrac{1}{3^2} = \dfrac{1}{9} \quad \text{(positive, not negative)}$$

**Trap 3: You cannot add exponents with different bases**
$$2^3 + 2^2 \neq 2^5$$
$$2^3 + 2^2 = 8 + 4 = 12$$

**Trap 4: $(x + y)^a \neq x^a + y^a$**
$$(x + y)^2 = x^2 + 2xy + y^2 \neq x^2 + y^2$$

### 3.8.3 — Simplifying Algebraic Expressions

The ACT frequently asks you to simplify expressions. The key techniques:

**Combining like terms:** Only terms with the exact same variable and exponent can be combined.
$$3x^2 + 5x - 2x^2 + 4x = x^2 + 9x$$

**Distributing:**
$$3(x + 4) = 3x + 12$$
$$-2(x - 5) = -2x + 10$$

**FOIL (First, Outer, Inner, Last) for binomial multiplication:**
$$(x + 3)(x - 5) = x^2 - 5x + 3x - 15 = x^2 - 2x - 15$$

**Special products to memorize:**
- $(a + b)^2 = a^2 + 2ab + b^2$
- $(a - b)^2 = a^2 - 2ab + b^2$
- $(a + b)(a - b) = a^2 - b^2$ (difference of squares)

### 3.8.4 — Factoring

Factoring is the reverse of expanding. It is essential for solving quadratics, simplifying rational expressions, and finding zeros of functions.

**Step 1: Always check for a GCF (Greatest Common Factor) first.**

$$6x^3 + 12x^2 - 18x = 6x(x^2 + 2x - 3)$$

**Step 2: Factor the remaining expression.**

For a trinomial $x^2 + bx + c$:
Find two numbers that **multiply to $c$** and **add to $b$**.

$$x^2 + 2x - 3 = (x + 3)(x - 1)$$
(Since $3 \cdot (-1) = -3$ and $3 + (-1) = 2$)

So: $6x^3 + 12x^2 - 18x = 6x(x + 3)(x - 1)$

**Difference of squares:**
$$a^2 - b^2 = (a + b)(a - b)$$
$$x^2 - 25 = (x + 5)(x - 5)$$
$$4x^2 - 9 = (2x + 3)(2x - 3)$$

**Perfect square trinomials:**
$$a^2 + 2ab + b^2 = (a + b)^2$$
$$a^2 - 2ab + b^2 = (a - b)^2$$

**Factoring when the leading coefficient is not 1 ($ax^2 + bx + c$):**

For $2x^2 + 7x + 3$:
1. Multiply $a \cdot c = 2 \cdot 3 = 6$
2. Find two numbers that multiply to 6 and add to 7: $6$ and $1$
3. Split the middle term: $2x^2 + 6x + x + 3$
4. Factor by grouping: $2x(x + 3) + 1(x + 3) = (2x + 1)(x + 3)$

---

## 3.9 — Coordinate Geometry of Lines

### 3.9.1 — Key Features of a Line in the Coordinate Plane

Every line (except vertical lines) has:

- **x-intercept:** Where the line crosses the x-axis ($y = 0$). Set $y = 0$ and solve for $x$.
- **y-intercept:** Where the line crosses the y-axis ($x = 0$). Set $x = 0$ and solve for $y$.
- **Slope:** The rate of change, $m = \dfrac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$

**For slope-intercept form $y = mx + b$:**
- y-intercept = $(0, b)$
- To find x-intercept: set $y = 0$: $0 = mx + b \Rightarrow x = -\dfrac{b}{m}$

**For standard form $Ax + By = C$:**
- x-intercept = $\left(\dfrac{C}{A}, 0\right)$
- y-intercept = $\left(0, \dfrac{C}{B}\right)$

### 3.9.2 — Writing the Equation of a Line Given Different Information

| Given | Method |
|---|---|
| Slope $m$ and y-intercept $b$ | Plug into $y = mx + b$ |
| Slope $m$ and a point $(x_1, y_1)$ | Use point-slope: $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$ |
| Two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ | Find slope, then use point-slope with either point |
| A parallel line and a point | Same slope as the parallel line, use point-slope |
| A perpendicular line and a point | Negative reciprocal slope, use point-slope |
| A graph | Read the y-intercept and calculate the slope from two visible points |

### 3.9.3 — Horizontal and Vertical Lines

These are frequently overlooked but regularly tested.

**Horizontal line:** $y = k$ (where $k$ is the y-coordinate of any point on the line)
- Slope = $0$
- Passes through $(x, k)$ for all $x$

**Vertical line:** $x = k$ (where $k$ is the x-coordinate of any point on the line)
- Slope = **undefined**
- Passes through $(k, y)$ for all $y$

**ACT trap:** Students sometimes try to find the slope of a vertical line using the slope formula and get confused by division by zero. Remember: vertical lines have undefined slope, period.

### 3.9.4 — Reflections and Symmetry

**Reflection across the x-axis:** $(x, y) \to (x, -y)$
- The equation $y = f(x)$ becomes $y = -f(x)$
- The x-intercept stays the same; the y-intercept changes sign

**Reflection across the y-axis:** $(x, y) \to (-x, y)$
- The equation $y = f(x)$ becomes $y = f(-x)$

**Reflection through the origin:** $(x, y) \to (-x, -y)$

The ACT occasionally tests whether you can identify the reflection of a line or a point. For a line $y = mx + b$:
- Reflection across x-axis: $y = -mx - b$
- Reflection across y-axis: $y = -mx + b$ (replace $x$ with $-x$)

### 3.9.5 — The Equation of a Circle

While technically not a "linear" concept, the circle equation appears in the coordinate geometry section and connects to the distance formula.

**Standard form of a circle:**
$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

- Center: $(h, k)$
- Radius: $r$

**Example:** $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 25$
- Center: $(3, -2)$
- Radius: $\sqrt{25} = 5$

**ACT application:** If the ACT gives you the center and a point on the circle, the radius is the distance between them (use the distance formula). If the ACT gives you the endpoints of a diameter, the center is the midpoint, and the radius is half the distance between the endpoints.

---

## 3.10 — Interpreting Linear Models in Context

### 3.10.1 — The "Story" of $y = mx + b$

On the ACT, linear equations in word problems always follow the same structural pattern:

$$y = (\text{rate of change}) \cdot x + (\text{starting value})$$

- **Slope ($m$):** The rate at which $y$ changes for each unit increase in $x$. In context, this is a **per-unit cost**, **speed**, **growth rate**, **fee per hour**, etc.
- **y-intercept ($b$):** The value of $y$ when $x = 0$. In context, this is a **starting amount**, **initial value**, **fixed cost**, **base fee**, etc.

### 3.10.2 — Systematic Approach to Linear Word Problems

**Step 1: Identify the variables.** What is $x$? What is $y$? Write this down.

**Step 2: Identify the slope.** What quantity changes at a constant rate? That rate is the slope.

**Step 3: Identify the y-intercept.** What is the starting value (when $x = 0$)?

**Step 4: Write the equation.** $y = mx + b$

**Step 5: Use the equation to answer the question.**

**Example:**
> "A gym charges a $\$100$ membership fee plus $\$35$ per month. Write an equation for the total cost $C$ after $m$ months."

- $C$ = total cost (dependent variable)
- $m$ = number of months (independent variable)
- Slope = $35$ (the monthly rate)
- y-intercept = $100$ (the initial membership fee)
- Equation: $C = 35m + 100$

**Follow-up question:** "What does the slope represent in this context?"
**Answer:** The monthly membership fee ($\$35$ per month).

**Follow-up question:** "What is the total cost after 6 months?"
**Answer:** $C = 35(6) + 100 = 210 + 100 = 310$

### 3.10.3 — Comparing Linear Functions

The ACT sometimes presents two linear models and asks you to compare them. Common comparison questions:

**Which has a greater rate of change?** Compare the slopes.
**Which starts at a greater value?** Compare the y-intercepts.
**When are the two functions equal?** Set the equations equal and solve for $x$ (this is equivalent to solving a system).
**What is the difference between the two functions?** Subtract one equation from the other.

**Example:**
> "Company A charges $\$50$ plus $\$0.10$ per page. Company B charges $\$30$ plus $\$0.15$ per page."

$$A(p) = 0.10p + 50$$
$$B(p) = 0.15p + 30$$

- Company A has a higher starting cost ($\$50 > \$30$) but a lower per-page rate ($\$0.10 < \$0.15$)
- They cost the same when: $0.10p + 50 = 0.15p + 30 \Rightarrow 20 = 0.05p \Rightarrow p = 400$
- For fewer than 400 pages, Company B is cheaper. For more than 400 pages, Company A is cheaper.

---

## 3.11 — Common ACT Traps and Strategic Warnings

### 3.11.1 — "Solve for $x$" vs. "Solve for $2x$"

The ACT is notorious for this. If a problem asks for the value of $2x$ and you solve for $x$, the value of $x$ will appear as a **distractor answer choice**. Always double-check what the question is asking for.

**Example:** If $3x + 7 = 22$, what is the value of $2x$?
- $3x = 15 \Rightarrow x = 5$
- $2x = 10$ ← This is the answer, not 5.

### 3.11.2 — Sign Errors with Inequalities

As discussed in Section 3.1.3, forgetting to reverse the inequality sign when multiplying or dividing by a negative number is one of the most common errors on the entire exam. The ACT deliberately includes the wrong answer (with the unreversed sign) as a trap.

### 3.11.3 — Confusing Slope and y-intercept in Context

When interpreting a linear model, students sometimes confuse which parameter represents which real-world quantity. Remember:
- **Slope** = the thing that accumulates (per-unit rate)
- **y-intercept** = the thing that's already there at the start (fixed amount)

### 3.11.4 — Assuming the Line Passes Through the Origin

Not every linear relationship has a y-intercept of zero. If a problem says "y is proportional to x," then the line passes through the origin ($b = 0$). But if it says "y is linearly related to x" or just gives a linear equation, the y-intercept may be non-zero.

**Direct variation:** $y = kx$ (passes through origin, $k$ is the constant of proportionality)
**Linear (not direct variation):** $y = mx + b$ where $b \neq 0$

### 3.11.5 — Forgetting to Check Your Work

On a 60-minute test with 60 questions (or 50 minutes with 45 questions on the Enhanced ACT), you may not have time to check every problem. But for linear equations and systems, checking is fast:
- **Plug your solution back into the original equation.** Does it work?
- **For systems, check in BOTH equations.** A solution must satisfy both.

### 3.11.6 — Misreading Standard Form

When the ACT gives you $3x - 4y = 12$ and asks for the slope, the slope is $-\dfrac{A}{B} = -\dfrac{3}{-4} = \dfrac{3}{4}$. Students sometimes read the coefficients incorrectly or forget the negative sign. Be deliberate: identify $A$, $B$, and $C$ explicitly before computing.

---

## 3.12 — Advanced Linear Concepts

### 3.12.1 — Piecewise Linear Functions

A piecewise function uses different formulas for different intervals of the domain. The ACT occasionally tests these, usually by asking you to evaluate the function at a specific $x$-value.

**Example:**
$$f(x) = \begin{cases} 2x + 1 & \text{if } x \ge 0 \\ -x + 3 & \text{if } x < 0 \end{cases}$$

To find $f(2)$: Since $2 \ge 0$, use $f(x) = 2x + 1$: $f(2) = 5$.
To find $f(-3)$: Since $-3 < 0$, use $f(x) = -x + 3$: $f(-3) = 6$.

### 3.12.2 — Linear Function Transformations

While transformations are covered more fully in the Functions chapter, the linear case is important:

Starting with $y = x$ (the parent linear function):

| Transformation | Equation | Effect |
|---|---|---|
| Vertical shift up by $k$ | $y = x + k$ | Moves graph up $k$ units |
| Vertical shift down by $k$ | $y = x - k$ | Moves graph down $k$ units |
| Vertical stretch by factor $a$ | $y = ax$ | Steepens (if $a > 1$) or flattens (if $0 < a < 1$) |
| Reflection across x-axis | $y = -x$ | Flips the line to slope the other way |

### 3.12.3 — Scatterplots and Lines of Best Fit

The ACT frequently presents scatterplots and asks you to draw or identify a **line of best fit** (trend line). Key concepts:

- A line of best fit is a straight line that best represents the trend of the data
- It does not need to pass through any specific data points
- It should have roughly equal numbers of points above and below it
- You can use two points on the line of best fit to calculate its slope, which represents the **estimated rate of change**
- You can use the line to **interpolate** (estimate within the data range) or **extrapolate** (estimate outside the data range)

**Correlation vs. Causation:** The ACT may test whether you understand that correlation does not imply causation. Just because two variables are correlated does not mean one causes the other.

---

## 3.13 — Quick Reference: All Essential Formulas

| Concept | Formula |
|---|---|
| Slope | $m = \dfrac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$ |
| Slope-Intercept Form | $y = mx + b$ |
| Point-Slope Form | $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$ |
| Standard Form | $Ax + By = C$ |
| Slope from Standard Form | $m = -\dfrac{A}{B}$ |
| Midpoint | $M = \left(\dfrac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \dfrac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$ |
| Distance | $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$ |
| Parallel Lines | $m_1 = m_2$ |
| Perpendicular Lines | $m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$ |
| Circle Equation | $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ |
| Absolute Value Equation | $|A| = b \Rightarrow A = b$ or $A = -b$ |
| Absolute Value Inequality (<) | $|A| < b \Rightarrow -b < A < b$ |
| Absolute Value Inequality (>) | $|A| > b \Rightarrow A < -b$ or $A > b$ |

---

## 3.14 — Strategic Summary: The Algebra Decision Tree

When you encounter an algebra or linear functions problem on the ACT, use this decision tree:

**1. What is the problem asking?**
- Solve for a variable → Equation solving
- Interpret a relationship → Identify slope and/or y-intercept
- Find a specific value → Evaluate the function
- Compare two relationships → System of equations or inequality

**2. What form is the equation in?**
- $y = mx + b$ → Slope and y-intercept are immediately visible
- $Ax + By = C$ → Slope is $-A/B$; intercepts are $C/A$ and $C/B$
- Point-slope → Convert to slope-intercept for interpretation

**3. How many equations do I have?**
- One equation, one unknown → Solve directly
- Two equations, two unknowns → Use substitution or elimination
- Inequality → Solve like an equation, but reverse sign when multiplying/dividing by negative

**4. Does the answer make sense?**
- Check units
- Check sign
- Check that you answered the question actually asked (not a distractor)

---

## 3.15 — Connections to Other Chapters

Algebra and linear functions are not isolated topics. They connect to virtually every other area tested on the ACT:

- **Chapter 4 (Quadratics):** The quadratic formula and factoring build directly on the algebraic techniques from this chapter. The vertex of a parabola is found using $x = -\dfrac{b}{2a}$, which is analogous to the midpoint concept.
- **Chapter 5 (Functions):** Linear functions are the simplest type of function. Function notation, domain, range, and transformations all apply to linear functions.
- **Chapter 6 (Geometry):** Coordinate geometry problems (finding lengths, midpoints, equations of lines in the coordinate plane) are direct applications of this chapter. Many geometry problems reduce to solving linear equations.
- **Chapter 7 (Data Analysis):** Lines of best fit, scatterplots, and linear regression all depend on understanding slope and y-intercept.
- **Chapter 8 (Modeling):** Word problems that require you to write and interpret linear models are the bridge between pure algebra and real-world application.

Mastering this chapter is not just about scoring points on "Algebra" questions — it is about building the foundational skills that enable you to succeed across the entire mathematics section.

---

*This chapter has provided the complete theoretical and strategic foundation for algebra and linear functions on the ACT. Every formula, property, interpretation technique, and common trap has been catalogued. The next step is deliberate, timed practice — applying these concepts under test conditions until they become automatic. Refer back to specific sections of this chapter whenever you encounter errors in your practice, and use the Error Log methodology described in the Introduction to track patterns in your mistakes.*

---


# Chapter 4: Quadratics, Polynomials, and Higher-Order Equations — Strategies, Formulas, and Discriminant Analysis

---

## 4.1 — The Centrality of Quadratics on the ACT Mathematics Test

Quadratic equations and their related functions represent one of the most heavily tested content areas within the ACT Mathematics section. Across every released form of the test, quadratics appear in approximately 6–10 questions, making them the single most common "advanced" topic. They surface in three broad categories:

1. **Pure algebra** — solving, factoring, finding roots, manipulating forms.
2. **Graphical analysis** — identifying vertices, axes of symmetry, intercepts, and the shape of parabolas.
3. **Modeling and application** — projectile motion, area optimization, and real-world scenarios that produce quadratic relationships.

The ACT does not merely test whether you *can* solve a quadratic equation. It tests whether you can **choose the fastest method**, **move fluidly between representations**, and **avoid the specific trap answers** that the test writers deliberately embed in the answer choices. This chapter will equip you with a complete, rigorous framework for every quadratic and higher-order polynomial question you will encounter.

---

## 4.2 — The Three Forms of a Quadratic Function and When to Use Each

Every quadratic function can be written in three algebraically equivalent forms. Each form reveals different information at a glance, and the ACT frequently tests your ability to move between them and to select the right form for the task at hand.

### 4.2.1 — Standard Form: $y = ax^2 + bx + c$

**What it reveals instantly:**
- The **$y$-intercept** is $(0, c)$. This is the only form where the $y$-intercept is immediately visible.
- The sign of $a$ tells you whether the parabola opens **upward** ($a > 0$, a "smile" or minimum) or **downward** ($a < 0$, a "frown" or maximum).
- The **axis of symmetry** is the vertical line $x = -\dfrac{b}{2a}$.
- The **$x$-coordinate of the vertex** is $-\dfrac{b}{2a}$, and you must substitute this back into the equation to find the $y$-coordinate.

**When to use it:**
- When the problem gives you the equation in this form and asks about the $y$-intercept.
- When you need to apply the quadratic formula directly (the formula is derived from this form).
- When the problem provides three points on a parabola and asks you to determine the equation — you set up a system of three equations in $a$, $b$, and $c$.

**Critical nuance:** The axis of symmetry formula $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ is not provided on any ACT formula sheet. You must have it memorized. One reliable mnemonic: the axis of symmetry is the *average of the two roots*, and from the quadratic formula, the two roots are $\frac{-b + \sqrt{\Delta}}{2a}$ and $\frac{-b - \sqrt{\Delta}}{2a}$. Their average is $-\frac{b}{2a}$. This derivation also reveals that the axis of symmetry is always the midpoint of any two points on the parabola with the same $y$-value.

### 4.2.2 — Vertex Form: $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$

**What it reveals instantly:**
- The **vertex** is at the point $(h, k)$. Note the sign: if the equation is $y = a(x - 3)^2 + 5$, the vertex is $(3, 5)$. If it is $y = a(x + 2)^2 - 7$, the vertex is $(-2, 7)$. The subtraction inside the parentheses means the $x$-coordinate of the vertex carries the *opposite* sign of the constant inside the parentheses.
- The **axis of symmetry** is $x = h$.
- The **direction of opening** and **narrowness/wideness** are determined by $a$, just as in standard form.

**When to use it:**
- When the problem gives you the vertex directly and asks for the equation.
- When the problem asks for the maximum or minimum value of a quadratic function — the answer is simply $k$ (the $y$-coordinate of the vertex).
- When the problem describes a transformation of a parent function $y = x^2$.

**Common ACT trap:** Students frequently misread the sign of $h$. The expression $(x - h)$ means the vertex has $x$-coordinate $h$, not $-h$. If you see $y = 2(x - 4)^2 + 1$, the vertex is at $(4, 1)$, not $(-4, 1)$. This is one of the most common errors the test exploits.

### 4.2.3 — Intercept (Factored) Form: $y = a(x - p)(x - q)$

**What it reveals instantly:**
- The **$x$-intercepts** (also called roots or zeros) are $p$ and $q$. The $y$-intercept requires substitution or expansion.
- The **axis of symmetry** lies halfway between the roots: $x = \dfrac{p + q}{2}$.
- The **vertex** can be found by computing the axis of symmetry $x = \frac{p+q}{2}$ and then substituting that $x$-value back into the equation.

**When to use it:**
- When the problem gives you the $x$-intercepts directly.
- When you need to solve a quadratic equation quickly — if the equation is already factored, the Zero Product Property gives the solutions instantly.
- When the problem asks about the number or nature of $x$-intercepts.

**Critical insight:** The intercept form makes it immediately obvious whether the parabola crosses the $x$-axis at two points, one point (a double root), or — if the factors involve complex numbers — not at all. This connects directly to the discriminant analysis we will cover in Section 4.6.

### 4.2.4 — Conversion Between Forms

The ACT frequently requires you to convert from one form to another. Here is the complete conversion toolkit:

**Standard → Vertex (Completing the Square):**
1. Factor $a$ from the $x^2$ and $x$ terms: $y = a\left(x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x\right) + c$
2. Take half of $\frac{b}{a}$, square it: $\left(\frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 = \frac{b^2}{4a^2}$
3. Add and subtract this quantity inside the parentheses.
4. Rewrite as a perfect square trinomial and simplify.

**Vertex → Standard (Expansion):**
1. Expand the squared binomial: $(x - h)^2 = x^2 - 2hx + h^2$
2. Multiply by $a$: $a(x^2 - 2hx + h^2) = ax^2 - 2ahx + ah^2$
3. Add $k$: $y = ax^2 - 2ahx + (ah^2 + k)$

**Standard → Intercept (Factoring or Quadratic Formula):**
1. Factor the expression if possible over the integers.
2. If factoring is not clean, use the quadratic formula to find the roots $p$ and $q$, then write $y = a(x - p)(x - q)$.

**Intercept → Standard (FOIL):**
1. Multiply the two binomials: $(x - p)(x - q) = x^2 - (p+q)x + pq$
2. Multiply by $a$: $y = ax^2 - a(p+q)x + apq$

---

## 4.3 — The Parabola: Geometric Properties and Graphical Analysis

### 4.3.1 — Shape and Direction

A quadratic function always produces a **parabola** as its graph. The coefficient $a$ controls:

- **Direction:** $a > 0$ → opens upward (has a minimum); $a < 0$ → opens downward (has a maximum).
- **Width:** $|a| > 1$ → narrower than $y = x^2$; $0 < |a| < 1$ → wider than $y = x^2$. The larger the $|a|$, the "skinnier" the parabola.

On the ACT, you may be shown a graph and asked to determine the sign of $a$, or you may be given an equation and asked to identify the correct graph from four options. Always check the direction of opening first — this alone often eliminates two answer choices immediately.

### 4.3.2 — The Vertex as Maximum or Minimum

The vertex is the **turning point** of the parabola. If the parabola opens upward, the vertex represents the **minimum value** of the function. If it opens downward, the vertex represents the **maximum value**.

This has enormous implications for word problems. When the ACT asks "What is the maximum area?" or "What is the minimum cost?" and the underlying relationship is quadratic, the answer is the $y$-coordinate of the vertex.

### 4.3.3 — Symmetry

The parabola is symmetric about its axis of symmetry. This means:
- If two points on the parabola have the same $y$-coordinate, their $x$-coordinates are equidistant from the axis of symmetry.
- The reflection of the $y$-intercept across the axis of symmetry gives another point on the parabola with the same $y$-value.

This symmetry property is frequently tested. For example, if a parabola has $x$-intercepts at $x = 1$ and $x = 7$, then by symmetry, the axis of symmetry is at $x = 4$, and the vertex has $x$-coordinate 4.

### 4.3.4 — The $y$-Intercept

The $y$-intercept of $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ is always $(0, c)$. In vertex form, the $y$-intercept is found by substituting $x = 0$: $y = a(0 - h)^2 + k = ah^2 + k$. In intercept form, it is $y = a(0 - p)(0 - q) = apq$.

### 4.3.5 — The Discriminant and $x$-Intercepts (Preview)

The number of $x$-intercepts is determined by the discriminant $\Delta = b^2 - 4ac$:
- $\Delta > 0$: Two distinct $x$-intercepts.
- $\Delta = 0$: One $x$-intercept (the vertex touches the $x$-axis; a double root).
- $\Delta < 0$: No $x$-intercepts (the parabola does not cross the $x$-axis).

This will be explored in full depth in Section 4.6.

---

## 4.4 — Solving Quadratic Equations: Method Selection Strategy

One of the most important strategic decisions you will make on the ACT is choosing the **fastest method** to solve a given quadratic equation. The four primary methods are:

### 4.4.1 — Factoring (Fastest When It Works)

**When to use:** When the quadratic can be factored over the integers. This is usually the case when the coefficients are small and the equation has rational roots.

**The method:**
1. Set the equation equal to zero.
2. Factor out the GCF first (always check!).
3. Factor the remaining trinomial into two binomials.
4. Apply the Zero Product Property: if $AB = 0$, then $A = 0$ or $B = 0$.
5. Solve each resulting linear equation.

**Example framework:** $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$ factors to $(x - 2)(x - 3) = 0$, giving $x = 2$ or $x = 3$.

**ACT pattern recognition for factorable quadratics:**
- $x^2 + bx + c$ where you need two numbers that multiply to $c$ and add to $b$.
- $ax^2 + bx + c$ where $a \neq 1$: use the AC method — multiply $a \cdot c$, find two factors of $ac$ that add to $b$, split the middle term, and factor by grouping.
- Difference of squares: $x^2 - k^2 = (x + k)(x - k)$. This appears frequently and is one of the fastest factorizations.
- Perfect square trinomials: $x^2 \pm 2kx + k^2 = (x \pm k)^2$.

**When factoring fails:** If you cannot find integer factors within about 15 seconds, move to another method. Do not waste time.

### 4.4.2 — The Quadratic Formula (Always Works)

$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$

**When to use:** When the equation cannot be easily factored, when the coefficients are large or messy, or when the problem asks for the exact form of irrational roots.

**Critical execution notes:**
1. **Always set the equation equal to zero first.** Identify $a$, $b$, and $c$ only after the equation is in the form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$.
2. **Watch the sign of $b$.** The formula has $-b$, so if $b$ is already negative (e.g., $3x^2 - 7x + 2 = 0$ where $b = -7$), then $-b = -(-7) = 7$. This is a frequent source of sign errors.
3. **Compute the discriminant first** ($b^2 - 4ac$) as a separate step. This tells you how many real solutions to expect and whether the radical will simplify.
4. **Simplify the radical** if possible. $\sqrt{8} = 2\sqrt{2}$, $\sqrt{12} = 2\sqrt{3}$, etc.
5. **Reduce the final fraction** by factoring out common factors from the numerator.

**ACT shortcut:** If the answer choices are in the form $\frac{-b \pm \sqrt{\Delta}}{2a}$, you often don't need to fully simplify — just match the structure to the answer choices.

### 4.4.3 — Completing the Square (Strategic Use)

**When to use:** When the problem explicitly asks for vertex form, when the quadratic has a leading coefficient of 1 and an even linear coefficient (making the process clean), or when deriving the vertex from standard form.

**The method for $x^2 + bx + c = 0$:**
1. Move $c$ to the right side: $x^2 + bx = -c$
2. Take half of $b$, square it: $\left(\frac{b}{2}\right)^2$
3. Add this to both sides: $x^2 + bx + \left(\frac{b}{2}\right)^2 = \left(\frac{b}{2}\right)^2 - c$
4. Write the left side as a binomial square: $\left(x + \frac{b}{2}\right)^2 = \left(\frac{b}{2}\right)^2 - c$
5. Take the square root of both sides and solve.

**When $a \neq 1$:** First divide every term by $a$ to make the leading coefficient 1, then proceed as above.

**ACT relevance:** Completing the square is the mechanism by which you convert standard form to vertex form. The vertex emerges naturally: $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$ where $h = -\frac{b}{2a}$ and $k = c - \frac{b^2}{4a}$.

### 4.4.4 — Square Root Method (Special Cases)

**When to use:** When the equation is of the form $(expression)^2 = k$ or can be rearranged into this form.

**The method:**
1. Isolate the squared expression.
2. Take the square root of both sides.
3. **Remember the $\pm$:** $\sqrt{k}$ gives both $+\sqrt{k}$ and $-\sqrt{k}$.

**Example:** $(x - 3)^2 = 16$ → $x - 3 = \pm 4$ → $x = 3 \pm 4$ → $x = 7$ or $x = -1$.

**ACT relevance:** This method is extremely fast when applicable. Watch for perfect square trinomials that can be rewritten as squared binomials.

### 4.4.5 — Method Selection Decision Tree

When you encounter a quadratic equation on the ACT, follow this decision hierarchy:

1. **Is it already factored?** → Use the Zero Product Property immediately.
2. **Can it be factored easily (small integer coefficients)?** → Factor.
3. **Is it of the form $(expression)^2 = k$?** → Square Root Method.
4. **Does the problem ask for vertex form or the vertex?** → Complete the Square (or use $-\frac{b}{2a}$).
5. **None of the above?** → Quadratic Formula.

This decision tree should become automatic through practice. The ACT rewards speed, and choosing the right method can save 30–60 seconds per question.

---

## 4.5 — The Zero Product Property and Its Applications

The Zero Product Property states: **If $A \cdot B = 0$, then $A = 0$ or $B = 0$ (or both).**

This is the foundational principle behind solving quadratic equations by factoring. It extends to any number of factors: if $(x - a)(x - b)(x - c) = 0$, then $x = a$, $x = b$, or $x = c$.

**Critical conditions:**
- The product must equal **zero**. If $(x - 2)(x - 3) = 6$, you **cannot** set each factor equal to 6. You must first expand, rearrange to set equal to zero, then factor.
- All factors must be on one side, and zero must be on the other.

**ACT trap:** The test frequently presents equations like $(x + 1)(x - 4) = 3$ and includes answer choices of $x = -1$ and $x = 4$ — these are the values that make each factor zero, but they do NOT satisfy the equation because the right side is 3, not 0. Always verify that the product equals zero before applying the Zero Product Property.

---

## 4.6 — Discriminant Analysis: The Complete Framework

The **discriminant** is the expression under the radical in the quadratic formula:

$$\Delta = b^2 - 4ac$$

It is one of the most powerful diagnostic tools in all of ACT mathematics, and it appears in a surprising variety of question types.

### 4.6.1 — What the Discriminant Tells You

| Discriminant Value | Nature of Roots | Graph Behavior | Number of $x$-Intercepts |
|---|---|---|---|
| $\Delta > 0$ | Two distinct **real** roots | Parabola crosses $x$-axis at two points | 2 |
| $\Delta = 0$ | One **real** root (double root) | Vertex of parabola lies on $x$-axis | 1 |
| $\Delta < 0$ | Two **complex** (non-real) roots | Parabola does not touch $x$-axis | 0 |

### 4.6.2 — Discriminant Questions on the ACT

The ACT tests the discriminant in several ways:

**Type 1: "How many real solutions does the equation have?"**
Compute $b^2 - 4ac$ and interpret the sign. This is the most straightforward discriminant question.

**Type 2: "For what value of $k$ does the equation have exactly one real solution?"**
Set $\Delta = 0$ and solve for the unknown parameter. For example, if the equation is $x^2 + kx + 9 = 0$, then $k^2 - 4(1)(9) = 0$, so $k^2 = 36$, giving $k = 6$ or $k = -6$.

**Type 3: "The graph of $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ does not intersect the $x$-axis. Which of the following must be true?"**
This means $\Delta < 0$, so $b^2 - 4ac < 0$.

**Type 4: "The equation has two irrational real solutions."**
This means $\Delta > 0$ but $\Delta$ is not a perfect square. The ACT sometimes tests this distinction.

### 4.6.3 — The Discriminant and Perfect Square Trinomials

When $\Delta$ is a perfect square, the quadratic formula produces **rational** roots (assuming integer coefficients). When $\Delta$ is positive but not a perfect square, the roots are **real but irrational** and involve a radical expression.

This distinction matters for the ACT because:
- If the answer choices are all rational numbers and the discriminant is not a perfect square, you know the roots are irrational and can eliminate rational answer choices.
- If the problem asks for the exact value of the roots and $\Delta$ is not a perfect square, the answer will contain a radical.

### 4.6.4 — Discriminant of Higher-Degree Polynomials

While the discriminant is formally defined for quadratics, the concept extends. For a cubic $ax^3 + bx^2 + cx + d$, there is a more complex discriminant, but on the ACT, you are primarily expected to analyze the discriminant of quadratics. However, understanding that a polynomial of degree $n$ has at most $n$ real roots (by the Fundamental Theorem of Algebra) is important for higher-order polynomial questions.

---

## 4.7 — Sum and Product of Roots

For a quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ with roots $p$ and $q$:

- **Sum of roots:** $p + q = -\dfrac{b}{a}$
- **Product of roots:** $pq = \dfrac{c}{a}$

These relationships are known as **Vieta's Formulas**, and they are tested on the ACT in several clever ways.

### 4.7.1 — When You Need the Sum or Product Without Finding the Roots

**Example scenario:** "The roots of $2x^2 - 7x + 3 = 0$ are $r$ and $s$. What is the value of $r \cdot s$?"

Without solving: $r \cdot s = \frac{c}{a} = \frac{3}{2}$.

This is much faster than factoring, finding both roots, and multiplying them.

### 4.7.2 — Constructing a Quadratic from Its Roots

If you know the roots are $p$ and $q$, the quadratic can be written as:
$$x^2 - (p + q)x + pq = 0$$

This follows directly from Vieta's formulas. If $a \neq 1$, multiply through by $a$:
$$ax^2 - a(p + q)x + apq = 0$$

### 4.7.3 — Finding the Equation Given Sum and Product

If the sum of the roots is $S$ and the product is $P$, the monic quadratic (leading coefficient 1) is:
$$x^2 - Sx + P = 0$$

---

## 4.8 — Quadratic Inequalities

The ACT occasionally tests quadratic inequalities, and these require careful reasoning about the graph of the parabola.

### 4.8.1 — Solving $ax^2 + bx + c > 0$ or $ax^2 + bx + c < 0$

**Method:**
1. Find the roots of $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ (these are the boundary points).
2. The roots divide the number line into three intervals.
3. Test a point from each interval, or use the graph of the parabola to determine where it is above or below the $x$-axis.

**Shortcut using the graph:**
- If $a > 0$ (parabola opens upward), then $ax^2 + bx + c > 0$ outside the roots and $ax^2 + bx + c < 0$ between the roots.
- If $a < 0$ (parabola opens downward), then $ax^2 + bx + c > 0$ between the roots and $ax^2 + bx + c < 0$ outside the roots.

**Mnemonic:** "Upward parabola: positive on the outside, negative on the inside. Downward parabola: positive on the inside, negative on the outside."

### 4.8.2 — Inclusive vs. Strict Inequalities

- $ax^2 + bx + c \geq 0$ includes the roots (use solid dots or square brackets).
- $ax^2 + bx + c > 0$ excludes the roots (use open dots or parentheses).

On the ACT, pay close attention to whether the inequality is strict ($<$, $>$) or non-strict ($\leq$, $\geq$), as this affects whether the boundary values are included in the solution set.

---

## 4.9 — Polynomials of Higher Degree

### 4.9.1 — Degree, Leading Coefficient, and End Behavior

For any polynomial, the **degree** (highest power of $x$) and the **leading coefficient** determine the end behavior:

| Degree | Leading Coefficient | End Behavior (as $x \to +\infty$) | End Behavior (as $x \to -\infty$) |
|---|---|---|---|
| Even | Positive | $y \to +\infty$ | $y \to +\infty$ |
| Even | Negative | $y \to -\infty$ | $y \to -\infty$ |
| Odd | Positive | $y \to +\infty$ | $y \to -\infty$ |
| Odd | Negative | $y \to -\infty$ | $y \to +\infty$ |

**Mnemonic for even degree:** "Both ends go the same direction." For odd degree: "Ends go opposite directions."

### 4.9.2 — The Factor Theorem and Remainder Theorem

**Factor Theorem:** For a polynomial $P(x)$, the value $x - k$ is a factor of $P(x)$ if and only if $P(k) = 0$.

**Remainder Theorem:** When $P(x)$ is divided by $x - k$, the remainder is $P(k)$.

These theorems are tested on the ACT in questions like:
- "If $P(x) = x^3 - 4x^2 + x + 6$, which of the following is a factor?"
  - Test each option by evaluating $P(k)$. If $P(k) = 0$, then $x - k$ is a factor.
- "What is the remainder when $P(x)$ is divided by $x - 3$?"
  - Simply compute $P(3)$.

### 4.9.3 — Factoring Higher-Degree Polynomials

**Step 1: Check for a GCF.** Always factor out the greatest common factor first. This simplifies the remaining polynomial.

**Step 2: Apply special factoring patterns.**
- **Difference of squares:** $a^2 - b^2 = (a + b)(a - b)$. This can apply to higher powers: $x^4 - 16 = (x^2 + 4)(x^2 - 4) = (x^2 + 4)(x + 2)(x - 2)$.
- **Sum/difference of cubes:**
  - $a^3 + b^3 = (a + b)(a^2 - ab + b^2)$
  - $a^3 - b^3 = (a - b)(a^2 + ab + b^2)$
- **Difference of $n$th powers:** $a^n - b^n$ is divisible by $a - b$ for any positive integer $n$.

**Step 3: Factor by grouping** (for polynomials with 4 or more terms).

**Step 4: Use the Rational Root Theorem** to identify possible rational roots. For a polynomial $a_nx^n + \cdots + a_0$, the possible rational roots are of the form $\frac{p}{q}$ where $p$ divides $a_0$ and $q$ divides $a_n$.

### 4.9.4 — The Fundamental Theorem of Algebra

A polynomial of degree $n$ has exactly $n$ roots in the complex number system (counting multiplicities). This means:
- A quadratic has exactly 2 roots (real or complex).
- A cubic has exactly 3 roots.
- A quartic has exactly 4 roots.

**Conjugate Root Theorem:** If a polynomial has real coefficients and $a + bi$ is a root (where $b \neq 0$), then $a - bi$ is also a root. Complex roots always come in conjugate pairs for polynomials with real coefficients.

This means:
- A quadratic can have 2 real roots or 2 complex conjugate roots (never 1 real and 1 complex).
- A cubic must have at least 1 real root (since 3 is odd, and complex roots come in pairs).

---

## 4.10 — Special Quadratic Patterns to Memorize

The ACT frequently uses the following patterns. Recognizing them instantly saves significant time.

### 4.10.1 — Difference of Squares
$$a^2 - b^2 = (a + b)(a - b)$$

**Variations:**
- $x^2 - 25 = (x + 5)(x - 5)$
- $4x^2 - 9 = (2x + 3)(2x - 3)$
- $x^4 - 1 = (x^2 + 1)(x^2 - 1) = (x^2 + 1)(x + 1)(x - 1)$

### 4.10.2 — Perfect Square Trinomials
$$(a + b)^2 = a^2 + 2ab + b^2$$
$$(a - b)^2 = a^2 - 2ab + b^2$$

**Variations:**
- $x^2 + 6x + 9 = (x + 3)^2$
- $x^2 - 10x + 25 = (x - 5)^2$
- $4x^2 + 12x + 9 = (2x + 3)^2$

### 4.10.3 — Sum and Difference of Cubes
$$a^3 + b^3 = (a + b)(a^2 - ab + b^2)$$
$$a^3 - b^3 = (a - b)(a^2 + ab + b^2)$$

**Variations:**
- $x^3 + 8 = x^3 + 2^3 = (x + 2)(x^2 - 2x + 4)$
- $x^3 - 27 = x^3 - 3^3 = (x - 3)(x^2 + 3x + 9)$
- $8x^3 + 27 = (2x)^3 + 3^3 = (2x + 3)(4x^2 - 6x + 9)$

**Common error:** Students frequently try to factor $a^3 + b^3$ as $(a + b)^3$ or make sign errors in the trinomial. The trinomial factor always has the **opposite** sign on the $ab$ term from the binomial factor's sign, and the last term is always positive.

---

## 4.11 — Projectile Motion and Real-World Quadratic Modeling

The ACT frequently embeds quadratic relationships in real-world contexts. The most common is **projectile motion**, where the height of an object thrown or launched is modeled by:

$$h(t) = -16t^2 + v_0t + h_0$$

where:
- $h(t)$ = height (in feet) at time $t$ (in seconds)
- $-16$ = acceleration due to gravity (half of $32$ ft/s²)
- $v_0$ = initial velocity (in ft/s)
- $h_0$ = initial height (in feet)

### 4.11.1 — Key Questions About Projectile Motion

**"When does the object hit the ground?"**
Set $h(t) = 0$ and solve for $t$. Discard any negative time values.

**"What is the maximum height?"**
Find the vertex. The $t$-coordinate is $t = -\frac{v_0}{2(-16)} = \frac{v_0}{32}$. Substitute this into the equation to find the maximum height.

**"At what time does the object reach its maximum height?"**
This is the $t$-coordinate of the vertex: $t = \frac{v_0}{32}$.

**"What is the height at time $t = 2$?"**
Substitute $t = 2$ into the equation.

### 4.11.2 — Area Problems

Many ACT geometry problems produce quadratic equations. For example:
- "A rectangle has a perimeter of 40 and an area of 96. What is the length of the longer side?"
  - Let $l$ and $w$ be the dimensions. Then $2l + 2w = 40$ (so $l + w = 20$) and $lw = 96$.
  - From $l + w = 20$, we get $w = 20 - l$. Substituting: $l(20 - l) = 96$, giving $20l - l^2 = 96$, or $l^2 - 20l + 96 = 0$.
  - Factoring: $(l - 12)(l - 8) = 0$, so $l = 12$ or $l = 8$. The longer side is 12.

### 4.11.3 — Profit Maximization

Quadratic profit functions are common: $P(x) = -ax^2 + bx - c$, where $x$ is the number of units sold or the price. The maximum profit occurs at the vertex.

---

## 4.12 — The Quadratic Formula and the Discriminant: Advanced Applications

### 4.12.1 — Finding the Nature of Roots Without Solving

Sometimes the ACT asks you to determine whether roots are real, rational, positive, negative, etc., without actually finding the roots. The discriminant and Vieta's formulas are your tools.

**To determine if both roots are positive:**
- $\Delta \geq 0$ (real roots)
- Sum of roots $= -\frac{b}{a} > 0$
- Product of roots $= \frac{c}{a} > 0$

**To determine if both roots are negative:**
- $\Delta \geq 0$
- Sum of roots $= -\frac{b}{a} < 0$
- Product of roots $= \frac{c}{a} > 0$

**To determine if roots have opposite signs:**
- Product of roots $= \frac{c}{a} < 0$ (this automatically means $\Delta > 0$ for real coefficients)

### 4.12.2 — Quadratic Equations with Parameters

The ACT frequently presents quadratic equations with an unknown parameter and ask for the value of that parameter that produces a specific condition:

- **"For what value of $k$ does $x^2 + kx + 16 = 0$ have exactly one real root?"**
  - Set $\Delta = 0$: $k^2 - 4(1)(16) = 0$ → $k^2 = 64$ → $k = \pm 8$.

- **"For what values of $k$ does $x^2 + kx + 9 = 0$ have two distinct real roots?"**
  - Set $\Delta > 0$: $k^2 - 36 > 0$ → $k^2 > 36$ → $|k| > 6$ → $k < -6$ or $k > 6$.

- **"For what values of $k$ does $x^2 + kx + 25 = 0$ have no real roots?"**
  - Set $\Delta < 0$: $k^2 - 100 < 0$ → $k^2 < 100$ → $|k| < 10$ → $-10 < k < 10$.

### 4.12.3 — The Discriminant as a Function of a Parameter

When the discriminant itself depends on a parameter, you can set up an inequality or equation in that parameter. This is a higher-level skill that separates top scorers from average ones.

**Example:** "The equation $3x^2 + mx + 12 = 0$ has two distinct real solutions. Which of the following is a possible value of $m$?"
