- $\Delta = m^2 - 4(3)(12) = m^2 - 144 > 0$
- $m^2 > 144$
- $|m| > 12$
- Any value with $|m| > 12$ works (e.g., $m = 13$, $m = -15$, etc.)

---

## 4.13 — Graphical Transformations of Quadratic Functions

Understanding how changes to the equation affect the graph is essential for the ACT.

### 4.13.1 — Transformations from the Parent Function $y = x^2$

| Transformation | Equation Change | Effect on Graph |
|---|---|---|
| Vertical shift up by $k$ | $y = x^2 + k$ | Moves graph up by $k$ units |
| Vertical shift down by $k$ | $y = x^2 - k$ | Moves graph down by $k$ units |
| Horizontal shift right by $h$ | $y = (x - h)^2$ | Moves graph right by $h$ units |
| Horizontal shift left by $h$ | $y = (x + h)^2$ | Moves graph left by $h$ units |
| Vertical stretch by factor $a$ | $y = ax^2$ ($a > 1$) | Narrows the parabola |
| Vertical compression by factor $a$ | $y = ax^2$ ($0 < a < 1$) | Widens the parabola |
| Reflection over $x$-axis | $y = -x^2$ | Flips parabola upside down |

**Combined transformation:** $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$ incorporates all of these simultaneously. The vertex moves from $(0, 0)$ to $(h, k)$, and the shape is controlled by $a$.

### 4.13.2 — Vertex from Any Form

- **Standard form $y = ax^2 + bx + c$:** Vertex is $\left(-\frac{b}{2a},\; f\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)\right)$
- **Vertex form $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$:** Vertex is $(h, k)$
- **Intercept form $y = a(x - p)(x - q)$:** Vertex is $\left(\frac{p+q}{2},\; f\left(\frac{p+q}{2}\right)\right)$

### 4.13.3 — Matching Equations to Graphs

When the ACT shows you four graphs and asks which matches a given equation (or vice versa), use this systematic approach:

1. **Check the sign of $a$** — eliminates graphs that open the wrong direction.
2. **Find the $y$-intercept** — eliminates graphs with the wrong $y$-intercept.
3. **Find the vertex** — eliminates graphs with the wrong vertex.
4. **Check the $x$-intercepts** — confirms the correct graph.

This four-step process can eliminate all wrong answers in under 30 seconds.

---

## 4.14 — Systems Involving Quadratics

The ACT occasionally tests systems of equations where one or both equations are quadratic.

### 4.14.1 — Linear-Quadratic Systems

A line and a parabola can intersect at 0, 1, or 2 points.

**Solving by substitution:**
1. Solve the linear equation for one variable.
2. Substitute into the quadratic equation.
3. Solve the resulting quadratic equation.
4. Find the corresponding values of the other variable.

**Geometric interpretation:**
- Two intersection points: the line cuts through the parabola.
- One intersection point: the line is tangent to the parabola.
- No intersection points: the line misses the parabola entirely.

**Discriminant connection:** After substitution, the discriminant of the resulting quadratic tells you the number of intersection points:
- $\Delta > 0$: 2 intersection points
- $\Delta = 0$: 1 intersection point (tangent)
- $\Delta < 0$: 0 intersection points

### 4.14.2 — Quadratic-Quadratic Systems

Two parabolas can intersect at 0, 1, 2, 3, or 4 points (though the ACT typically only tests 0, 1, or 2). The substitution or elimination methods apply, but these are rare on the ACT and usually only appear in the hardest questions.

---

## 4.15 — Radical Equations That Produce Quadratics

Some equations involving radicals can be transformed into quadratic equations by squaring both sides. This introduces the critical issue of **extraneous solutions**.

### 4.15.1 — The Extraneous Solution Problem

When you square both sides of an equation, you may introduce solutions that satisfy the squared equation but not the original equation. **You must always check your solutions in the original equation.**

**Example framework:** $\sqrt{x + 3} = x - 3$
1. Square both sides: $x + 3 = (x - 3)^2 = x^2 - 6x + 9$
2. Rearrange: $0 = x^2 - 7x + 6 = (x - 1)(x - 6)$
3. Potential solutions: $x = 1$ and $x = 6$
4. Check: $\sqrt{1 + 3} = \sqrt{4} = 2$, but $1 - 3 = -2$. So $x = 1$ is extraneous.
5. Check: $\sqrt{6 + 3} = \sqrt{9} = 3$, and $6 - 3 = 3$. So $x = 6$ is valid.

**ACT relevance:** The test frequently includes the extraneous solution as one of the answer choices. If you forget to check, you will select the wrong answer.

### 4.15.2 — Domain Restrictions

For $\sqrt{f(x)} = g(x)$, we need:
1. $f(x) \geq 0$ (the radicand must be non-negative)
2. $g(x) \geq 0$ (since the square root is always non-negative)

These domain restrictions help identify extraneous solutions.

---

## 4.16 — Rational Expressions and Equations That Reduce to Quadratics

Some rational equations, when cleared of denominators, produce quadratic equations. The same extraneous solution concerns apply — any solution that makes a denominator zero is invalid.

**Example framework:** $\frac{x+1}{x-2} = \frac{3}{x+1}$
1. Cross-multiply: $(x+1)^2 = 3(x-2)$
2. Expand: $x^2 + 2x + 1 = 3x - 6$
3. Rearrange: $x^2 - x + 7 = 0$
4. Discriminant: $(-1)^2 - 4(1)(7) = 1 - 28 = -27 < 0$
5. No real solutions.

**Key insight:** Computing the discriminant early can save time. If $\Delta < 0$, you know there are no real solutions without needing to complete the solve.

---

## 4.17 — Exponential Equations That Reduce to Quadratics

Equations of the form $a^{2x} + b \cdot a^x + c = 0$ can be solved by substitution. Let $u = a^x$, then $u^2 + bu + c = 0$, which is a quadratic in $u$.

**Example framework:** $2^{2x} - 5 \cdot 2^x + 6 = 0$
1. Let $u = 2^x$. Then $u^2 - 5u + 6 = 0$.
2. Factor: $(u - 2)(u - 3) = 0$, so $u = 2$ or $u = 3$.
3. Back-substitute: $2^x = 2$ → $x = 1$, or $2^x = 3$ → $x = \log_2 3$.

**ACT relevance:** These problems test whether you recognize the quadratic structure hidden in the exponential form. The substitution technique is the same as the "Plugging In" strategy from Chapter 2 — you are replacing a complex expression with a simpler variable.

---

## 4.18 — Summary for Quadratic Problems

When you encounter any quadratic or higher-order polynomial problem on the ACT, execute this mental checklist:

### Step 1: Classify the Problem
- Is it a pure equation to solve?
- Is it a function to analyze (graph, vertex, intercepts)?
- Is it a word problem that produces a quadratic relationship?
- Is it a system involving a quadratic?
- Is it a radical, exponential, or rational equation that reduces to a quadratic?

### Step 2: Choose the Fastest Method
- **Factoring** for small integer coefficients and rational roots.
- **Quadratic formula** for everything else, especially when exact answers are needed.
- **Square root method** for $(expression)^2 = k$ forms.
- **Vertex formula** ($-\frac{b}{2a}$) when only the vertex is needed.
- **Discriminant** when the question is about the nature or number of roots.
- **Vieta's formulas** when the question is about sums or products of roots.

### Step 3: Execute Carefully
- Set equations equal to zero before identifying $a$, $b$, $c$.
- Watch for sign errors, especially with $-b$ in the quadratic formula.
- Check for extraneous solutions when squaring both sides.
- Verify that your answer answers the actual question asked (not an intermediate value).

### Step 4: Verify and Move On
- If time permits, substitute your answer back into the original equation.
- If the problem involves a graph, mentally check that your answer is consistent with the shape and position of the parabola.
- Do not linger — if a quadratic problem is taking more than 90 seconds, flag it and return later.

---

## 4.19 — Common ACT Traps and How to Avoid Them

### Trap 1: The "Solve for $x$" When the Question Asks for Something Else
If you solve $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$ and get $x = 2$ and $x = 3$, but the question asks for "the sum of the solutions," the answer is $5$, not $2$ or $3$. The values $2$ and $3$ will appear as trap answer choices.

### Trap 2: The Sign Error in the Quadratic Formula
For $2x^2 - 3x - 5 = 0$, the value of $b$ is $-3$, not $3$. So $-b = -(-3) = 3$. The formula gives $x = \frac{3 \pm \sqrt{9 + 40}}{4} = \frac{3 \pm 7}{4}$.

### Trap 3: Forgetting to Set Equal to Zero
$(x + 1)(x - 4) = 5$ does NOT mean $x + 1 = 5$ or $x - 4 = 5$. You must expand, rearrange, and then factor or use the quadratic formula.

### Trap 4: Misreading the Vertex Form
$y = 2(x + 3)^2 - 4$ has vertex $(-3, -4)$, NOT $(3, -4)$ or $(3, 4)$.

### Trap 5: Ignoring the Leading Coefficient
In $3x^2 + 12x + 9 = 0$, you can divide through by 3 to get $x^2 + 4x + 3 = 0$, which is much easier to factor. Always look for a GCF first.

### Trap 6: Assuming All Quadratics Have Two Real Roots
If $\Delta < 0$, there are no real solutions. The answer might be "no real solutions" or the problem might be testing whether you recognize this case.

### Trap 7: Extraneous Solutions in Radical Equations
Always check solutions when you have squared both sides of an equation. The extraneous solution will almost certainly appear as an answer choice.

### Trap 8: Confusing the Discriminant with the Quadratic Formula
The discriminant is $b^2 - 4ac$ — just the expression under the radical. It is a single number, not a formula for $x$. Students sometimes write the discriminant when the question asks for the solutions, or vice versa.

---

## 4.20 — Advanced Topics: Polynomial Division and the Remainder Theorem

### 4.20.1 — Synthetic Division

Synthetic division is a shortcut for dividing a polynomial by a binomial of the form $x - k$. It is faster than long division and directly gives both the quotient and the remainder.

**Setup for dividing $2x^3 - 5x^2 + 3x - 7$ by $x - 3$:**
1. Write the coefficients: $2, -5, 3, -7$
2. Write $k = 3$ to the left.
3. Bring down the first coefficient (2).
4. Multiply by $k$: $2 \times 3 = 6$. Write under the next coefficient.
5. Add: $-5 + 6 = 1$.
6. Multiply by $k$: $1 \times 3 = 3$. Write under the next coefficient.
7. Add: $3 + 3 = 6$.
8. Multiply by $k$: $6 \times 3 = 18$. Write under the last coefficient.
9. Add: $-7 + 18 = 11$.

The result is $2x^2 + x + 6$ with remainder $11$. So:
$$\frac{2x^3 - 5x^2 + 3x - 7}{x - 3} = 2x^2 + x + 6 + \frac{11}{x - 3}$$

### 4.20.2 — Using the Remainder Theorem to Evaluate Polynomials

To find $P(k)$, you can simply use synthetic division — the remainder is $P(k)$. This is often faster than direct substitution, especially for higher-degree polynomials.

**Example:** Find $P(4)$ for $P(x) = x^4 - 3x^3 + 2x^2 - x + 5$.
- Synthetic division with $k = 4$ gives the remainder, which equals $P(4)$.

### 4.20.3 — The Connection to Factoring

If the remainder is 0, then $x - k$ is a factor of $P(x)$. This is the Factor Theorem in action. You can use synthetic division to test potential factors quickly.

---

## 4.21 — Polynomial Inequalities

Similar to quadratic inequalities, higher-degree polynomial inequalities are solved by finding the roots and testing intervals.

**Method:**
1. Factor the polynomial completely.
2. Find all real roots (these are the boundary points).
3. Arrange the roots in order on a number line.
4. Test the sign of the polynomial in each interval.
5. Select the intervals that satisfy the inequality.

**Multiplicity:** If a root has **odd** multiplicity, the sign changes at that root. If a root has **even** multiplicity, the sign does **not** change (the graph touches the $x$-axis and bounces back).

**Example:** $(x + 2)(x - 1)^2(x - 4) < 0$
- Roots: $x = -2$ (multiplicity 1, odd), $x = 1$ (multiplicity 2, even), $x = 4$ (multiplicity 1, odd).
- Sign changes at $x = -2$ and $x = 4$; no sign change at $x = 1$.
- Test intervals: $(-\infty, -2)$, $(-2, 1)$, $(1, 4)$, $(4, \infty)$.

---

## 4.22 — The Relationship Between Roots and Factors

For any polynomial $P(x)$ with roots $r_1, r_2, \ldots, r_n$:
$$P(x) = a(x - r_1)(x - r_2)\cdots(x - r_n)$$

This factorization is unique (up to ordering of factors). Understanding this relationship allows you to:
- Construct a polynomial from its roots.
- Find the remaining roots if some are known.
- Determine the degree of a polynomial from the number of distinct roots.

**ACT application:** "A polynomial $P(x)$ has roots at $x = -1$, $x = 2$, and $x = 5$. If $P(0) = 20$, what is $P(x)$?"
- $P(x) = a(x + 1)(x - 2)(x - 5)$
- $P(0) = a(1)(-2)(-5) = 10a = 20$, so $a = 2$
- $P(x) = 2(x + 1)(x - 2)(x - 5)$

---

## 4.23 — Comprehensive Formula Reference for This Chapter

| Concept | Formula/Property |
|---|---|
| Quadratic Formula | $x = \dfrac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$ |
| Discriminant | $\Delta = b^2 - 4ac$ |
| Vertex $x$-coordinate | $x = -\dfrac{b}{2a}$ |
| Sum of Roots | $-\dfrac{b}{a}$ |
| Product of Roots | $\dfrac{c}{a}$ |
| Difference of Squares | $a^2 - b^2 = (a+b)(a-b)$ |
| Perfect Square (sum) | $(a+b)^2 = a^2 + 2ab + b^2$ |
| Perfect Square (difference) | $(a-b)^2 = a^2 - 2ab + b^2$ |
| Sum of Cubes | $a^3 + b^3 = (a+b)(a^2 - ab + b^2)$ |
| Difference of Cubes | $a^3 - b^3 = (a-b)(a^2 + ab + b^2)$ |
| Zero Product Property | If $AB = 0$, then $A = 0$ or $B = 0$ |
| Factor Theorem | $x - k$ is a factor of $P(x)$ iff $P(k) = 0$ |
| Remainder Theorem | $P(k)$ = remainder when $P(x)$ is divided by $x - k$ |

---

## 4.24 — Strategic Mindset and Test-Day Execution

### 4.24.1 — Recognizing Quadratic Structure

The single most important skill for this chapter is **pattern recognition**. Many ACT problems disguise quadratic structure:
- A word problem about area produces a quadratic.
- An equation with $x^4$ can be treated as a quadratic in $x^2$.
- An exponential equation with $a^{2x}$ and $a^x$ can be treated as a quadratic in $a^x$.
- A rational equation, when cleared of denominators, may produce a quadratic.

Train yourself to see the quadratic skeleton beneath every wrapper.

### 4.24.2 — Speed Through Memorization

You must have the following memorized cold — no derivation time on test day:
- The quadratic formula
- The discriminant expression
- The vertex formula $-\frac{b}{2a}$
- Vieta's formulas (sum and product of roots)
- All special factoring patterns (difference of squares, perfect square trinomials, sum/difference of cubes)
- The Zero Product Property

### 4.24.3 — Calculator Strategy for Quadratics

Use your calculator strategically:
- **For the quadratic formula:** Compute the discriminant first, then compute each root separately.
- **For checking solutions:** Substitute your answer back into the original equation.
- **For graphing:** If you have a graphing calculator, use it to visualize the parabola and verify your algebraic work.
- **For evaluating polynomials:** Use the calculator to evaluate $P(k)$ quickly, especially for higher-degree polynomials.

Do not use the calculator for simple factoring or mental math problems — this wastes time.

### 4.24.4 — The 90-Second Rule for Quadratic Problems

If you have not made clear progress on a quadratic problem within 90 seconds:
1. Reassess your method — is there a faster approach?
2. Consider whether the discriminant or Vieta's formulas can shortcut the computation.
3. If still stuck, flag the question and move on. Return if time permits.

Every question is worth one raw point. A quadratic problem that takes 3 minutes is time stolen from two easier problems later in the test.

---

## 4.25 — Interconnections with Other Chapters

This chapter does not exist in isolation. Quadratic and polynomial concepts connect to:

- **Chapter 3 (Algebra & Linear Functions):** Systems of linear equations can extend to linear-quadratic systems. The concept of slope connects to the derivative-like behavior of quadratics (the slope of the tangent line at the vertex is zero).
- **Chapter 5 (Functions & Modeling):** Quadratic functions are a major function type tested on the ACT. Function notation, domain, range, and transformations all apply to quadratics.
- **Chapter 6 (Geometry & Trigonometry):** Area problems frequently produce quadratic equations. Projectile motion problems connect to physics applications. The equation of a circle $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ is a quadratic equation in two variables.
- **Chapter 8 (Data Analysis & Statistics):** The deviation formula involves squaring, and the concept of minimizing squared deviations is fundamentally connected to quadratic optimization.

Understanding these interconnections will deepen your comprehension and improve your ability to recognize the underlying structure of any problem the ACT presents.

---

*This chapter has provided a comprehensive, exhaustive treatment of quadratic equations, polynomials, and higher-order equations as they appear on the ACT Mathematics Test. Mastery of these concepts, formulas, and strategies is essential for any score above 25 and is foundational for the advanced topics covered in subsequent chapters.*

---


# Chapter 5: Geometry and Trigonometry Deep-Dive — Theorems, Proofs, Properties, and Planar Reasoning

---

## 5.1 — Why Geometry and Trigonometry Demand a Deep-Dive Approach

Geometry and Trigonometry collectively account for approximately 12–15% of all ACT Mathematics questions under the "Geometry" reporting category within Preparing for Higher Math. However, this percentage understates their true importance. Geometric reasoning bleeds into algebra (coordinate geometry), functions (graphing conics and trigonometric functions), and even statistics (interpreting visual data displays). A student who is truly fluent in geometry and trig possesses a flexible, visual reasoning toolkit that can be deployed across the entire exam.

The ACT does not merely ask you to plug numbers into formulas. It tests whether you understand *why* formulas work, *when* to apply them, and *how* geometric relationships interact in multi-step problems. A question might combine the Pythagorean Theorem with similar triangles, or embed a trigonometric ratio inside a circle geometry problem. The exam rewards students who see the connections between topics.

This chapter provides a rigorous, comprehensive treatment of every geometric and trigonometric concept that can appear on the ACT. We will build from foundational definitions, progress through theorems and their proofs, explore special cases and "ACT traps," and develop a strategic framework for approaching any geometry problem on test day.

---

## 5.2 — Foundational Definitions: The Building Blocks of Geometry

Before we can reason about triangles, circles, and trigonometric ratios, we must establish a precise vocabulary. Every geometric argument rests on these definitions.

### Points, Lines, and Planes

- **Point:** A location in space with no dimension (no length, width, or thickness). Points are typically labeled with capital letters (e.g., point $A$, point $B$).
- **Line:** A one-dimensional figure that extends infinitely in both directions. A line is determined by any two points on it. We denote a line through points $A$ and $B$ as $\overleftrightarrow{AB}$.
- **Line Segment:** A portion of a line between two endpoints. Segment $\overline{AB}$ has endpoints $A$ and $B$, and its length is denoted $AB$ (without the bar).
- **Ray:** A portion of a line that starts at one endpoint and extends infinitely in one direction. Ray $\overrightarrow{AB}$ starts at $A$ and passes through $B$.
- **Plane:** A flat, two-dimensional surface that extends infinitely in all directions. A plane is determined by three non-collinear points.

### Angles

An **angle** is formed by two rays sharing a common endpoint called the **vertex**. The measure of an angle is determined by the amount of rotation between the two rays, typically measured in degrees (°).

- **Acute Angle:** Measures strictly between $0°$ and $90°$.
- **Right Angle:** Measures exactly $90°$. Right angles are often indicated by a small square at the vertex.
- **Obtuse Angle:** Measures strictly between $90°$ and $180°$.
- **Straight Angle:** Measures exactly $180°$. The two rays form a straight line.
- **Reflex Angle:** Measures strictly between $180°$ and $360°$ (rarely tested on the ACT but worth knowing).

### Angle Relationships

- **Complementary Angles:** Two angles whose measures sum to $90°$. For example, if one angle measures $37°$, its complement measures $53°$.
- **Supplementary Angles:** Two angles whose measures sum to $180°$. For example, if one angle measures $112°$, its supplement measures $68°$.
- **Adjacent Angles:** Two angles that share a common vertex and a common side but do not overlap.
- **Vertical (Opposite) Angles:** When two lines intersect, the angles opposite each other are called vertical angles. **Vertical angles are always congruent** (equal in measure). This is a frequently tested property on the ACT.
- **Linear Pair:** Two adjacent angles whose non-common sides form a straight line. Angles in a linear pair are always supplementary.

### Parallel Lines and Transversals

When a **transversal** (a line that intersects two or more other lines) cuts through two parallel lines, several angle relationships are created:

- **Corresponding Angles:** Angles in the same relative position at each intersection. Corresponding angles are **congruent** when the lines are parallel.
- **Alternate Interior Angles:** Angles on opposite sides of the transversal and inside the two parallel lines. Alternate interior angles are **congruent** when the lines are parallel.
- **Alternate Exterior Angles:** Angles on opposite sides of the transversal and outside the two parallel lines. Alternate exterior angles are **congruent** when the lines are parallel.
- **Same-Side (Consecutive) Interior Angles:** Angles on the same side of the transversal and inside the two parallel lines. Same-side interior angles are **supplementary** (sum to $180°$) when the lines are parallel.

**ACT Strategy Note:** When you see parallel lines on the ACT, immediately mark all known angle relationships. Even if the problem doesn't explicitly state that two lines are parallel, look for the arrow notation (small arrows on lines indicating parallelism). Many students miss "free points" by failing to recognize these angle relationships.

---

## 5.3 — Triangles: The Most Important Polygon

Triangles are the single most tested polygon on the ACT. Their properties form the foundation for nearly all of geometry, including trigonometry, circle geometry, and coordinate geometry.

### 5.3.1 — Classification of Triangles

**By Side Length:**
- **Equilateral Triangle:** All three sides are congruent. All three angles measure $60°$.
- **Isosceles Triangle:** At least two sides are congruent. The angles opposite the congruent sides (called **base angles**) are also congruent.
- **Scalene Triangle:** No two sides are congruent. No two angles are congruent.

**By Angle Measure:**
- **Acute Triangle:** All three angles are acute (less than $90°$).
- **Right Triangle:** One angle measures exactly $90°$. The side opposite the right angle is the **hypotenuse**; the other two sides are **legs**.
- **Obtuse Triangle:** One angle is obtuse (greater than $90°$).

### 5.3.2 — The Triangle Angle Sum Theorem

**Theorem:** The sum of the interior angles of any triangle is $180°$.

**Proof Sketch:** Given triangle $ABC$, draw a line through point $C$ parallel to $\overline{AB}$. The alternate interior angles at $A$ and $B$ are congruent to the angles formed at $C$ by the parallel line. The three angles at $C$ form a straight angle ($180°$), so $\angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180°$.

**ACT Application:** This theorem is used on virtually every triangle problem. If you know two angles, you can always find the third. If the problem states that one angle is twice another, or that the angles are in a ratio like $2:3:5$, you can set up an equation: $2x + 3x + 5x = 180°$, so $10x = 180°$, giving $x = 18°$.

### 5.3.3 — The Exterior Angle Theorem

**Theorem:** The measure of an exterior angle of a triangle equals the sum of the measures of the two **remote interior angles** (the two interior angles that are not adjacent to the exterior angle).

**Proof Sketch:** At vertex $C$ of triangle $ABC$, extend side $\overline{BC}$ to point $D$. The exterior angle $\angle ACD$ and the interior angle $\angle ACB$ form a linear pair, so they sum to $180°$. But the three interior angles also sum to $180°$. Therefore, $\angle ACD = \angle A + \angle B$.

**ACT Application:** The exterior angle theorem is a powerful shortcut. If a problem gives you an exterior angle and asks for a remote interior angle, or vice versa, this theorem gives you the relationship immediately. Also, the sum of all three exterior angles (one at each vertex) of any convex polygon is always $360°$.

### 5.3.4 — The Triangle Inequality Theorem

**Theorem:** The length of any side of a triangle must be **strictly less than** the sum of the lengths of the other two sides and **strictly greater than** the positive difference of the lengths of the other two sides.

Formally, for a triangle with side lengths $a$, $b$, and $c$:
$$|b - c| < a < b + c$$

**ACT Application:** The ACT frequently tests this concept. A typical question might say: "Two sides of a triangle have lengths 5 and 9. Which of the following could be the length of the third side?" The answer must satisfy $9 - 5 < x < 9 + 5$, i.e., $4 < x < 14$. Any value in this range (but not equal to 4 or 14) is valid.

**Trap Alert:** The third side cannot *equal* the sum or difference. A "triangle" with sides 4, 5, 9 would be degenerate (a straight line), not a triangle.

### 5.3.5 — Side-Angle Relationships in Triangles

**Theorem:** In any triangle, the longest side is opposite the largest angle, and the shortest side is opposite the smallest angle. Conversely, the largest angle is opposite the longest side.

**ACT Application:** This theorem allows you to order the sides of a triangle if you know the angles, and vice versa. For example, if a triangle has angles measuring $30°$, $60°$, and $90°$, the side opposite the $30°$ angle is the shortest, the side opposite the $60°$ angle is the middle-length side, and the hypotenuse (opposite the $90°$ angle) is the longest.

---

## 5.4 — The Pythagorean Theorem and Its Converse

### 5.4.1 — The Theorem

**Theorem:** In a right triangle with legs of lengths $a$ and $b$ and hypotenuse of length $c$:
$$a^2 + b^2 = c^2$$

**Proof Sketch (Geometric):** Construct a square with side length $(a + b)$. Inside it, arrange four copies of the right triangle, each with legs $a$ and $b$. The area of the large square is $(a + b)^2$. The area can also be computed as the area of the four triangles plus the area of the inner square (which has side length $c$):
$$(a + b)^2 = 4 \cdot \frac{1}{2}ab + c^2$$
$$a^2 + 2ab + b^2 = 2ab + c^2$$
$$a^2 + b^2 = c^2$$

### 5.4.2 — The Converse

**Theorem (Converse of the Pythagorean Theorem):** If a triangle has side lengths $a$, $b$, and $c$ such that $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$, then the triangle is a right triangle with hypotenuse $c$.

**ACT Application:** The converse is tested frequently. A question might give you three side lengths (e.g., 7, 24, 25) and ask whether the triangle is right. Check: $7^2 + 24^2 = 49 + 576 = 625 = 25^2$. Yes, it is a right triangle.

### 5.4.3 — Pythagorean Triples

A **Pythagorean triple** consists of three positive integers $a$, $b$, and $c$ such that $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$. The ACT frequently uses Pythagorean triples because they allow for quick recognition without a calculator.

**Essential Pythagorean Triples to Memorize:**

| Triple | Multiples to Know |
|---|---|
| 3, 4, 5 | 6, 8, 10; 9, 12, 15; 12, 16, 20; 15, 20, 25 |
| 5, 12, 13 | 10, 24, 26 |
| 8, 15, 17 | 16, 30, 34 |
| 7, 24, 25 | 14, 48, 50 |

**ACT Strategy Note:** When you see a right triangle with two sides from a known triple, you can immediately identify the third side. For example, if a right triangle has a hypotenuse of 25 and one leg of 15, the other leg must be 20 (from the 15-20-25 triple, which is a multiple of 3-4-5 scaled by 5). This saves significant time.

### 5.4.4 — Extensions: Distance in the Coordinate Plane

The Pythagorean Theorem is the foundation of the **Distance Formula**. For two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ in the coordinate plane:
$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

This formula is derived by constructing a right triangle where the segment connecting the two points is the hypotenuse, the horizontal leg has length $|x_2 - x_1|$, and the vertical leg has length $|y_2 - y_1|$.

---

## 5.5 — Special Right Triangles

Two right triangles appear so frequently on the ACT that their side ratios must be memorized cold. These are the **45°-45°-90°** triangle and the **30°-60°-90°** triangle.

### 5.5.1 — The 45°-45°-90° Triangle

**Properties:**
- This is an isosceles right triangle.
- The two legs are congruent.
- The angles measure $45°$, $45°$, and $90°$.
- **Side Ratio:** If each leg has length $s$, the hypotenuse has length $s\sqrt{2}$.

**Derivation:** By the Pythagorean Theorem, if both legs equal $s$, then the hypotenuse $c$ satisfies $s^2 + s^2 = c^2$, so $2s^2 = c^2$, giving $c = s\sqrt{2}$.

**ACT Application:** If a 45°-45°-90° triangle has a leg of length 7, the hypotenuse is $7\sqrt{2}$. If the hypotenuse is $10$, each leg is $\frac{10}{\sqrt{2}} = 5\sqrt{2}$ (after rationalizing the denominator).

**Connection to Squares:** The diagonal of a square with side length $s$ is $s\sqrt{2}$. This is because the diagonal divides the square into two 45°-45°-90° triangles.

### 5.5.2 — The 30°-60°-90° Triangle

**Properties:**
- The angles measure $30°$, $60°$, and $90°$.
- **Side Ratio:** If the shortest leg (opposite the $30°$ angle) has length $s$, then the longer leg (opposite the $60°$ angle) has length $s\sqrt{3}$, and the hypotenuse has length $2s$.

**Derivation:** Start with an equilateral triangle of side length $2s$. Draw an altitude from one vertex to the opposite side. This altitude bisects the opposite side (into two segments of length $s$) and bisects the $60°$ angle (into two $30°$ angles). The altitude creates two congruent 30°-60°-90° triangles. By the Pythagorean Theorem on one of these triangles: $s^2 + h^2 = (2s)^2$, so $h^2 = 4s^2 - s^2 = 3s^2$, giving $h = s\sqrt{3}$.

**ACT Application:** If a 30°-60°-90° triangle has a short leg of length 4, the long leg is $4\sqrt{3}$ and the hypotenuse is 8. If the hypotenuse is 14, the short leg is 7 and the long leg is $7\sqrt{3}$.

**Connection to Equilateral Triangles:** The altitude of an equilateral triangle with side length $s$ is $\frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$. This comes directly from the 30°-60°-90° relationship.

### 5.5.3 — The ACT's Favorite Angle: 53° (and the 3-4-5 Connection)

The ACT occasionally uses a right triangle with angles approximately $37°$, $53°$, and $90°$. This is the **3-4-5 triangle** viewed from a trigonometric perspective:
- $\sin(53°) \approx 0.80 = \frac{4}{5}$
- $\cos(53°) \approx 0.60 = \frac{3}{5}$
- $\tan(53°) \approx 1.33 = \frac{4}{3}$

These approximations are not exact (the actual angle whose sine is 0.80 is approximately $53.13°$), but the ACT uses them as convenient approximations. If you see a problem involving a 3-4-5 triangle and trigonometric ratios, these values will appear.

---

## 5.6 — Congruence and Similarity

### 5.6.1 — Congruent Triangles

Two triangles are **congruent** if they have the same size and shape — all corresponding sides and angles are equal. We denote congruence with the symbol $\cong$.

**Congruence Postulates (Sufficient Conditions):**

1. **SSS (Side-Side-Side):** If all three sides of one triangle are congruent to all three sides of another, the triangles are congruent.
2. **SAS (Side-Angle-Side):** If two sides and the included angle of one triangle are congruent to the corresponding parts of another, the triangles are congruent.
3. **ASA (Angle-Side-Angle):** If two angles and the included side of one triangle are congruent to the corresponding parts of another, the triangles are congruent.
4. **AAS (Angle-Angle-Side):** If two angles and a non-included side of one triangle are congruent to the corresponding parts of another, the triangles are congruent. (This follows from ASA because if two angles are known, the third is determined by the angle sum.)
5. **HL (Hypotenuse-Leg):** Applicable only to right triangles. If the hypotenuse and one leg of a right triangle are congruent to the hypotenuse and one leg of another right triangle, the triangles are congruent.

**Important Note — SSA is NOT a valid congruence postulate:** Two sides and a non-included angle do not guarantee congruence. This is called the "ambiguous case" and can produce two different triangles.

### 5.6.2 — Similar Triangles

Two triangles are **similar** if they have the same shape but not necessarily the same size. Corresponding angles are congruent, and corresponding sides are proportional. We denote similarity with the symbol $\sim$.

**Similarity Postulates (Sufficient Conditions):**

1. **AA (Angle-Angle):** If two angles of one triangle are congruent to two angles of another, the triangles are similar. (The third angle is automatically congruent by the angle sum.)
2. **SSS Similarity:** If all three pairs of corresponding sides are proportional, the triangles are similar.
3. **SAS Similarity:** If two pairs of corresponding sides are proportional and the included angles are congruent, the triangles are similar.

**Why Similarity Matters on the ACT:** Similar triangles are one of the most frequently tested geometry concepts. The ACT loves to embed similar triangles inside larger figures — for example, a shadow problem, a flagpole problem, or a problem involving parallel lines cutting through a triangle.

### 5.6.3 — The Midsegment Theorem

**Theorem:** The segment connecting the midpoints of two sides of a triangle (called a **midsegment**) is parallel to the third side and half its length.

**ACT Application:** If a midsegment has length 7, the third side has length 14. Furthermore, the midsegment creates a smaller triangle that is similar to the original triangle with a scale factor of $1:2$.

### 5.6.4 — The Triangle Proportionality Theorem (Basic Proportionality)

**Theorem:** If a line is parallel to one side of a triangle and intersects the other two sides, it divides those sides proportionally.

**ACT Application:** This is the foundation for many "parallel line inside a triangle" problems. If $\overline{DE} \parallel \overline{BC}$ in triangle $ABC$, with $D$ on $\overline{AB}$ and $E$ on $\overline{AC}$, then $\frac{AD}{DB} = \frac{AE}{EC}$.

---

## 5.7 — Triangle Centers and Special Points

### 5.7.1 — Centroid

The **centroid** is the point where the three **medians** of a triangle intersect. A median connects a vertex to the midpoint of the opposite side.

**Properties:**
- The centroid divides each median in a $2:1$ ratio, with the longer segment being closer to the vertex.
- The centroid is the **center of mass** (balance point) of the triangle.
- The three medians divide the triangle into six smaller triangles of equal area.

### 5.7.2 — Orthocenter

The **orthocenter** is the point where the three **altitudes** of a triangle intersect. An altitude is a perpendicular segment from a vertex to the line containing the opposite side.

**Properties:**
- In an acute triangle, the orthocenter lies inside the triangle.
- In a right triangle, the orthocenter is at the vertex of the right angle.
- In an obtuse triangle, the orthocenter lies outside the triangle.

### 5.7.3 — Circumcenter

The **circumcenter** is the point where the three **perpendicular bisectors** of the sides intersect.

**Properties:**
- The circumcenter is equidistant from all three vertices.
- It is the center of the **circumcircle** (the circle passing through all three vertices).
- In an acute triangle, the circumcenter lies inside. In a right triangle, it lies on the hypotenuse (at its midpoint). In an obtuse triangle, it lies outside.

### 5.7.4 — Incenter

The **incenter** is the point where the three **angle bisectors** intersect.

**Properties:**
- The incenter is equidistant from all three sides.
- It is the center of the **incircle** (the circle tangent to all three sides).
- The incenter always lies inside the triangle.

**ACT Note:** The ACT rarely asks about all four centers in detail, but you should know the definitions and the key property of each. The most commonly tested are the centroid (center of mass) and the incenter/circumcenter (circle-related).

---

## 5.8 — Polygons

### 5.8.1 — Polygon Angle Sum

**Theorem:** The sum of the interior angles of a convex polygon with $n$ sides is:
$$(n - 2) \times 180°$$

**Proof Sketch:** From any vertex of the polygon, draw all possible diagonals to non-adjacent vertices. This divides the polygon into $(n - 2)$ triangles. Since each triangle has an angle sum of $180°$, the total is $(n - 2) \times 180°$.

**Examples:**
- Triangle ($n = 3$): $(3 - 2) \times 180° = 180°$
- Quadrilateral ($n = 4$): $(4 - 2) \times 180° = 360°$
- Pentagon ($n = 5$): $(5 - 2) \times 180° = 540°$
- Hexagon ($n = 6$): $(6 - 2) \times 180° = 720°$

### 5.8.2 — Regular Polygons

A **regular polygon** has all sides congruent and all angles congruent.

**Measure of each interior angle of a regular $n$-gon:**
$$\frac{(n - 2) \times 180°}{n}$$

**Measure of each exterior angle of a regular $n$-gon:**
$$\frac{360°}{n}$$

**ACT Application:** The sum of the exterior angles (one at each vertex) of *any* convex polygon is always $360°$, regardless of the number of sides. This is a frequently tested fact.

### 5.8.3 — Quadrilaterals

**Parallelogram:**
- Opposite sides are parallel and congruent.
- Opposite angles are congruent.
- Consecutive angles are supplementary.
- Diagonals bisect each other.

**Rectangle:**
- A parallelogram with four right angles.
- Diagonals are congruent.

**Rhombus:**
- A parallelogram with four congruent sides.
- Diagonals are perpendicular.
- Diagonals bisect the interior angles.

**Square:**
- Both a rectangle and a rhombus.
- Four congruent sides and four right angles.
- Diagonals are congruent, perpendicular, and bisect the angles.

**Trapezoid:**
- Exactly one pair of parallel sides (called **bases**).
- The **median** (or midsegment) of a trapezoid connects the midpoints of the non-parallel sides and its length equals the average of the bases: $\frac{b_1 + b_2}{2}$.

**Isosceles Trapezoid:**
- Non-parallel sides (legs) are congruent.
- Base angles are congruent.
- Diagonals are congruent.

---

## 5.9 — Area, Perimeter, and the Scaling Laws

### 5.9.1 — Essential Area Formulas

| Shape | Area Formula | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Triangle | $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$ | $b$ = base, $h$ = height (perpendicular to base) |
| Rectangle | $A = lw$ | $l$ = length, $w$ = width |
| Square | $A = s^2$ | $s$ = side length |
| Parallelogram | $A = bh$ | $b$ = base, $h$ = perpendicular height |
| Trapezoid | $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$ | $b_1$ and $b_2$ are the parallel bases |
| Circle | $A = \pi r^2$ | $r$ = radius |
| Sector of a Circle | $A = \frac{\theta}{360°} \times \pi r^2$ | $\theta$ = central angle in degrees |
| Regular Polygon | $A = \frac{1}{2}ap$ | $a$ = apothem, $p$ = perimeter |

### 5.9.2 — Essential Perimeter and Circumference Formulas

| Shape | Perimeter Formula | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Triangle | $P = a + b + c$ | Sum of all three sides |
| Rectangle | $P = 2l + 2w$ | |
| Square | $P = 4s$ | |
| Circle (Circumference) | $C = 2\pi r = \pi d$ | $d$ = diameter = $2r$ |

### 5.9.3 — The Scaling Laws for Similar Figures

When two figures are similar with a linear scale factor of $k$ (the ratio of corresponding lengths):

| Property | Scale Factor |
|---|---|
| Lengths | $k$ |
| Perimeters | $k$ |
| Areas | $k^2$ |
| Volumes (3D) | $k^3$ |

**ACT Application:** If the side length of a square is tripled, the area increases by a factor of $3^2 = 9$, not 3. If the radius of a circle is doubled, the area increases by a factor of $2^2 = 4$.

**Trap Recognition:** The scaling law for area is one of the most common traps on the ACT. Students frequently apply the linear scale factor to area problems. Always square the scale factor for areas, cube it for volumes.

---

## 5.10 — Circles: Properties, Arcs, Sectors, and Angles

### 5.10.1 — Fundamental Circle Definitions

- **Center:** The point equidistant from all points on the circle.
- **Radius:** A segment from the center to any point on the circle. All radii of a given circle are congruent.
- **Chord:** A segment whose endpoints lie on the circle.
- **Diameter:** A chord that passes through the center. The diameter is twice the radius: $d = 2r$.
- **Tangent:** A line that intersects the circle at exactly one point. The tangent line is perpendicular to the radius drawn to the point of tangency.
- **Secant:** A line that intersects the circle at two points.
- **Arc:** A continuous portion of the circle's circumference.
- **Sector:** The region bounded by two radii and the intercepted arc (like a "slice of pie").
- **Segment:** The region bounded by a chord and the intercepted arc.

### 5.10.2 — The Equation of a Circle

The standard form of a circle with center $(h, k)$ and radius $r$ is:
$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

**Example:** $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 25$ has center $(3, -2)$ and radius $r = \sqrt{25} = 5$.

**ACT Trap:** The signs inside the parentheses are reversed for the center coordinates. $(x + 1)^2 + (y - 4)^2 = 9$ has center $(-1, 4)$, NOT $(1, -4)$.

### 5.10.3 — Arc Length and Sector Area

**Arc Length:** The length of an arc with central angle $\theta$ (in degrees) in a circle of radius $r$ is:
$$\text{Arc Length} = \frac{\theta}{360°} \times 2\pi r$$

**Sector Area:** The area of a sector with central angle $\theta$ (in degrees) in a circle of radius $r$ is:
$$\text{Sector Area} = \frac{\theta}{360°} \times \pi r^2$$

**Conceptual Understanding:** Both formulas are based on the fraction of the circle represented by the central angle. A $90°$ sector is $\frac{90°}{360°} = \frac{1}{4}$ of the full circle, so its area is $\frac{1}{4}\pi r^2$ and its arc length is $\frac{1}{4}(2\pi r) = \frac{\pi r}{2}$.

### 5.10.4 — Inscribed Angles and Intercepted Arcs

**Theorem:** The measure of an inscribed angle is **half** the measure of its intercepted arc.

**ACT Application:** If an inscribed angle intercepts an arc of $80°$, the inscribed angle measures $40°$.

**Thales' Theorem:** An inscribed angle that intercepts a diameter is always a right angle ($90°$). This means any triangle inscribed in a circle with the diameter as one side is a right triangle.

### 5.10.5 — Central Angles and Intercepted Arcs

**Theorem:** The measure of a central angle equals the measure of its intercepted arc.

**ACT Application:** A central angle of $120°$ intercepts an arc of $120°$.

### 5.10.6 — Tangent Lines and Radii

**Theorem:** A tangent line to a circle is perpendicular to the radius drawn to the point of tangency.

**ACT Application:** If a line is tangent to a circle at point $P$, and $O$ is the center of the circle, then $\overline{OP}$ is perpendicular to the tangent line. This creates a right triangle, which allows the use of the Pythagorean Theorem.

---

## 5.11 — Three-Dimensional Geometry

### 5.11.1 — Volume Formulas

| Solid | Volume Formula | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Rectangular Prism | $V = lwh$ | $l$ = length, $w$ = width, $h$ = height |
| Cylinder | $V = \pi r^2 h$ | $r$ = radius of base, $h$ = height |
| Sphere | $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ | $r$ = radius |
| Cone | $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ | $r$ = radius of base, $h$ = height |
| Pyramid | $V = \frac{1}{3}Bh$ | $B$ = area of base, $h$ = height |

**Critical Insight:** The volume of a cone is exactly $\frac{1}{3}$ the volume of a cylinder with the same base and height. The volume of a pyramid is exactly $\frac{1}{3}$ the volume of a prism with the same base and height. This relationship is frequently tested.

### 5.11.2 — Surface Area Formulas

| Solid | Surface Area Formula | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Rectangular Prism | $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$ | Sum of areas of all 6 faces |
| Cylinder | $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ | Two circular bases + lateral surface |
| Sphere | $SA = 4\pi r^2$ | |

### 5.11.3 — Volume Scaling

If all linear dimensions of a solid are multiplied by a factor of $k$:
- Surface area is multiplied by $k^2$
- Volume is multiplied by $k^3$

**Example:** If a sphere's radius is doubled, its volume increases by a factor of $2^3 = 8$, not 2 or 4.

---

## 5.12 — Trigonometry: Ratios, Identities, and Applications

### 5.12.1 — The Three Basic Trigonometric Ratios

For a right triangle with an acute angle $\theta$:

$$\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$

$$\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$

$$\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$$

**Mnemonic:** SOH CAH TOA (Sine = Opposite/Hypotenuse, Cosine = Adjacent/Hypotenuse, Tangent = Opposite/Adjacent)

**Labeling the Sides:**
- **Opposite:** The side across from the angle $\theta$.
- **Adjacent:** The side next to the angle $\theta$ that is NOT the hypotenuse.
- **Hypotenuse:** The longest side, always opposite the right angle.

### 5.12.2 — The Connection to Similar Triangles

The reason trigonometric ratios work is that all right triangles with the same acute angle are similar. Therefore, the ratios of corresponding sides are constant. $\sin(53°) = 0.80$ means that in *any* right triangle with a $53°$ angle, the ratio of the opposite side to the hypotenuse is always $0.80$.

### 5.12.3 — The Pythagorean Identity

For any angle $\theta$:
$$\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$

**ACT Application:** If you know $\sin(\theta) = \frac{3}{5}$, you can find $\cos(\theta)$:
$$\cos^2(\theta) = 1 - \sin^2(\theta) = 1 - \frac{9}{25} = \frac{16}{25}$$
$$\cos(\theta) = \frac{4}{5}$$

### 5.12.4 — The Relationship Between Sine and Cosine of Complementary Angles

$$\sin(\theta) = \cos(90° - \theta)$$

**ACT Application:** $\sin(30°) = \cos(60°) = \frac{1}{2}$.

### 5.12.5 — Solving Right Triangles with Trigonometry

**When to use trig on the ACT:**
- You know one acute angle and one side, and need to find another side.
- You know two sides and need to find an angle.

**Example:** In a right triangle, the hypotenuse is 10 and one acute angle is $30°$. Find the length of the side opposite the $30°$ angle.

$$\sin(30°) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{10}$$
$$\frac{1}{2} = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{10}$$
$$\text{Opposite} = 5$$

### 5.12.6 — The Law of Sines (Rare on ACT, but Worth Knowing)

For any triangle with sides $a$, $b$, $c$ opposite angles $A$, $B$, $C$ respectively:
$$\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{c}{\sin C}$$

**ACT Note:** The Law of Sines appears very rarely on the ACT. When it does, it is typically in the last 10 questions of the section.

### 5.12.7 — The Law of Cosines (Rare on ACT)

For any triangle with sides $a$, $b$, $c$ where $C$ is the angle opposite side $c$:
$$c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - 2ab\cos(C)$$

**ACT Note:** The Law of Cosines is even rarer than the Law of Sines on the ACT. It generalizes the Pythagorean Theorem to non-right triangles.

---

## 5.13 — Coordinate Geometry of Geometric Figures

### 5.13.1 — Equations of Lines in the Coordinate Plane

**Slope-Intercept Form:** $y = mx + b$
- $m$ = slope
- $b$ = y-intercept

**Point-Slope Form:** $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$
- $m$ = slope
- $(x_1, y_1)$ = a point on the line

**Standard Form:** $Ax + By = C$
- Slope = $-\frac{A}{B}$
- x-intercept = $\frac{C}{A}$
- y-intercept = $\frac{C}{B}$

### 5.13.2 — Parallel and Perpendicular Lines in the Coordinate Plane

**Parallel Lines:** Have the same slope. If $y = 3x + 2$ and $y = 3x - 5$, the lines are parallel.

**Perpendicular Lines:** Have slopes that are negative reciprocals. If $y = \frac{2}{3}x + 1$, a perpendicular line has slope $-\frac{3}{2}$.

### 5.13.3 — Reflections and Symmetry in the Coordinate Plane

**Reflection across the x-axis:** $(x, y) \to (x, -y)$
**Reflection across the y-axis:** $(x, y) \to (-x, y)$
**Reflection through the origin:** $(x, y) \to (-x, -y)$
**Reflection across the line $y = x$:** $(x, y) \to (y, x)$

---

## 5.14 — Common ACT Traps and Strategic Warnings for Geometry and Trigonometry

### 5.14.1 — The "Not Drawn to Scale" Trap

The ACT includes the disclaimer (unless otherwise stated) that **figures are not necessarily drawn to scale**. This means:
- An angle that looks like $90°$ may not be $90°$.
- A side that looks longer than another may actually be shorter.
- A point that looks like it is in the middle of a segment may not be.

**Never rely on visual estimation from the provided figure.** Use the figure as a reference for the *structure* of the problem, but rely on the **given numerical values** and **stated relationships** for your calculations.

### 5.14.2 — The "Solve for $x$" vs. "Solve for $2x$" Trap

If a problem asks for the value of $2x$ and you solve for $x$, the value of $x$ will appear as a **distractor answer choice**. Always double-check what the question is asking for.

### 5.14.3 — The "Area vs. Perimeter" Trap

The ACT frequently asks for area when students compute perimeter, or vice versa. Circle the word "area" or "perimeter" in the question stem to ensure you are computing the correct quantity.

### 5.14.4 — The "Radius vs. Diameter" Trap

If a problem gives you the diameter but the formula requires the radius (or vice versa), the ACT will include the answer that results from using the wrong value. Always check whether you are working with $r$ or $d = 2r$.

### 5.14.5 — The "Degrees vs. Radians" Trap

The ACT almost always uses degrees for angle measures. If a problem involves radians, it will be explicitly stated. However, be aware that some calculator functions default to radians — make sure your calculator is in the correct mode.

### 5.14.6 — The "Similar Figures Area Ratio" Trap

When two similar figures have a linear scale factor of $k$, the area ratio is $k^2$, not $k$. The ACT includes the linear scale factor as a distractor for area questions.

### 5.14.7 — The "Tangent Line Perpendicular to Radius" Trap

When a tangent line meets a circle, it is perpendicular to the radius at the point of tangency. This creates a right triangle, which is often the key to solving the problem. Students who miss this perpendicular relationship often cannot solve tangent problems.

---

## 5.15 — Strategic Framework for Geometry Problems

### 5.15.1 — The Drawing Protocol

1. **If a figure is provided:** Label all given measurements and relationships immediately.
2. **If no figure is provided:** Draw one. Label all given information.
3. **As you solve:** Add new information to your figure as you discover it.
4. **If you get stuck:** Re-examine your figure. Is there a relationship you missed? A right triangle you didn't notice? A pair of similar triangles?

### 5.15.2 — The Formula Selection Protocol

When you encounter a geometry problem, ask yourself:
1. **What shape(s) are involved?** (Triangle, circle, rectangle, etc.)
2. **What am I trying to find?** (Area, perimeter, length, angle measure, etc.)
3. **What information is given?** (Side lengths, angles, relationships, etc.)
4. **What formula connects the given information to the unknown?**

### 5.15.3 — The "Look for Right Triangles" Protocol

Right triangles are the workhorses of ACT geometry. Whenever you see:
- A tangent line meeting a radius
- A diagonal of a rectangle
- An altitude of a triangle
- A diagonal of a square or cube
- A height measurement

...look for a right triangle. The Pythagorean Theorem and trigonometric ratios are your most powerful tools.

### 5.15.4 — The "Look for Similar Triangles" Protocol

Similar triangles appear frequently in ACT geometry problems. Look for:
- Parallel lines cutting through a triangle
- Shadow problems (a person and their shadow, a building and its shadow)
- Overlapping triangles
- Triangles sharing an angle

When you find similar triangles, set up a proportion and solve for the unknown.

---

## 5.16 — Comprehensive Formula Reference for Geometry and Trigonometry

### Triangle Formulas
| Concept | Formula |
|---|---|
| Angle Sum | $\angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180°$ |
| Pythagorean Theorem | $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ |
| Area | $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$ |
| 45°-45°-90° Triangle | Legs = $s$, Hypotenuse = $s\sqrt{2}$ |
| 30°-60°-90° Triangle | Short leg = $s$, Long leg = $s\sqrt{3}$, Hypotenuse = $2s$ |

### Circle Formulas
| Concept | Formula |
|---|---|
| Area | $A = \pi r^2$ |
| Circumference | $C = 2\pi r = \pi d$ |
| Arc Length | $\frac{\theta}{360°} \times 2\pi r$ |
| Sector Area | $\frac{\theta}{360°} \times \pi r^2$ |
| Equation of a Circle | $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ |

### Quadrilateral Formulas
| Shape | Area | Perimeter |
|---|---|---|
| Rectangle | $A = lw$ | $P = 2l + 2w$ |
| Square | $A = s^2$ | $P = 4s$ |
| Parallelogram | $A = bh$ | $P = 2a + 2b$ |
| Trapezoid | $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$ | $P = a + b_1 + c + b_2$ |

### Solid Geometry Formulas
| Solid | Volume | Surface Area |
|---|---|---|
| Rectangular Prism | $V = lwh$ | $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$ |
| Cylinder | $V = \pi r^2 h$ | $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ |
| Sphere | $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ | $SA = 4\pi r^2$ |
| Cone | $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ | — |
| Pyramid | $V = \frac{1}{3}Bh$ | — |

### Trigonometric Ratios
| Ratio | Definition |
|---|---|
| $\sin(\theta)$ | $\frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ |
| $\cos(\theta)$ | $\frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ |
| $\tan(\theta)$ | $\frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$ |

### Coordinate Geometry Formulas
| Concept | Formula |
|---|---|
| Slope | $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$ |
| Distance | $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$ |
| Midpoint | $M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$ |

---

## 5.17 — Strategic Summary: The Geometry Decision Tree

When you encounter a geometry problem on the ACT, use this decision tree:

**1. What shape(s) are involved?**
- Triangle → Check for right triangles, special right triangles, similar triangles
- Circle → Check for tangent lines, inscribed angles, arc/sector formulas
- Quadrilateral → Identify the specific type and apply its properties
- Solid → Identify the shape and apply volume/surface area formulas

**2. What am I trying to find?**
- Length → Pythagorean Theorem, trigonometric ratios, similar triangles, distance formula
- Area → Area formula, decomposition into simpler shapes
- Angle measure → Angle sum, parallel lines, inscribed angles, trigonometry
- Volume → Volume formula

**3. What information is given?**
- Side lengths → Can I use the Pythagorean Theorem? Similar triangles?
- Angles → Can I use angle sum? Trigonometric ratios?
- Relationships → Are there parallel lines? Perpendicular lines? Tangent lines?

**4. Does the answer make sense?**
- Check units (linear units for lengths, square units for areas, cubic units for volumes)
- Check that the answer is reasonable (e.g., a length should be positive, an angle in a triangle should be between $0°$ and $180°$)
- Check that you answered the question actually asked (not a distractor)

---

## 5.18 — Connections to Other Chapters

Geometry and trigonometry are not isolated topics. They connect to virtually every other area tested on the ACT:

- **Chapter 3 (Algebra & Linear Functions):** Coordinate geometry problems (finding lengths, midpoints, equations of lines in the coordinate plane) are direct applications of this chapter. Many geometry problems reduce to solving linear equations.
- **Chapter 4 (Quadratics):** The equation of a circle $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ is a quadratic equation in two variables. Projectile motion problems connect to physics applications.
- **Chapter 5 (Functions):** Trigonometric functions (sine, cosine, tangent) are functions that can be graphed and transformed. The unit circle connects to function concepts.
- **Chapter 7 (Data Analysis):** Geometric probability problems combine geometry and probability concepts.
- **Chapter 8 (Roots, Exponents, and Radicals):** The Pythagorean Theorem involves square roots. Special right triangles involve $\sqrt{2}$ and $\sqrt{3}$.

Understanding these interconnections will deepen your comprehension and improve your ability to recognize the underlying structure of any problem the ACT presents.

## 6.1 — The Concept of a Function

Before diving into the mechanics of function notation, transformations, and modeling, it is essential to build an unshakable conceptual understanding of what a function actually *is*. Many students treat functions as mere algebraic expressions to be evaluated, but on the ACT, functions appear in a wide variety of guises—as tables, graphs, verbal descriptions, and nested compositions. A deep, intuitive grasp of the function concept separates students who can handle routine function questions from those who can tackle the most abstract and novel problems the test can generate.

### 6.1.1 — Definition of a Function

A **function** is a rule that assigns to each element of one set (called the **domain**) exactly one element of another set (called the **range**). In the context of the ACT, we almost always deal with functions whose domain and range are subsets of the real numbers.

The critical word in this definition is **exactly one**. A function cannot assign two different outputs to the same input. If an input $x$ is fed into a function $f$, there must be one and only one output $f(x)$. This defining characteristic distinguishes a function from a more general **relation**.

**Formal Definition:** A relation $f$ from set $D$ (the domain) to set $R$ (the range) is a function if and only if for every $x \in D$, there exists a unique $y \in R$ such that $(x, y) \in f$.

In simpler terms: every input gets one and only one output. No exceptions.

### 6.1.2 — The Vertical Line Test

Because the ACT frequently presents graphs and asks whether they represent functions, the **Vertical Line Test** is an indispensable tool.

**Vertical Line Test:** A graph in the coordinate plane represents a function if and only if no vertical line intersects the graph at more than one point.

If you can draw *any* vertical line that crosses the graph twice (or more), the graph does **not** represent a function. This follows directly from the definition: a vertical line at $x = a$ checks whether the input $a$ is assigned more than one output. If the line hits the graph at two points, say $(a, b)$ and $(a, c)$ with $b \neq c$, then the input $a$ has two different outputs, violating the definition.

**Common graphs that pass the vertical line test (are functions):**
*   Any non-vertical line
*   Parabolas opening upward or downward
*   Exponential curves
*   The upper half of a circle (but not the full circle)

**Common graphs that fail the vertical line test (are NOT functions):**
*   A full circle
*   A horizontal parabola (sideways, $x = y^2$)
*   An ellipse
*   Any graph that "doubles back" vertically

### 6.1.3 — Domain and Range

**Domain:** The set of all possible input values ($x$-values) for which the function is defined. In the context of the ACT, unless a domain is explicitly restricted by the problem, the domain is assumed to be all real numbers for which the expression produces a real output.

**Range:** The set of all possible output values ($y$-values, or $f(x)$-values) that the function actually produces.

**Restrictions on the Domain (Critical for the ACT):**

1.  **Denominators cannot be zero.** If a function involves a fraction, any $x$-value that makes the denominator zero must be excluded from the domain.
    *   Example: $f(x) = \dfrac{1}{x - 3}$ has domain $\{x \in \mathbb{R} \mid x \neq 3\}$.

2.  **Expressions under even roots must be non-negative.** If a function involves a square root (or any even root), the radicand (the expression inside the root) must be $\ge 0$.
    *   Example: $f(x) = \sqrt{x + 4}$ requires $x + 4 \ge 0$, so $x \ge -4$.

3.  **Logarithmic arguments must be positive.** (Rare on the ACT, but worth noting.) $\log_b(u)$ requires $u > 0$.

4.  **Contextual restrictions.** In word problems, the domain may be restricted by the real-world context. For example, if $x$ represents the number of people, $x$ must be a non-negative integer.

**Finding the Range:** The range is often harder to determine than the domain. For the ACT, the most common approach is to:
*   Identify the shape of the graph (parabola, line, etc.)
*   Find the vertex or turning point
*   Determine whether the graph extends to $+\infty$, $-\infty$, or both
*   Consider any maximum or minimum values

### 6.1.4 — Independent and Dependent Variables

In the function $f(x)$:
*   $x$ is the **independent variable** (the input, the variable you choose or control)
*   $f(x)$ is the **dependent variable** (the output, the variable that *depends* on your choice of $x$)

In modeling contexts, the independent variable is often time, and the dependent variable is the quantity being measured (population, temperature, revenue, etc.).

---

## 6.2 — Function Notation: The Language of Mathematics

Function notation is one of the most frequently tested concepts on the ACT, and it appears in virtually every section of the math test. Mastering this notation is non-negotiable.

### 6.2.1 — Reading and Interpreting $f(x)$

The notation $f(x)$ is read as **"$f$ of $x$"** and represents the output of the function $f$ when the input is $x$.

**Critical Distinction:** $f(x)$ does **not** mean $f$ multiplied by $x$. The notation $f(x)$ is a single, indivisible symbol representing the output value. Treating it as multiplication is one of the most common and costly errors students make.

**Example:** If $f(x) = 3x^2 - 2x + 7$, then:
*   $f(2) = 3(2)^2 - 2(2) + 7 = 12 - 4 + 7 = 15$
*   $f(-1) = 3(-1)^2 - 2(-1) + 7 = 3 + 2 + 7 = 12$
*   $f(a) = 3a^2 - 2a + 7$ (substitute the variable $a$ for every $x$)
*   $f(x + h) = 3(x + h)^2 - 2(x + h) + 7$ (substitute the expression $x + h$ for every $x$)

### 6.2.2 — Evaluating Functions: Step-by-Step Protocol

When the ACT asks you to evaluate $f(a)$ for a specific value $a$, follow this protocol:

1.  **Identify the function rule.** Write down the expression for $f(x)$.
2.  **Replace every instance of $x$** with the input value (or expression) enclosed in parentheses.
3.  **Simplify** using the order of operations (PEMDAS).
4.  **Check your work** by re-evaluating or estimating.

**Worked Example:** Let $f(x) = 2x^2 - 5x + 3$. Find $f(3)$.

*   Step 1: $f(x) = 2x^2 - 5x + 3$
*   Step 2: $f(3) = 2(3)^2 - 5(3) + 3$
*   Step 3: $f(3) = 2(9) - 15 + 3 = 18 - 15 + 3 = 6$
*   Step 4: Verify: $2(9) = 18$, $18 - 15 = 3$, $3 + 3 = 6$. ✓

### 6.2.3 — Evaluating Functions with Expressions as Inputs

The ACT frequently tests whether students can substitute an *expression* (not just a number) into a function. This is where many students falter.

**Worked Example:** Let $f(x) = x^2 - 4$. Find $f(x + 2)$.

*   $f(x + 2) = (x + 2)^2 - 4$
*   $= x^2 + 4x + 4 - 4$
*   $= x^2 + 4x$

**Common Error:** Writing $f(x + 2) = x^2 + 2^2 - 4 = x^2 + 4 - 4 = x^2$. This is **wrong** because $(x + 2)^2 \neq x^2 + 2^2$. The square must be expanded properly: $(x + 2)^2 = x^2 + 4x + 4$.

### 6.2.4 — Functions Defined by Tables

The ACT frequently presents functions as tables rather than algebraic expressions. When a function is defined by a table, you simply look up the input value and read off the corresponding output.

**Example:**

| $x$ | $f(x)$ |
|---|---|
| 1 | 7 |
| 2 | 10 |
| 3 | 13 |
| 4 | 16 |

*   $f(2) = 10$ (read directly from the table)
*   If $f(x) = 13$, then $x = 3$ (reverse lookup)

**Key Insight:** A table defines a function if and only if no $x$-value appears more than once with different $f(x)$ values. If the same $x$ appears twice with different outputs, the table does not represent a function.

### 6.2.5 — Functions Defined by Graphs

When a function is presented graphically:
*   $f(a)$ is the $y$-coordinate of the point on the graph with $x$-coordinate $a$.
*   To solve $f(x) = b$, find the point(s) on the graph with $y$-coordinate $b$, and read off the $x$-coordinate(s).

**Example:** If the graph of $f$ passes through the point $(3, -5)$, then $f(3) = -5$.

### 6.2.6 — Piecewise-Defined Functions

A **piecewise function** is a function defined by different rules on different intervals of its domain. The ACT tests these regularly.

**Example:**
$$f(x) = \begin{cases} x + 1 & \text{if } x < 0 \\ x^2 & \text{if } x \ge 0 \end{cases}$$

To evaluate $f(-3)$: Since $-3 < 0$, use the first rule: $f(-3) = -3 + 1 = -2$.

To evaluate $f(2)$: Since $2 \ge 0$, use the second rule: $f(2) = 2^2 = 4$.

To evaluate $f(0)$: Since $0 \ge 0$, use the second rule: $f(0) = 0^2 = 0$.

**Critical Detail:** Pay close attention to the inequality symbols ($<$, $\le$, $>$, $\ge$). The value $x = 0$ falls into the second piece because the condition is $x \ge 0$, not $x > 0$. A common ACT trap involves testing boundary values where the rule changes.

### 6.2.7 — Nested Function Evaluation

Sometimes the ACT asks you to evaluate a function at the *output* of another expression, or to work with multiple functions simultaneously.

**Example:** Let $f(x) = 2x + 1$ and $g(x) = x^2 - 3$. Find $f(g(2))$.

*   First, evaluate the inner function: $g(2) = 2^2 - 3 = 4 - 3 = 1$.
*   Then, evaluate the outer function at that result: $f(1) = 2(1) + 1 = 3$.
*   Therefore, $f(g(2)) = 3$.

This is **function composition**, which we will explore in depth in Section 6.5.

---

## 6.3 — Parent Functions: The Building Blocks of All Functions

Every function on the ACT can be understood as a transformation of a small set of **parent functions** (also called **toolkit functions**). These are the simplest form of each function type, before any shifts, stretches, reflections, or compressions are applied. Knowing these parent functions cold—their shapes, key features, domains, and ranges—is one of the highest-yield investments you can make in your ACT preparation.

### 6.3.1 — The Linear Function: $f(x) = x$

**Graph:** A straight line through the origin $(0, 0)$ with slope $1$.

**Key Features:**
*   Passes through $(0, 0)$ and $(1, 1)$
*   Domain: $(-\infty, \infty)$ (all real numbers)
*   Range: $(-\infty, \infty)$ (all real numbers)
*   Constant rate of change (slope = 1)
*   Odd function: $f(-x) = -f(x)$ (symmetric about the origin)

**ACT Relevance:** All linear functions $f(x) = mx + b$ are transformations of this parent function. The slope $m$ controls steepness and direction; the $y$-intercept $b$ controls vertical shift.

### 6.3.2 — The Quadratic Function: $f(x) = x^2$

**Graph:** A **parabola** opening upward with vertex at the origin $(0, 0)$.

**Key Features:**
*   Vertex at $(0, 0)$
*   Axis of symmetry: $x = 0$ (the $y$-axis)
*   Domain: $(-\infty, \infty)$
*   Range: $[0, \infty)$ (all non-negative real numbers)
*   Even function: $f(-x) = f(x)$ (symmetric about the $y$-axis)
*   Decreasing on $(-\infty, 0)$, increasing on $(0, \infty)$

**Key Points:** $(-2, 4)$, $(-1, 1)$, $(0, 0)$, $(1, 1)$, $(2, 4)$

**ACT Relevance:** The quadratic function is the single most tested function type on the ACT. Every parabola $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$ or $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$ is a transformation of this parent function. The coefficient $a$ controls the direction (up if $a > 0$, down if $a < 0$) and the "width" of the parabola.

### 6.3.3 — The Cubic Function: $f(x) = x^3$

**Graph:** An S-shaped curve passing through the origin, rising from left to right.

**Key Features:**
*   Passes through $(0, 0)$, $(1, 1)$, $(-1, -1)$
*   Domain: $(-\infty, \infty)$
*   Range: $(-\infty, \infty)$
*   Odd function: $f(-x) = -f(x)$ (symmetric about the origin)
*   Always increasing (no local maxima or minima)

**Key Points:** $(-2, -8)$, $(-1, -1)$, $(0, 0)$, $(1, 1)$, $(2, 8)$

**ACT Relevance:** Cubic functions appear less frequently than quadratics but are tested, especially in questions about end behavior, symmetry, and the number of real roots.

### 6.3.4 — The Square Root Function: $f(x) = \sqrt{x}$

**Graph:** The upper half of a sideways parabola, starting at the origin and curving gradually upward to the right.

**Key Features:**
*   Starts at $(0, 0)$
*   Domain: $[0, \infty)$ (cannot take the square root of a negative number in the real number system)
*   Range: $[0, \infty)$
*   Increasing on its entire domain
*   Neither even nor odd (domain is not symmetric about 0)

**Key Points:** $(0, 0)$, $(1, 1)$, $(4, 2)$, $(9, 3)$

**ACT Relevance:** The domain restriction is a favorite ACT trap. Questions often ask for the domain of a square root function, and students who forget the restriction lose points.

### 6.3.5 — The Cube Root Function: $f(x) = \sqrt[3]{x}$

**Graph:** Similar in shape to the cubic function but flatter near the origin and steeper far from the origin.

**Key Features:**
*   Passes through $(0, 0)$, $(1, 1)$, $(-1, -1)$
*   Domain: $(-\infty, \infty)$ (unlike the square root, cube roots are defined for negative numbers)
*   Range: $(-\infty, \infty)$
*   Odd function: $f(-x) = -f(x)$
*   Always increasing

**Key Points:** $(-8, -2)$, $(-1, -1)$, $(0, 0)$, $(1, 1)$, $(8, 2)$

**ACT Relevance:** The contrast between square root and cube root domains is a common point of confusion that the ACT exploits.

### 6.3.6 — The Absolute Value Function: $f(x) = |x|$

**Graph:** A V-shaped graph with the vertex at the origin, opening upward.

**Key Features:**
*   Vertex at $(0, 0)$
*   Domain: $(-\infty, \infty)$
*   Range: $[0, \infty)$
*   Even function: $f(-x) = f(x)$ (symmetric about the $y$-axis)
*   Decreasing on $(-\infty, 0)$, increasing on $(0, \infty)$
*   The two "arms" of the V have slopes of $-1$ and $+1$

**Key Points:** $(-2, 2)$, $(-1, 1)$, $(0, 0)$, $(1, 1)$, $(2, 2)$

**ACT Relevance:** Absolute value functions appear in questions about distance, inequalities, and piecewise definitions. The V-shape is instantly recognizable and should be memorized.

### 6.3.7 — The Reciprocal (Rational) Function: $f(x) = \dfrac{1}{x}$

**Graph:** A **hyperbola** with two branches, one in Quadrant I and one in Quadrant III.

**Key Features:**
*   **Vertical asymptote** at $x = 0$ (the $y$-axis): the function is undefined at $x = 0$
*   **Horizontal asymptote** at $y = 0$ (the $x$-axis): as $x \to \pm\infty$, $f(x) \to 0$
*   Domain: $(-\infty, 0) \cup (0, \infty)$ (all real numbers except 0)
*   Range: $(-\infty, 0) \cup (0, \infty)$ (all real numbers except 0)
*   Odd function: $f(-x) = -f(x)$
*   Decreasing on $(-\infty, 0)$ and decreasing on $(0, \infty)$ (but not decreasing on the combined domain—this is a subtle point)

**Key Points:** $(-1, -1)$, $(-2, -1/2)$, $(1, 1)$, $(2, 1/2)$

**ACT Relevance:** Asymptotes are a key concept. The ACT may ask about the behavior of the function near the asymptotes or about the domain and range.

### 6.3.8 — The Exponential Function: $f(x) = 2^x$ (or $f(x) = e^x$)

**Graph:** A curve that starts near the $x$-axis on the left and rises steeply to the right.

**Key Features:**
*   Passes through $(0, 1)$ because any non-zero number to the power 0 equals 1
*   **Horizontal asymptote** at $y = 0$ (the $x$-axis): as $x \to -\infty$, $f(x) \to 0$
*   Domain: $(-\infty, \infty)$
*   Range: $(0, \infty)$ (always positive)
*   Always increasing (for base $> 1$)

**Key Points:** $(-2, 1/4)$, $(-1, 1/2)$, $(0, 1)$, $(1, 2)$, $(2, 4)$

**ACT Relevance:** Exponential growth and decay are heavily tested in modeling contexts (population growth, radioactive decay, compound interest). The horizontal asymptote and the fact that $f(0) = 1$ are key features.

### 6.3.9 — The Logarithmic Function: $f(x) = \log_2(x)$ (or $f(x) = \ln(x)$)

**Graph:** The inverse of the exponential function—a curve that rises slowly to the right and approaches the $y$-axis asymptotically.

**Key Features:**
*   Passes through $(1, 0)$ because $\log_b(1) = 0$ for any base $b$
*   **Vertical asymptote** at $x = 0$ (the $y$-axis)
*   Domain: $(0, \infty)$ (logarithms are only defined for positive numbers)
*   Range: $(-\infty, \infty)$
*   Always increasing (for base $> 1$)

**Key Points:** $(1/4, -2)$, $(1/2, -1)$, $(1, 0)$, $(2, 1)$, $(4, 2)$

**ACT Relevance:** Logarithmic functions appear less frequently on the ACT than exponentials, but they do appear, especially in questions about inverse functions and domain restrictions.

---

## 6.4 — Transformations of Functions: The Complete Framework

Transformations are one of the most heavily tested topics on the ACT Math section. The test presents a parent function and asks you to identify the transformed graph, or vice versa. Understanding the transformation framework deeply will allow you to answer these questions with speed and confidence.

### 6.4.1 — The General Transformation Model

Any transformed function can be written in the form:

$$g(x) = a \cdot f(b(x - h)) + k$$

where $f(x)$ is the parent function and the parameters $a$, $b$, $h$, and $k$ control the transformations:

| Parameter | Type of Transformation | Effect |
|---|---|---|
| $a$ | Vertical stretch/compression and reflection | Multiplies all $y$-values by $a$ |
| $b$ | Horizontal stretch/compression and reflection | Divides all $x$-values by $b$ |
| $h$ | Horizontal shift (translation) | Shifts the graph $h$ units right (if $h > 0$) or left (if $h < 0$) |
| $k$ | Vertical shift (translation) | Shifts the graph $k$ units up (if $k > 0$) or down (if $k < 0$) |

**Critical Order of Operations for Transformations:** When applying multiple transformations, the order matters. The standard order is:
1.  Horizontal shift ($h$)
2.  Horizontal stretch/compression and reflection ($b$)
3.  Vertical stretch/compression and reflection ($a$)
4.  Vertical shift ($k$)

However, when the function is written as $a \cdot f(b(x - h)) + k$, the transformations are applied in the order determined by the algebraic structure: first the horizontal transformations (inside the function's argument), then the vertical transformations (outside the function).

### 6.4.2 — Vertical Shifts: $f(x) + k$

Adding a constant $k$ to the *output* of a function shifts the entire graph **vertically**.

*   $f(x) + k$ with $k > 0$: shifts the graph **up** by $k$ units
*   $f(x) + k$ with $k < 0$: shifts the graph **down** by $|k|$ units

**Example:** $g(x) = x^2 + 3$ is the parent quadratic shifted **up 3 units**. The vertex moves from $(0, 0)$ to $(0, 3)$.

**Example:** $g(x) = \sqrt{x} - 2$ is the parent square root function shifted **down 2 units**. The starting point moves from $(0, 0)$ to $(0, -2)$.

**Key Insight:** Vertical shifts affect the **range** of the function but not the domain. If $f(x) = x^2$ has range $[0, \infty)$, then $g(x) = x^2 + 3$ has range $[3, \infty)$.

### 6.4.3 — Horizontal Shifts: $f(x - h)$

Replacing $x$ with $(x - h)$ in the function's argument shifts the graph **horizontally**.

*   $f(x - h)$ with $h > 0$: shifts the graph **right** by $h$ units
*   $f(x - h)$ with $h < 0$: shifts the graph **left** by $|h|$ units

**⚠️ THE SIGN REVERSAL TRAP:** This is one of the most common sources of error on the ACT. The direction of the horizontal shift is **opposite** to the sign inside the parentheses. $f(x - 3)$ shifts **right** (not left), and $f(x + 2) = f(x - (-2))$ shifts **left** (not right).

**Example:** $g(x) = (x - 4)^2$ is the parent quadratic shifted **right 4 units**. The vertex moves from $(0, 0)$ to $(4, 0)$.

**Example:** $g(x) = |x + 5|$ is the parent absolute value function shifted **left 5 units**. The vertex moves from $(0, 0)$ to $(-5, 0)$.

**Key Insight:** Horizontal shifts affect the **domain** of the function (when domain restrictions exist) but not the range. If $f(x) = \sqrt{x}$ has domain $[0, \infty)$, then $g(x) = \sqrt{x - 3}$ has domain $[3, \infty)$.

### 6.4.4 — Vertical Stretches and Compressions: $a \cdot f(x)$

Multiplying the *output* of a function by a constant $a$ stretches or compresses the graph **vertically**.

*   If $|a| > 1$: **vertical stretch** by a factor of $a$ (the graph becomes "taller" or "steeper")
*   If $0 < |a| < 1$: **vertical compression** by a factor of $a$ (the graph becomes "shorter" or "flatter")
*   If $a < 0$: the graph is also **reflected across the $x$-axis**

**Example:** $g(x) = 3x^2$ is the parent quadratic stretched vertically by a factor of 3. The point $(1, 1)$ on the parent becomes $(1, 3)$.

**Example:** $g(x) = \frac{1}{2}\sqrt{x}$ is the parent square root compressed vertically by a factor of $1/2$. The point $(4, 2)$ on the parent becomes $(4, 1)$.

### 6.4.5 — Horizontal Stretches and Compressions: $f(bx)$

Multiplying the *input* $x$ by a constant $b$ inside the function's argument stretches or compresses the graph **horizontally**.

*   If $|b| > 1$: **horizontal compression** by a factor of $1/b$ (the graph becomes "narrower")
*   If $0 < |b| < 1$: **horizontal stretch** by a factor of $1/b$ (the graph becomes "wider")
*   If $b < 0$: the graph is also **reflected across the $y$-axis**

**⚠️ THE RECIPROCAL TRAP:** The factor for horizontal stretches/compressions is the **reciprocal** of what you might intuitively expect. $f(2x)$ compresses horizontally by a factor of $1/2$ (not 2), and $f(x/2) = f(\frac{1}{2}x)$ stretches horizontally by a factor of 2.

**Example:** $g(x) = (2x)^2 = 4x^2$ is the parent quadratic compressed horizontally by a factor of $1/2$. The point $(1, 1)$ on the parent moves to $(1/2, 1)$.

**Example:** $g(x) = (\frac{1}{3}x)^2 = \frac{1}{9}x^2$ is the parent quadratic stretched horizontally by a factor of 3. The point $(1, 1)$ on the parent moves to $(3, 1)$.

### 6.4.6 — Reflections

Reflections flip the graph across an axis.

**Reflection across the $x$-axis:** $-f(x)$
*   Every $y$-value changes sign.
*   The graph is flipped upside down.
*   Example: $g(x) = -x^2$ is a parabola opening **downward** (vertex still at origin).

**Reflection across the $y$-axis:** $f(-x)$
*   Every $x$-value changes sign.
*   The graph is flipped left-to-right.
*   Example: $g(x) = (-x)^2 = x^2$—for the quadratic, this produces the same graph because the quadratic is an **even function**. But for $g(x) = 2^{-x}$, this is a reflection of $f(x) = 2^x$ across the $y$-axis, producing a decreasing exponential.

**Key Insight:** To determine if a reflection across the $y$-axis changes the graph, check if the function is even. If $f(-x) = f(x)$, the graph is unchanged by a $y$-axis reflection.

### 6.4.7 — Combining Multiple Transformations

The ACT frequently presents functions that combine several transformations. The key is to identify each transformation systematically.

**Worked Example:** Describe the transformations applied to $f(x) = x^2$ to obtain $g(x) = -2(x - 3)^2 + 5$.

1.  **$h = 3$:** Horizontal shift **right 3 units**
2.  **$a = -2$:** Vertical **stretch** by a factor of 2, and **reflection across the $x$-axis** (because $a < 0$)
3.  **$k = 5$:** Vertical shift **up 5 units**

The vertex moves from $(0, 0)$ to $(3, 5)$. The parabola opens downward and is narrower than the parent.

**Worked Example:** Describe the transformations applied to $f(x) = \sqrt{x}$ to obtain $g(x) = \sqrt{-x + 4} - 1$.

First, rewrite to match the standard form: $g(x) = \sqrt{-(x - 4)} - 1$.

1.  **$h = 4$:** Horizontal shift **right 4 units**
2.  **$b = -1$:** **Reflection across the $y$-axis**
3.  **$k = -1$:** Vertical shift **down 1 unit**

The starting point moves from $(0, 0)$ to $(4, -1)$, and the graph opens to the **left** instead of to the right.

### 6.4.8 — Transformations and Key Points

A powerful strategy for transformation questions is to track what happens to **specific key points** on the parent function.

For $g(x) = a \cdot f(b(x - h)) + k$:
*   A point $(x_0, y_0)$ on the graph of $f$ moves to $\left(\frac{x_0}{b} + h, \quad a \cdot y_0 + k\right)$ on the graph of $g$.

**Example:** If $(2, 4)$ is on the graph of $f(x) = x^2$, where does this point move under $g(x) = 3(x - 1)^2 + 2$?

*   $a = 3$, $b = 1$, $h = 1$, $k = 2$
*   New $x$-coordinate: $\frac{2}{1} + 1 = 3$
*   New $y$-coordinate: $3(4) + 2 = 14$
