*   The point $(2, 4)$ moves to $(3, 14)$.

---

## 6.5 — Function Composition

### 6.5.1 — Definition of Composition

The **composition** of two functions $f$ and $g$, denoted $(f \circ g)(x)$, is defined as:

$$(f \circ g)(x) = f(g(x))$$

This means: first apply $g$ to the input $x$, then apply $f$ to the result.

**Critical Distinction:** $(f \circ g)(x)$ is generally **not** the same as $(g \circ f)(x)$. Function composition is **not commutative**.

### 6.5.2 — Evaluating Compositions

**Example:** Let $f(x) = x^2 + 1$ and $g(x) = 3x - 2$.

Find $(f \circ g)(x)$:
$$f(g(x)) = f(3x - 2) = (3x - 2)^2 + 1 = 9x^2 - 12x + 4 + 1 = 9x^2 - 12x + 5$$

Find $(g \circ f)(x)$:
$$g(f(x)) = g(x^2 + 1) = 3(x^2 + 1) - 2 = 3x^2 + 3 - 2 = 3x^2 + 1$$

Note that $9x^2 - 12x + 5 \neq 3x^2 + 1$, confirming that composition is not commutative.

### 6.5.3 — Compositions with Specific Values

The ACT often asks for the value of a composition at a specific input.

**Example:** Let $f(x) = 2x - 3$ and $g(x) = x^2$. Find $(g \circ f)(2)$.

*   Method 1 (evaluate inner first): $f(2) = 2(2) - 3 = 1$, then $g(1) = 1^2 = 1$.
*   Method 2 (find the composition first): $(g \circ f)(x) = g(2x - 3) = (2x - 3)^2$, then $(g \circ f)(2) = (2(2) - 3)^2 = 1^2 = 1$.

Both methods yield the same result. Method 1 is usually faster for a single value.

---

## 6.6 — Inverse Functions

### 6.6.1 — Definition of an Inverse Function

The **inverse function** of $f$, denoted $f^{-1}(x)$, "undoes" what $f$ does. Formally:

$$f^{-1}(f(x)) = x \quad \text{and} \quad f(f^{-1}(x)) = x$$

**Important:** The notation $f^{-1}(x)$ does **not** mean $\frac{1}{f(x)}$. The superscript $-1$ denotes the inverse function, not a reciprocal.

### 6.6.2 — The Horizontal Line Test

A function has an inverse that is also a function if and only if it passes the **Horizontal Line Test**: no horizontal line intersects the graph more than once. Such functions are called **one-to-one** functions.

*   $f(x) = x^2$ does **not** pass the horizontal line test (a horizontal line at $y = 4$ hits the graph at both $x = 2$ and $x = -2$), so its inverse is not a function unless the domain is restricted.
*   $f(x) = x^3$ **does** pass the horizontal line test, so it has an inverse function.

### 6.6.3 — Finding the Inverse Function Algebraically

To find $f^{-1}(x)$:
1.  Replace $f(x)$ with $y$.
2.  Swap $x$ and $y$.
3.  Solve for $y$.
4.  Replace $y$ with $f^{-1}(x)$.

**Example:** Find the inverse of $f(x) = 3x - 7$.

1.  $y = 3x - 7$
2.  $x = 3y - 7$
3.  $x + 7 = 3y \implies y = \frac{x + 7}{3}$
4.  $f^{-1}(x) = \frac{x + 7}{3}$

**Verification:** $f(f^{-1}(x)) = 3\left(\frac{x + 7}{3}\right) - 7 = x + 7 - 7 = x$ ✓

### 6.6.4 — Graphical Relationship Between a Function and Its Inverse

The graph of $f^{-1}(x)$ is the **reflection of the graph of $f(x)$ across the line $y = x$**. This is because swapping $x$ and $y$ (which is how we find the inverse) is geometrically equivalent to reflecting across the line $y = x$.

**Key Insight:** If $(a, b)$ is on the graph of $f$, then $(b, a)$ is on the graph of $f^{-1}$.

---

## 6.7 — The Coordinate Plane: Quadrants and Their Properties

### 6.7.1 — The Four Quadrants

The coordinate plane is divided into four **quadrants** by the $x$-axis and $y$-axis. The quadrants are numbered using Roman numerals, starting from the upper right and proceeding counterclockwise.

| Quadrant | $x$-coordinate | $y$-coordinate | Sign Pattern |
|---|---|---|---|
| **I** | Positive | Positive | $(+, +)$ |
| **II** | Negative | Positive | $(-, +)$ |
| **III** | Negative | Negative | $(-, -)$ |
| **IV** | Positive | Negative | $(+, -)$ |

**Mnemonic:** "All Students Take Calculus"
*   **A**ll (Quadrant I): all coordinates positive
*   **S**ine (Quadrant II): sine is positive (and $y$ is positive)
*   **T**angent (Quadrant III): tangent is positive (negative/negative = positive)
*   **C**osine (Quadrant IV): cosine is positive (and $x$ is positive)

This mnemonic is more commonly used in trigonometry, but it reinforces the sign patterns of the quadrants.

### 6.7.2 — Points on the Axes

Points that lie on an axis do **not** belong to any quadrant:
*   Points on the positive $x$-axis: $(a, 0)$ where $a > 0$
*   Points on the negative $x$-axis: $(a, 0)$ where $a < 0$
*   Points on the positive $y$-axis: $(0, b)$ where $b > 0$
*   Points on the negative $y$-axis: $(0, b)$ where $b < 0$
*   The origin: $(0, 0)$

### 6.7.3 — Quadrant Signatures of Common Functions

Understanding which quadrants a function's graph passes through is a common ACT question type.

*   **$f(x) = x$ (linear, positive slope):** Passes through Quadrants I and III
*   **$f(x) = -x$ (linear, negative slope):** Passes through Quadrants II and IV
*   **$f(x) = x^2$ (quadratic):** Passes through Quadrants I and II (always non-negative $y$)
*   **$f(x) = -x^2$ (inverted quadratic):** Passes through Quadrants III and IV
*   **$f(x) = x^3$ (cubic):** Passes through Quadrants I and III
*   **$f(x) = \sqrt{x}$:** Exists only in Quadrant I (and on the positive $x$-axis)
*   **$f(x) = \dfrac{1}{x}$:** Passes through Quadrants I and III
*   **$f(x) = 2^x$:** Exists only in Quadrants I and II (always positive $y$)
*   **$f(x) = \log_2(x)$:** Exists only in Quadrants I and IV (positive $x$ only)

### 6.7.4 — Reflections Across Axes and Quadrants

**Reflection across the $x$-axis:** $(x, y) \to (x, -y)$
*   Quadrant I $\leftrightarrow$ Quadrant IV
*   Quadrant II $\leftrightarrow$ Quadrant III

**Reflection across the $y$-axis:** $(x, y) \to (-x, y)$
*   Quadrant I $\leftrightarrow$ Quadrant II
*   Quadrant III $\leftrightarrow$ Quadrant IV

**Reflection across the origin:** $(x, y) \to (-x, -y)$
*   Quadrant I $\leftrightarrow$ Quadrant III
*   Quadrant II $\leftrightarrow$ Quadrant IV

**Reflection across the line $y = x$:** $(x, y) \to (y, x)$
*   This is the graphical manifestation of finding an inverse function.
*   Quadrant I stays in Quadrant I (but points swap coordinates)
*   Quadrant II $\leftrightarrow$ Quadrant IV

### 6.7.5 — Symmetry and Quadrants

**Even Functions** ($f(-x) = f(x)$): Symmetric about the $y$-axis. If a point $(a, b)$ is on the graph, so is $(-a, b)$. The graph has mirror symmetry across the $y$-axis, meaning the portions in Quadrants I and II mirror each other, and the portions in Quadrants III and IV mirror each other.

**Odd Functions** ($f(-x) = -f(x)$): Symmetric about the origin. If a point $(a, b)$ is on the graph, so is $(-a, -b)$. The graph has rotational symmetry of 180° about the origin, meaning the portion in Quadrant I rotates to Quadrant III, and the portion in Quadrant II rotates to Quadrant IV.

---

## 6.8 — Modeling with Functions

### 6.8.1 — What Is Mathematical Modeling?

**Mathematical modeling** is the process of representing real-world situations using mathematical structures—equations, functions, graphs, tables, and inequalities. On the ACT, modeling questions ask you to:
1.  Translate a word problem into a function or equation
2.  Interpret the meaning of function components (slope, intercept, vertex) in context
3.  Use a given model to make predictions
4.  Determine the reasonable domain and range of a model in context

### 6.8.2 — Linear Models

A linear model has the form $y = mx + b$ (or $f(x) = mx + b$).

*   **$m$ (slope):** The rate of change. In context, this is "how much $y$ changes for each 1-unit increase in $x$." Units: (units of $y$) per (units of $x$).
*   **$b$ ($y$-intercept):** The starting value. In context, this is "the value of $y$ when $x = 0$." Units: same as $y$.

**Example:** A plumber charges a \$50 flat fee plus \$30 per hour. The total cost $C$ as a function of hours $h$ is:
$$C(h) = 30h + 50$$
*   Slope (30): The plumber charges \$30 per hour.
*   $y$-intercept (50): The flat fee is \$50 (the cost when $h = 0$).

### 6.8.3 — Exponential Models

An exponential model has the form $y = a \cdot b^x$ or $y = a \cdot e^{kx}$.

*   **$a$:** The initial value (when $x = 0$)
*   **$b$:** The growth factor (if $b > 1$) or decay factor (if $0 < b < 1$)
*   The quantity grows or decays by a **constant percentage** per unit of $x$.

**Example:** A population of 500 bacteria doubles every hour. The population $P$ after $t$ hours is:
$$P(t) = 500 \cdot 2^t$$
*   Initial value (500): The starting population.
*   Growth factor (2): The population multiplies by 2 each hour (100% growth per hour).

**Exponential Growth vs. Decay:**
*   Growth: $b > 1$ (or equivalently, $r > 0$ where $b = 1 + r$)
*   Decay: $0 < b < 1$ (or equivalently, $r < 0$ where $b = 1 + r$)

**Example of Decay:** A car worth \$20,000 depreciates 15% per year. The value $V$ after $t$ years is:
$$V(t) = 20000 \cdot (0.85)^t$$
*   The decay factor is $0.85 = 1 - 0.15$ (retaining 85% of value each year).

### 6.8.4 — Quadratic Models

A quadratic model has the form $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ or $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$.

*   The **vertex** represents the maximum or minimum value of the model.
*   If $a > 0$: the parabola opens upward; the vertex is a **minimum**.
*   If $a < 0$: the parabola opens downward; the vertex is a **maximum**.

**Example:** A ball is thrown upward from a height of 6 feet with an initial velocity of 40 feet per second. The height $h$ after $t$ seconds is:
$$h(t) = -16t^2 + 40t + 6$$
*   The $-16t^2$ term represents the effect of gravity.
*   The $40t$ term represents the initial upward velocity.
*   The constant 6 represents the initial height.
*   The vertex gives the maximum height and the time at which it occurs.

### 6.8.5 — Choosing the Right Model

| Situation | Model Type | Key Feature |
|---|---|---|
| Constant rate of change | Linear | Constant slope |
| Constant percentage change | Exponential | Constant growth/decay factor |
| Maximum or minimum value | Quadratic | Vertex gives max/min |
| Quantity proportional to reciprocal | Rational ($y = k/x$) | Inverse relationship |
| Quantity proportional to square root | Radical ($y = k\sqrt{x}$) | Diminishing returns |

### 6.8.6 — Interpreting Graphs of Models

When the ACT presents a graph of a model, you should be able to identify:
*   The **type** of function (linear, quadratic, exponential, etc.)
*   The **$y$-intercept** and its meaning in context
*   The **slope** (for linear) or **rate of change** (for other types)
*   Any **maximum or minimum** values and where they occur
*   The **intervals** where the function is increasing or decreasing
*   The **intervals** where the function is concave up or concave down

---

## 6.9 — Intervals of Increase and Decrease

### 6.9.1 — Definitions

A function $f$ is **increasing** on an interval if, as $x$ increases, $f(x)$ also increases. Formally: for any $x_1 < x_2$ in the interval, $f(x_1) < f(x_2)$.

A function $f$ is **decreasing** on an interval if, as $x$ increases, $f(x)$ decreases. Formally: for any $x_1 < x_2$ in the interval, $f(x_1) > f(x_2)$.

A function is **constant** on an interval if $f(x)$ does not change as $x$ increases.

### 6.9.2 — Identifying Intervals from Graphs

To determine where a function is increasing or decreasing from its graph:
*   Trace the graph from left to right.
*   If the graph goes **uphill** (rising), the function is increasing.
*   If the graph goes **downhill** (falling), the function is decreasing.
*   If the graph is **flat** (horizontal), the function is constant.

**Example:** For $f(x) = x^2$:
*   Decreasing on $(-\infty, 0)$
*   Increasing on $(0, \infty)$
*   The function changes from decreasing to increasing at $x = 0$ (the vertex).

### 6.9.3 — Local and Global Extrema

A **local maximum** (or relative maximum) is a point where the function changes from increasing to decreasing. It is the highest point in its immediate neighborhood.

A **local minimum** (or relative minimum) is a point where the function changes from decreasing to increasing. It is the lowest point in its immediate neighborhood.

A **global maximum** (or absolute maximum) is the highest point on the entire graph (over the domain of interest).

A **global minimum** (or absolute minimum) is the lowest point on the entire graph.

**For the ACT:** The most common extrema questions involve quadratic functions, where the vertex is the global maximum (if $a < 0$) or global minimum (if $a > 0$).

---

## 6.10 — End Behavior

### 6.10.1 — Definition

**End behavior** describes what happens to $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches positive infinity ($x \to +\infty$) and negative infinity ($x \to -\infty$).

### 6.10.2 — End Behavior of Common Parent Functions

| Function | As $x \to +\infty$ | As $x \to -\infty$ |
|---|---|---|
| $f(x) = x$ (positive slope) | $f(x) \to +\infty$ | $f(x) \to -\infty$ |
| $f(x) = x^2$ | $f(x) \to +\infty$ | $f(x) \to +\infty$ |
| $f(x) = x^3$ | $f(x) \to +\infty$ | $f(x) \to -\infty$ |
| $f(x) = \sqrt{x}$ | $f(x) \to +\infty$ | Not defined for $x < 0$ |
| $f(x) = \dfrac{1}{x}$ | $f(x) \to 0^+$ | $f(x) \to 0^-$ |
| $f(x) = 2^x$ | $f(x) \to +\infty$ | $f(x) \to 0^+$ |
| $f(x) = \log_2(x)$ | $f(x) \to +\infty$ | Not defined for $x \le 0$ |

### 6.10.3 — End Behavior of Polynomial Functions

For polynomial functions, the end behavior is determined entirely by the **leading term** (the term with the highest power of $x$).

*   If the degree is **even** and the leading coefficient is **positive**: both ends go up ($f(x) \to +\infty$ as $x \to \pm\infty$).
*   If the degree is **even** and the leading coefficient is **negative**: both ends go down ($f(x) \to -\infty$ as $x \to \pm\infty$).
*   If the degree is **odd** and the leading coefficient is **positive**: the right end goes up, the left end goes down.
*   If the degree is **odd** and the leading coefficient is **negative**: the right end goes down, the left end goes up.

**Mnemonic:** "Even = same direction on both ends. Odd = opposite directions."

---

## 6.11 — Zeros of a Function (Roots/x-Intercepts)

### 6.11.1 — Definition

A **zero** of a function $f$ is any value $x = a$ such that $f(a) = 0$. Graphically, the zeros are the $x$-intercepts of the graph—the points where the graph crosses or touches the $x$-axis.

### 6.11.2 — Finding Zeros Algebraically

To find the zeros of a function, set $f(x) = 0$ and solve for $x$.

**Example:** Find the zeros of $f(x) = x^2 - 5x + 6$.
$$x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$$
$$(x - 2)(x - 3) = 0$$
$$x = 2 \quad \text{or} \quad x = 3$$

The zeros are $x = 2$ and $x = 3$. The graph crosses the $x$-axis at $(2, 0)$ and $(3, 0)$.

### 6.11.3 — Multiplicity of Zeros

The **multiplicity** of a zero is the number of times the corresponding factor appears in the factored form of the function.

*   **Odd multiplicity:** The graph **crosses** the $x$-axis at the zero.
*   **Even multiplicity:** The graph **touches** the $x$-axis at the zero and turns around (bounces off).

**Example:** $f(x) = (x - 1)^2(x + 3)$
*   Zero at $x = 1$ with multiplicity 2 (even): the graph **touches** the $x$-axis at $(1, 0)$.
*   Zero at $x = -3$ with multiplicity 1 (odd): the graph **crosses** the $x$-axis at $(-3, 0)$.

### 6.11.4 — Number of Zeros

A polynomial of degree $n$ has exactly $n$ zeros in the complex number system (counting multiplicities), according to the **Fundamental Theorem of Algebra**. However, on the ACT, we are typically concerned with **real** zeros only.

*   A quadratic function can have 0, 1, or 2 real zeros (determined by the discriminant).
*   A cubic function has at least 1 real zero (and at most 3).

---

## 6.12 — Asymptotes: Understanding Unreachable Boundaries

### 6.12.1 — Vertical Asymptotes

A **vertical asymptote** is a vertical line $x = a$ that the graph approaches but never touches or crosses. The function's values grow without bound (positively or negatively) as $x$ approaches $a$.

**How to find vertical asymptotes:** Set the denominator of a rational function equal to zero and solve for $x$. Each solution is a candidate for a vertical asymptote (provided the numerator is not also zero at that value—if both are zero, there may be a **hole** instead).

**Example:** $f(x) = \dfrac{1}{x - 3}$ has a vertical asymptote at $x = 3$.

### 6.12.2 — Horizontal Asymptotes

A **horizontal asymptote** is a horizontal line $y = b$ that the graph approaches as $x \to +\infty$ or $x \to -\infty$.

**How to find horizontal asymptotes of rational functions:**
*   If the degree of the numerator < degree of the denominator: horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$.
*   If the degree of the numerator = degree of the denominator: horizontal asymptote at $y = \dfrac{\text{leading coefficient of numerator}}{\text{leading coefficient of denominator}}$.
*   If the degree of the numerator > degree of the denominator: no horizontal asymptote (there may be a **slant/oblique asymptote**).

**Example:** $f(x) = \dfrac{2x + 1}{x - 5}$ has a horizontal asymptote at $y = \dfrac{2}{1} = 2$.

### 6.12.3 — Asymptotes in Exponential and Logarithmic Functions

*   $f(x) = 2^x$: horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$ (as $x \to -\infty$)
*   $f(x) = \log_2(x)$: vertical asymptote at $x = 0$

---

## 6.13 — Connecting the Representations: Tables, Equations, and Graphs

One of the most important skills the ACT tests is the ability to move fluidly between the three representations of a function:

1.  **Algebraic** (equation/formula)
2.  **Tabular** (table of values)
3.  **Graphical** (graph in the coordinate plane)

### 6.13.1 — From Equation to Table

Choose several $x$-values, evaluate $f(x)$ for each, and record the results.

**Example:** For $f(x) = 2x - 1$:

| $x$ | $f(x)$ |
|---|---|
| -1 | -3 |
| 0 | -1 |
| 1 | 1 |
| 2 | 3 |
| 3 | 5 |

### 6.13.2 — From Table to Equation

Look for patterns:
*   **Constant first differences** in $f(x)$ $\to$ linear function
*   **Constant second differences** $\to$ quadratic function
*   **Constant ratios** between successive $f(x)$ values $\to$ exponential function

**Example:** Given the table:

| $x$ | $f(x)$ |
|---|---|
| 0 | 3 |
| 1 | 6 |
| 2 | 12 |
| 3 | 24 |

The ratio between successive values is constant ($6/3 = 2$, $12/6 = 2$, $24/12 = 2$), so this is exponential with growth factor 2. Since $f(0) = 3$, the equation is $f(x) = 3 \cdot 2^x$.

### 6.13.3 — From Graph to Equation

Identify the type of function from the shape, then use key points to determine the specific parameters.

**Example:** A parabola with vertex at $(2, -1)$ passing through $(0, 3)$:
*   Vertex form: $f(x) = a(x - 2)^2 - 1$
*   Substitute $(0, 3)$: $3 = a(0 - 2)^2 - 1 = 4a - 1 \implies 4a = 4 \implies a = 1$
*   Equation: $f(x) = (x - 2)^2 - 1$

---

## 6.14 — Advanced Function Concepts for High Scorers

### 6.14.1 — Concavity

A function is **concave up** on an interval if its graph curves upward (like a cup or the shape of $y = x^2$). A function is **concave down** if its graph curves downward (like an upside-down cup or the shape of $y = -x^2$).

*   For a quadratic $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$: concave up if $a > 0$, concave down if $a < 0$.
*   The **point of inflection** is where the concavity changes (relevant for cubic functions).

### 6.14.2 — Average Rate of Change

The **average rate of change** of a function $f$ over the interval $[a, b]$ is:

$$\text{Average Rate of Change} = \frac{f(b) - f(a)}{b - a}$$

This is the slope of the **secant line** connecting the points $(a, f(a))$ and $(b, f(b))$ on the graph.

**Connection to Slope:** For a linear function, the average rate of change over any interval equals the slope of the line. For nonlinear functions, the average rate of change varies depending on the interval chosen.

**ACT Relevance:** The ACT frequently asks for the average rate of change over a specified interval. This is a direct application of the slope formula applied to two points on the function's graph.

### 6.14.3 — Difference Quotient

The **difference quotient** $\dfrac{f(x + h) - f(x)}{h}$ represents the average rate of change of $f$ over the interval $[x, x + h]$. While the ACT does not typically use this terminology, the concept appears in questions that ask you to compute expressions like $\dfrac{f(3) - f(1)}{3 - 1}$.

### 6.14.4 — Rate of Change for Different Function Types

| Function Type | Rate of Change |
|---|---|
| Linear | Constant (equal to the slope) |
| Quadratic | Linear (changes at a constant rate) |
| Exponential | Proportional to the current value |
| Logarithmic | Decreasing (gets flatter as $x$ increases) |

### 6.14.5 — Systems of Functions

The ACT occasionally asks about the intersection points of two functions' graphs. The intersection points of $y = f(x)$ and $y = g(x)$ are the solutions to the equation $f(x) = g(x)$.

**Example:** Where do the graphs of $f(x) = x^2$ and $g(x) = x + 2$ intersect?
$$x^2 = x + 2$$
$$x^2 - x - 2 = 0$$
$$(x - 2)(x + 1) = 0$$
$$x = 2 \quad \text{or} \quad x = -1$$

The intersection points are $(2, 4)$ and $(-1, 1)$.

---

## 6.15 — Common ACT Traps and How to Avoid Them

### 6.15.1 — The Sign Reversal Trap (Horizontal Shifts)

As discussed in Section 6.4.3, $f(x + 2)$ shifts the graph **left**, not right. The sign inside the argument is always opposite to the direction of the shift.

**How to avoid:** Always rewrite $f(x + 2)$ as $f(x - (-2))$ to see that $h = -2$, which means a shift of 2 units in the negative $x$-direction (left).

### 6.15.2 — The Reciprocal Trap (Horizontal Stretches/Compressions)

$f(2x)$ compresses the graph horizontally by a factor of $1/2$, not 2. The factor is the reciprocal of the coefficient.

**How to avoid:** Remember that horizontal transformations affect $x$ "inversely." A larger coefficient means the function reaches the same output values at smaller $x$-values, so the graph is compressed.

### 6.15.3 — The Domain Trap

When a function involves a square root, denominator, or logarithm, the domain is restricted. The ACT loves to ask for the domain and provide answer choices that ignore these restrictions.

**How to avoid:** Always check for denominators (set $\neq 0$), even roots (set radicand $\ge 0$), and logarithms (set argument $> 0$).

### 6.15.4 — The $f(x)$ vs. $f^{-1}(x)$ Trap

The ACT may ask for $f^{-1}(3)$ when you know $f(3) = 7$. The answer is **not** 7—it's the value of $x$ such that $f(x) = 3$.

**How to avoid:** Remember that $f^{-1}(a) = b$ means $f(b) = a$. The input and output are swapped.

### 6.15.5 — The "Solve for $x$" vs. "Solve for $2x$" Trap

After solving an equation, the ACT often includes the value you found as a distractor answer choice, while the question actually asks for a related quantity.

**How to avoid:** Before selecting your answer, re-read the question stem to confirm exactly what quantity you need to find. Circle or underline the target quantity.

### 6.15.6 — The Even/Odd Function Trap

Students sometimes confuse even and odd functions. An even function satisfies $f(-x) = f(x)$; an odd function satisfies $f(-x) = -f(x)$.

**How to avoid:** Remember: "Even = $y$-axis symmetry" and "Odd = origin symmetry." Test with a simple value: for $f(x) = x^2$, $f(-3) = 9 = f(3)$, so it's even. For $f(x) = x^3$, $f(-2) = -8 = -f(2)$, so it's odd.

---

## 6.16 — Summary of Key Formulas and Properties

### Function Notation
*   $f(a)$ = output when input is $a$
*   $f(g(x))$ = composition: apply $g$ first, then $f$
*   $f^{-1}(x)$ = inverse: swap $x$ and $y$, solve for $y$

### Transformations
*   $f(x) + k$: vertical shift ($k > 0$ = up, $k < 0$ = down)
*   $f(x - h)$: horizontal shift ($h > 0$ = right, $h < 0$ = left)
*   $a \cdot f(x)$: vertical stretch/compression (and $x$-axis reflection if $a < 0$)
*   $f(bx)$: horizontal compression/stretch (and $y$-axis reflection if $b < 0$)

### Parent Functions at a Glance

| Parent Function | Equation | Shape | Domain | Range |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Linear | $f(x) = x$ | Line | $(-\infty, \infty)$ | $(-\infty, \infty)$ |
| Quadratic | $f(x) = x^2$ | Parabola (up) | $(-\infty, \infty)$ | $[0, \infty)$ |
| Cubic | $f(x) = x^3$ | S-curve | $(-\infty, \infty)$ | $(-\infty, \infty)$ |
| Square Root | $f(x) = \sqrt{x}$ | Half parabola (right) | $[0, \infty)$ | $[0, \infty)$ |
| Cube Root | $f(x) = \sqrt[3]{x}$ | S-curve (flatter) | $(-\infty, \infty)$ | $(-\infty, \infty)$ |
| Absolute Value | $f(x) = |x|$ | V-shape | $(-\infty, \infty)$ | $[0, \infty)$ |
| Reciprocal | $f(x) = \dfrac{1}{x}$ | Hyperbola | $x \neq 0$ | $y \neq 0$ |
| Exponential | $f(x) = 2^x$ | J-curve | $(-\infty, \infty)$ | $(0, \infty)$ |
| Logarithmic | $f(x) = \log_2(x)$ | Log curve | $(0, \infty)$ | $(-\infty, \infty)$ |

### Quadrant Reference

| Quadrant | $x$ | $y$ |
|---|---|---|
| I | $+$ | $+$ |
| II | $-$ | $+$ |
| III | $-$ | $-$ |
| IV | $+$ | $-$ |

---

## 6.17 — Strategic Review: How the ACT Tests This Chapter

The concepts in this chapter appear on approximately 12–18% of ACT Math questions (the "Functions" reporting category). Here is how the most common question types map to the content:

1.  **Function evaluation** (e.g., "If $f(x) = 3x^2 - 2$, what is $f(-1)$?") — Tests Section 6.2
2.  **Transformation identification** (e.g., "The graph of $g(x) = f(x - 2) + 3$ is a transformation of $f$. Which describes the transformation?") — Tests Section 6.4
3.  **Graph recognition** (e.g., "Which equation corresponds to the graph shown?") — Tests Sections 6.3 and 6.4
4.  **Domain and range** (e.g., "What is the domain of $f(x) = \sqrt{x - 5}$?") — Tests Section 6.1
5.  **Composition** (e.g., "If $f(x) = x + 1$ and $g(x) = x^2$, what is $f(g(2))$?") — Tests Section 6.5
6.  **Modeling** (e.g., "A population is modeled by $P(t) = 500(1.03)^t$. What does 1.03 represent?") — Tests Section 6.8
7.  **Increasing/decreasing intervals** (e.g., "On which interval is the function decreasing?") — Tests Section 6.9
8.  **Zeros/roots** (e.g., "What are the zeros of $f(x) = x^2 - 7x + 10$?") — Tests Section 6.11
9.  **Quadrant identification** (e.g., "If $(a, b)$ is in Quadrant III, what is the sign of $a \cdot b$?") — Tests Section 6.7

**The single most important takeaway from this chapter:** Functions are not just algebraic expressions—they are relationships between quantities that can be represented as equations, graphs, and tables. The ACT tests your ability to move between these representations and to understand how changes to the equation affect the graph (and vice versa). Master the parent functions, master the transformations, and you will have the tools to handle any function question the ACT can devise.

---


# Chapter 7: Percents, Ratios, Rates, Profit, and Mixture Problems — Translation and Dimensional Analysis

---

## 7.1 — The Architecture of Percentages: Understanding the Foundation

### What a Percent Actually Is

The word "percent" derives from the Latin *per centum*, meaning "by the hundred." A percentage is not merely a number with a decorative symbol attached—it is a **ratio whose denominator is always 100**. This is the single most important conceptual fact about percentages, and every strategy in this chapter flows from it.

When the ACT writes "25%," it is writing a shorthand for the fraction $\frac{25}{100}$, which reduces to $\frac{1}{4}$, which equals the decimal $0.25$. These three representations—fraction, percent, and decimal—are three different costumes worn by the exact same mathematical entity. The student who can fluently convert between these forms possesses a decisive speed advantage.

### The Three Conversion Operations

**Percent → Decimal:** Divide by 100 (move the decimal point two places to the left).

- $75\% = 0.75$
- $8\% = 0.08$
- $130\% = 1.30$
- $0.5\% = 0.005$

Watch carefully what happens with values less than 10%. Many students write $8\% = 0.8$ instead of $0.08$. This is one of the most common arithmetic errors on the ACT and can turn a correct method into a wrong answer. The decimal point moves **two** places, always.

**Decimal → Percent:** Multiply by 100 (move the decimal point two places to the right).

- $0.45 = 45\%$
- $0.003 = 0.3\%$
- $2.75 = 275\%$

**Percent → Fraction:** Write the percent over 100, then simplify.

- $25\% = \frac{25}{100} = \frac{1}{4}$
- $40\% = \frac{40}{100} = \frac{2}{5}$
- $12.5\% = \frac{12.5}{100} = \frac{1}{8}$

The last example ($12.5\% = \frac{1}{8}$) is particularly important. Many ACT problems involve repeating decimal-to-fraction conversions. Below is a reference table of the most common fraction-decimal-percent equivalents you must have at instant recall:

| Fraction | Decimal | Percent |
|---|---|---|
| $\frac{1}{2}$ | 0.50 | 50% |
| $\frac{1}{3}$ | 0.333... | 33$\frac{1}{3}$% |
| $\frac{1}{4}$ | 0.25 | 25% |
| $\frac{1}{5}$ | 0.20 | 20% |
| $\frac{1}{6}$ | 0.1666... | 16$\frac{2}{3}$% |
| $\frac{1}{8}$ | 0.125 | 12.5% |
| $\frac{1}{10}$ | 0.10 | 10% |
| $\frac{2}{3}$ | 0.666... | 66$\frac{2}{3}$% |
| $\frac{3}{4}$ | 0.75 | 75% |
| $\frac{3}{5}$ | 0.60 | 60% |

There is no substitute for memorizing this table. Not a single problem on the ACT will provide these conversions, and the 50-minute time limit does not afford you the luxury of deriving $\frac{1}{7}$ as a decimal from scratch.

### The Part-Whole Relationship: The Master Equation

Every percent problem on the ACT is, at its core, a variation of a single master equation:

$$\text{Part} = \text{Percent} \times \text{Whole}$$

This is sometimes written with the percent already converted to decimal form:

$$\text{Part} = \left(\frac{\text{Percent}}{100}\right) \times \text{Whole}$$

The power of this equation lies in its three variations. Depending on what the problem provides and what it asks for, you solve for a different component:

1. **Finding the Part:** $\text{Part} = \text{Percent (as decimal)} \times \text{Whole}$
   - *"What is 35% of 280?"* → $0.35 \times 280 = 98$

2. **Finding the Percent:** $\text{Percent} = \frac{\text{Part}}{\text{Whole}} \times 100$
   - *"42 is what percent of 280?"* → $\frac{42}{280} \times 100 = 15\%$

3. **Finding the Whole:** $\text{Whole} = \frac{\text{Part}}{\text{Percent (as decimal)}}$
   - *"42 is 15% of what number?"* → $\frac{42}{0.15} = 280$

The ACT will frequently disguise which of the three you need to solve for. Word problems are deliberately constructed with the unknown placed in unexpected positions. The single most reliable strategy is to **identify the "of" word** — in English, "of" translates to multiplication in mathematical language. When you read "25% of 400," the word "of" signals that you multiply: $0.25 \times 400$.

### "Percent More Than" and "Percent Less Than" — The Multiplicative Approach

This is one of the highest-frequency traps on the entire ACT Math section. Consider the following scenario:

**Problem:** A store increases the price of a $40 jacket by 25%. What is the new price?

**Incorrect approach** that catches many students: $40 + 0.25 = 40.25$. This makes no sense because 25% of 40 is $10, not $0.25. The error is failing to take the percent **of the base value first**, then adding.

**Correct approach:** $40 + (0.25 \times 40) = 40 + 10 = \$50$.

But there is an even faster method, and understanding it will save you significant time over the course of the test:

**The multiplier method.** If something increases by 25%, the new value is 125% of the original. As a decimal, that is 1.25. So:

$$40 \times 1.25 = 50$$

Similarly:
- **Decrease by 15%:** Multiply by $(1 - 0.15) = 0.85$
- **Increase by 200%:** Multiply by $(1 + 2.00) = 3.00$ (the value triples — a 200% increase does NOT double the value)
- **Decrease by 50%:** Multiply by $(1 - 0.50) = 0.50$

The critical insight is this: **"200% of" and "200% more than" mean different things.**
- "200% of $40" means $2.00 \times 40 = 80$
- "200% more than $40" means $40 + (2.00 \times 40) = $40 + $80 = $120 — which is 300% of the original

The ACT writes trap answers for both interpretations. You must read with surgical precision.

### Successive Percent Changes — The Multiplicative Chain

When a problem applies two or more percent changes in sequence (a discount followed by a tax, a markup followed by a discount, a population increase followed by a decrease), the **absolute worst** approach is to add the percentages together and apply them as a single change. This is mathematically wrong, and the ACT deliberately includes the result of this incorrect method as a trap answer.

**Problem:** A $200 coat goes on sale for 20% off, and then a 10% sales tax is applied to the sale price. What is the final price?

**Step 1 — Apply the discount:** $200 \times 0.80 = 160$

**Step 2 — Apply the tax:** $160 \times 1.10 = 176$

The final price is **$176**.

**The trap answers** you would expect to see:
- $170 (because $200 \times 0.85 = 170$ — someone added 20% - 10% = 10% off and then added 5% — nonsense, but students make compound errors)
- $160 (forgot the tax — perhaps the most common trap, designed to catch students who stop one step short)
- $180 (20% off = $40 discount, then 10% of $200 = $20 tax, giving 200 - 40 + 20 = 180 — this applies the tax to the ORIGINAL price, not the sale price)

The correct strategy is to **multiply the factors sequentially**. For the general case of a $p_1$% change followed by a $p_2$% change:

$$\text{Final} = \text{Original} \times (1 \pm \frac{p_1}{100}) \times (1 \pm \frac{p_2}{100})$$

Use the $(1 +)$ form for increases and the $(1 -)$ form for decreases. This same principle extends to any number of successive changes. A 10% increase followed by a 10% decrease does NOT return you to the original value — and this is a fact that the ACT exploits repeatedly:

$$100 \times 1.10 \times 0.90 = 99 \neq 100$$

The net result is a 1% decrease. The asymmetry between percent increase and decrease is not intuitive, and the test-makers know this. They will include the answer 100 as a trap.

---

## 7.2 — Ratio Mastery: Beyond Cross Multiplication

### Ratios as Scaled Blueprints

A ratio is a relationship, not a quantity. The ratio 3:2 does not mean "3 of something and 2 of something else" — it means "for every 3 units of the first quantity, there are 2 units of the second." The actual amounts could be 3 and 2, or 6 and 4, or 300 and 200, or $3\pi$ and $2\pi$. The ratio constrains the relationship but not the scale.

This leads to one of the most powerful ratio strategies on the ACT: **the multiplier method**.

When a problem states "the ratio of boys to girls is 3:2 and there are 40 students total," the fastest approach is:

1. Let the number of boys be $3m$ and the number of girls be $2m$, where $m$ is a positive integer (the **multiplier** or **scaling factor**).
2. Write: $3m + 2m = 40$
3. Solve: $5m = 40$, so $m = 8$
4. Boys: $3 \times 8 = 24$; Girls: $2 \times 8 = 16$

This approach works because ratios define **relative** quantities. The multiplier $m$ converts relative quantities into absolute quantities. Every ratio problem on the ACT can be approached this way, and doing so eliminates the mental friction of "figuring out what to divide by what."

### Three (and More) Part Ratios

When a ratio involves three or more parts, the multiplier method becomes even more indispensable. Consider:

*"The ratio of freshmen to sophomores to juniors is 4:3:5, and there are 360 students total."*

Let the numbers be $4m$, $3m$, and $5m$. Then:

$$4m + 3m + 5m = 360$$
$$12m = 360$$
$$m = 30$$

Freshmen: $4(30) = 120$; Sophomores: $3(30) = 90$; Juniors: $5(30) = 150$.

The multiplier method handles any number of parts uniformly. It is also the key to answering questions that ask for the **difference** between the largest and smallest groups, the **fraction** that one group represents of the whole, or the **percentage** that one group constitutes.

### Ratios from Real-World Data

Not all ratio problems present you with a ratio explicitly. Many require you to **derive** a ratio from data in a table, graph, or word problem. If a table shows that Machine A produces 120 widgets in 3 hours and Machine B produces 200 widgets in 5 hours, the production rate ratio is:

**Machine A:** $\frac{120}{3} = 40$ widgets per hour
**Machine B:** $\frac{200}{5} = 40$ widgets per hour

The ratio is 1:1 — they produce at the same rate. The ACT frequently presents students with raw data and then asks for a ratio, requiring you to first compute the individual quantities, then form the ratio.

### Equivalent Ratios — Scaling Up and Down

Two ratios are equivalent if one can be obtained from the other by multiplying both terms by the same factor. The ratio 3:5 is equivalent to 6:10, 9:15, 300:500, and $\frac{6}{10} = \frac{9}{15} = 0.6$. You can verify ratio equivalence by checking whether the fractions reduce to the same value, or by checking whether the cross-products are equal:

$$\frac{a}{b} = \frac{c}{d} \iff ad = bc$$

This cross-multiplication technique is the engine behind proportion solving. However, for the ACT, it is often faster to use a different recognition pattern: **can you multiply both terms of the smaller ratio by the same number to get the larger ratio?** If yes, they're equivalent. If no, they're not.

### Combining Ratios (Finding a Common Term)

Some of the more challenging ratio problems on the ACT give you two separate ratios involving a common element, and ask for the combined ratio of all elements. For example:

*"The ratio of boys to girls in Class A is 3:4. The ratio of boys to girls in Class B is 5:7. If the two classes are combined in a 2:1 ratio (Class A to Class B), what is the ratio of boys to girls in the combined group?"*

This problem requires you to find the actual numbers of boys and girls, apply the merging ratio, then form the new ratio. While this is an advanced problem type, the underlying mechanics still rely on the multiplier method. You would:

1. Express Class A's composition as $3m:4m$ and Class B's as $5n:7n$.
2. Set up relationships based on the merging ratio.
3. Compute total boys and total girls.
4. Form the combined ratio and simplify.

The key insight is that **you cannot simply add the ratios element-wise** (3:4 + 5:7 does NOT equal 8:11). Ratios with different totals cannot be combined without first being expressed through a common framework—either actual quantities or a common multiplier.

---

## 7.3 — Proportions: Setting Up the Equation and Tracking Units

### The Proportion as a Ratio Equation

A proportion is simply an equation stating that two ratios are equal:

$$\frac{a}{b} = \frac{c}{d}$$

This is the most important equation in all of practical mathematics. It appears on the ACT in contexts ranging from recipe scaling to map reading to unit pricing to similar triangles. The proportions you set up on the ACT are always **direct proportions** (one quantity increases as the other increases) or **inverse proportions** (one quantity increases as the other decreases).

### Unit Tracking — Dimensional Analysis

**Dimensional analysis** (also called the factor-label method) is the practice of writing units next to every number and canceling them as if they were algebraic variables. This is not merely a classroom technique — it is the single most powerful error-prevention tool available on the ACT. When units cancel to produce the desired output unit, you know your setup is correct. When they don't, you know you've made an error before you even compute anything.

**Example 1 — Unit Conversion:**
*"How many seconds are there in 3.5 hours?"*

Write the conversion as a chain of multiplication by fractions equal to 1:

$$3.5 \text{ hours} \times \frac{60 \text{ minutes}}{1 \text{ hour}} \times \frac{60 \text{ seconds}}{1 \text{ minute}}$$

The units cancel in sequence:

$$3.5 \bcancel{\text{ hours}} \times \frac{60 \bcancel{\text{ minutes}}}{1 \bcancel{\text{ hour}}} \times \frac{60 \text{ seconds}}{1 \bcancel{\text{ minute}}} = 3.5 \times 60 \times 60 \text{ seconds} = 12{,}600 \text{ seconds}$$

At each step, the unwanted unit cancels and the desired unit survives. This is not optional — it is the method by which you verify your setup is correct. If you accidentally write the conversion factor upside down ($1 \text{ hour} / 60 \text{ minutes}$), you'll get:

$$3.5 \text{ hours} \times \frac{1 \text{ hour}}{60 \text{ minutes}}$$

This would give units of hours²/minutes, which is nonsensical. **Units catch the error.** This is why I require students to write units on every number in every proportion.

**Example 2 — Rate Problem:**
*"A car travels at 65 miles per hour. How many feet does it travel in 30 seconds?"*

This requires two conversion chains:

$$65 \frac{\text{miles}}{\text{hour}} \times \frac{5280 \text{ feet}}{1 \text{ mile}} \times \frac{1 \text{ hour}}{3600 \text{ seconds}} = \frac{65 \times 5280}{3600} \text{ feet per second}$$

$$= \frac{343{,}200}{3600} = 95.33 \text{ feet per second}$$

In 30 seconds: $95.33 \times 30 = 2860$ feet.

Every one of those fractions ($5280 \text{ feet} / 1 \text{ mile}$, $1 \text{ hour} / 3600 \text{ seconds}$) equals 1 — you are multiplying by 1, which doesn't change the value, only the units in which it's expressed.

### The Proportion Setup Protocol

For any proportion problem, the recommended setup protocol is:

1. **Identify the two quantities** being compared and determine which is the numerator and which is the denominator (based on what the problem asks you to find).
2. **Write the given ratio** as a fraction with units.
3. **Write the unknown ratio** as a fraction with units, placing the unknown on top if you're solving for it, or on bottom if that's where it goes based on the unit alignment.
4. **Set the two fractions equal**.
5. **Cross-multiply and solve**.
6. **Check that the answer has the correct units** and a reasonable magnitude.

**Example:** *"If 5 pounds of apples cost $8.75, how much do 12 pounds cost?"*

$$\frac{\$8.75}{5 \text{ lb}} = \frac{x}{12 \text{ lb}}$$

Cross-multiply: $5x = 8.75 \times 12 = 105$

$x = \$21.00$

This answer makes sense: 12 pounds is more than double 5 pounds, and $21.00 is more than double $8.75. Reasonableness checks are quick and prevent careless errors.

### Inverse Proportions — When the Relationship Flips

In an **inverse proportion**, as one quantity increases, the other decreases such that their product remains constant:

$$a \times b = k \text{ (constant)}$$

The most common ACT context is **work problems**: if 4 workers can complete a task in 12 days, then (assuming each worker works at the same rate), 6 workers will complete it in fewer days:

$$4 \times 12 = 6 \times x$$
$$48 = 6x$$
$$x = 8 \text{ days}$$

The product-constant relationship is the defining feature. You set up the equation by computing the product in the first scenario, then dividing by the new value of one variable to find the other. **Do not confuse inverse proportions with direct proportions.** Read the problem carefully: "more workers, fewer days" is inverse. "more hours, more distance covered at constant speed" is direct. The test-makers know which one confuses students more, and they're not above exploiting the confusion.

---

## 7.4 — Rates: Distance, Work, and Flow

### The Speed Equation and Its Two Forms

The fundamental rate equation for speed is:

$$\text{Speed} = \frac{\text{Distance}}{\text{Time}}$$

This yields two other forms by algebraic rearrangement:

- $\text{Distance} = \text{Speed} \times \text{Time}$
- $\text{Time} = \frac{\text{Distance}}{\text{Speed}}$

All three forms are useful depending on what the problem gives you and what it asks. The critical requirement is **unit consistency**: if speed is in miles per hour, then distance must be in miles and time must be in hours. Any other unit combination requires conversion before you proceed.

**Example:** *"A train leaves Station A at 2:00 PM traveling at 75 mph. Another train leaves Station B (450 miles away) at 3:00 PM traveling at 90 mph toward Station A. At what time do they meet?"*

This is a **closing speed** problem. The two trains are approaching each other, so their relative speed is the sum: $75 + 90 = 165$ mph. But Train A has a 1-hour head start, during which it covers 75 miles. The remaining distance at 3:00 PM is $450 - 75 = 375$ miles. The time to close that gap is:

$$\text{Time} = \frac{375}{165} = 2.2727... \text{ hours} = 2 \text{ hours } 16 \text{ minutes (approximately)}$$

They meet at approximately **5:16 PM**.

The most common error on meeting problems is forgetting the head start. The second most common error is failing to add (or correctly handle) the speeds when objects move toward each other or away from each other.

### Average Speed — The Most Trapped Concept in Rate Problems

The average speed over an entire journey is defined as:

$$\text{Average Speed} = \frac{\text{Total Distance}}{\text{Total Time}}$$

This is **NOT** the average of the two speeds. If you drive 60 mph for 1 hour and 40 mph for 1 hour:

- **Wrong:** $\frac{60 + 40}{2} = 50$ mph
- **Correct:** Total distance = $60 + 40 = 100$ miles; Total time = 2 hours; Average speed = $100 / 2 = 50$ mph

In this particular case (equal times), the answers happen to match. But what if you drive 60 mph for 100 miles and 40 mph for 100 miles?

- **Wrong:** $\frac{60 + 40}{2} = 50$ mph
- **Correct:** Time at 60 mph = $100/60 = 1.667$ hours; Time at 40 mph = $100/40 = 2.5$ hours; Average speed = $200 / (1.667 + 2.5) = 200 / 4.167 = 48$ mph

The average is NOT 50. It is 48. The reason: **average speed weights each speed by the time spent at that speed, not by the distance traveled.** When times are unequal, the slower speed "drags down" the average more because it's sustained for longer. The harmonic mean is at play here, not the arithmetic mean.

The ACT includes the answer 50 as a trap for every such problem. This is not a minor detail — it is one of the most frequently tested traps in the entire rate-problem category.

### Combined Work Problems

When two machines or people work together on a task, their rates add:

$$\text{Combined Rate} = \text{Rate}_1 + \text{Rate}_2$$

Rates are typically expressed as "tasks per hour" or "fraction of the job completed per hour."

**Example:** *"Printer A can complete a print job in 40 minutes. Printer B can complete the same job in 60 minutes. How long would it take both printers working together?"*

**Step 1 — Express each as a rate (jobs per minute):**

Printer A: $\frac{1}{40}$ jobs per minute
Printer B: $\frac{1}{60}$ jobs per minute

**Step 2 — Add the rates:**

Combined rate: $\frac{1}{40} + \frac{1}{60} = \frac{3}{120} + \frac{2}{120} = \frac{5}{120} = \frac{1}{24}$ jobs per minute

**Step 3 — Invert to find time per job:**

Time = $\frac{1}{\frac{1}{24}} = 24$ minutes

**Sanity check:** Working together should be faster than either printer alone. 24 minutes is between 40 and 60 but closer to 40 (because Printer A does more of the work), which makes sense. The 24-minute answer is reasonable.

A common error is to average the times: $(40 + 60)/2 = 50$ minutes. This is wrong for the same fundamental reason that averaging speeds is wrong. **You must work with rates (outputs per unit time), not with the time values themselves.**

### Flow Rate Problems

Flow rate problems are algebraically identical to work problems. The "work" is filling or draining a tank, and the "rate" is gallons per minute (or liters per second, etc.).

**Example:** *"Pipe A fills a tank in 30 minutes. Pipe B fills the same tank in 45 minutes. Pipe C drains the tank in 60 minutes. How long does it take to fill the tank with all three pipes open?"*

Rates:
- Pipe A: $+\frac{1}{30}$ tanks per minute
- Pipe B: $+\frac{1}{45}$ tanks per minute
- Pipe C: $-\frac{1}{60}$ tanks per minute (draining, so negative)

Combined rate: $\frac{1}{30} + \frac{1}{45} - \frac{1}{60}$

Common denominator (180): $\frac{6}{180} + \frac{4}{180} - \frac{3}{180} = \frac{7}{180}$ tanks per minute

Time to fill = $\frac{1}{\frac{7}{180}} = \frac{180}{7} \approx 25.7$ minutes

The key structural elements are: **(1)** express each contribution as a rate, **(2)** assign positive signs to filling and negative signs to draining, **(3)** add to get the net rate, **(4)** invert to find the time.

---

## 7.5 — Profit, Cost, and Revenue

### The Profit Framework

Profit problems on the ACT deal with the relationship between cost, revenue, selling price, and profit. The fundamental equations are:

$$\text{Revenue} = \text{Units Sold} \times \text{Selling Price per Unit}$$
$$\text{Profit} = \text{Revenue} - \text{Total Cost}$$
$$\text{Profit per Unit} = \text{Selling Price} - \text{Cost per Unit}$$

These relationships are straightforward but can be buried inside elaborate word problems. The challenge on the ACT is not the math — which is simple arithmetic — but the **translation task**: extracting these quantities from text.

### Revenue vs. Profit — The ACT's Favorite Distinction

The single most common trap in profit problems is the failure to distinguish between **revenue** (total money brought in) and **profit** (money remaining after costs are paid).

**Example:** *"A bakery sells 200 cupcakes at $3 each. The ingredients cost $1.50 per cupcake, and the bakery pays $200 in rent. What is the bakery's profit?"*

- **Revenue:** $200 \times 3 = \$600$
- **Total cost:** $(200 \times 1.50) + 200 = 300 + 200 = \$500$
- **Profit:** $600 - 500 = \$100$

**Trap answer:** $600 (the revenue — students stop here because the "money coming in" looks like profit)
**Trap answer:** $400 (revenue minus just the ingredients: $600 - 300 = 300$ — students forget the rent)
**Trap answer:** $300 (ingredients minus something, or another partial calculation)

The ACT writes every one of these incorrect answers and places them as options. The correct calculation requires tracking all costs, including fixed costs like rent, not just per-unit costs.

### Break-Even Analysis

Some ACT problems ask for the **break-even point** — the number of units that must be sold for profit to be exactly zero:

$$\text{Break-Even Units} = \frac{\text{Fixed Costs}}{\text{Profit per Unit}}$$

**Example:** *"A company spends $5,000 on equipment and makes $25 profit per item. How many items must they sell to break even?"*

$$\frac{5000}{25} = 200 \text{ items}$$

At 200 units, total revenue equals total cost. Above 200 units, the company earns a profit. Below 200 units, it operates at a loss.

### What-If Profit Analysis

Advanced ACT problems introduce scenarios where cost or price changes:

*"If the cost per unit increases by $2, how many more units must be sold to maintain the same total profit?"*

This requires setting up equations for profit under both the old and new cost structures and comparing the break-even or target-sales values. The approach is always algebraic: define variables, write the profit equations, and solve. The specific numbers change from test to test, but the structure is remarkably stable.

---

## 7.6 — Mixture Problems: The Weighted Concentration Equation

### The Mixture Master Equation

Mixture problems are, at their core, **addition problems applied to concentrations**. When you combine two or more solutions with different concentrations, the key principle is:

$$(\text{Concentration}_1 \times \text{Volume}_1) + (\text{Concentration}_2 \times \text{Volume}_2) = \text{Target Concentration} \times \text{Total Volume}$$

Concentration is usually expressed as a decimal or fraction (e.g., 30% = 0.30). The total volume is $V_1 + V_2$.

**Example:** *"A chemist mixes 5 liters of a 40% acid solution with 3 liters of a 70% acid solution. What is the concentration of the resulting mixture?"*

**Step 1 — Compute the total amount of acid in each original solution:**

Solution 1: $0.40 \times 5 = 2$ liters of pure acid
Solution 2: $0.70 \times 3 = 2.1$ liters of pure acid

**Step 2 — Add the acid amounts and the volumes:**

Total acid: $2 + 2.1 = 4.1$ liters
Total volume: $5 + 3 = 8$ liters

**Step 3 — Divide to get the new concentration:**

$$\frac{4.1}{8} = 0.5125 = 51.25\%$$

The result (51.25%) is between 40% and 70%, which is a necessary sanity check. A mixture concentration always falls between the concentrations of the original solutions. If your answer falls outside this range, you've made an error.

### Mixture Problems with an Unknown Variable

The ACT frequently gives you the target concentration and one of the volumes, and asks you to find the other volume or the amount of pure substance to add.

**Example:** *"How many liters of pure acid (100% concentration) must be added to 10 liters of a 20% acid solution to produce a 50% solution?"*

Apply the master equation with volumes:

$$(0.20 \times 10) + (1.00 \times x) = 0.50 \times (10 + x)$$
$$2 + x = 5 + 0.50x$$
$$x - 0.50x = 5 - 2$$
$$0.50x = 3$$
$$x = 6 \text{ liters}$$

**Verification:** Starting with 10 liters at 20% (2 liters of acid), adding 6 liters of pure acid gives 8 liters of acid in 16 liters total: $8/16 = 50\%$. Correct.

**Common error:** Students sometimes write the equation with the total volume as 10 instead of $(10 + x)$, forgetting that adding acid increases the total volume. In a problem like this, if the question asks how much **pure acid** to add, the added volume increases the total. If the question asks how much **water** to add, the same principle applies but the water contributes volume without acid. If the question asks how much **acid to evaporate**, the total volume decreases.

Each variation requires careful tracking of what's being added or removed and whether it contributes to the numerator (pure substance), the denominator (total volume), or both.

### Replacement Problems — A Special Mixture Variant

Some ACT problems involve **replacing** part of a mixture:

*"Ten liters of a 50% solution has 4 liters removed and replaced with 4 liters of pure water. What is the new concentration?"*

**Step 1 — After removal:** $10 - 4 = 6$ liters of 50% solution remain. This contains $6 \times 0.50 = 3$ liters of pure acid.

**Step 2 — After replacement:** Adding 4 liters of water doesn't add any acid. Pure acid is still 3 liters, but total volume is back to 10 liters. New concentration: $3/10 = 30\%$.

The key insight: **removing solution removes acid proportionally.** If half the volume is removed, half the acid is removed. The dilution formula for replacement is:

$$\text{New concentration} = \text{Original concentration} \times \left(\frac{V_{\text{remaining}}}{V_{\text{total}}}\right)^n$$

where $n$ is the number of replacement steps. For a single replacement of $r$ liters from a total volume $V$:

$$\text{New concentration} = \text{Original concentration} \times \frac{V - r}{V}$$

This elegant formula allows you to solve replacement problems in a single line, without the two-step process. However, understanding why it works (proportionality of removal) is essential for adapting to unfamiliar problem contexts.

---

## 7.7 — Advanced Proportion and Ratio Techniques

### Map Scale and Model Scale Problems

Map scale problems are proportion problems in disguise. A scale of 1:500,000 means 1 unit on the map represents 500,000 units on the ground. The key skill is **unit conversion** in the context of the ratio.

**Example:** *"On a map with a scale of 1 inch = 25 miles, two cities are 3.5 inches apart. What is the actual distance?"*

$$\frac{1 \text{ inch}}{25 \text{ miles}} = \frac{3.5 \text{ inches}}{x \text{ miles}}$$
$$x = 3.5 \times 25 = 87.5 \text{ miles}$$

**Example (reverse):** *"Two cities are 120 miles apart. On a map with a scale of 1:1,000,000, what is the distance in inches?"*

First convert 120 miles to inches: $120 \times 5280 \times 12 = 7{,}603{,}200$ inches.

Apply the scale: $\frac{1}{1{,}000{,}000} = \frac{x}{7{,}603{,}200}$
$$x = \frac{7{,}603{,}200}{1{,}000{,}000} = 7.6032 \text{ inches}$$

These problems require **multiple unit conversions within a proportion**. The strategic approach is to convert everything to a consistent unit first, then set up the proportion. Trying to convert as you go within the ratio often leads to errors.

### Similar Figure Proportions

When geometric figures are similar (same shape, proportional sizes), all corresponding linear dimensions share the same ratio. This ratio is called the **scale factor**. The proportion setup for similar figures is:

$$\frac{\text{Dimension in Figure A}}{\text{Corresponding Dimension in Figure B}} = \frac{\text{Another Dimension in Figure A}}{\text{Corresponding Another Dimension in Figure B}}$$

This is used extensively in the Geometry section but originates from the same ratio logic as the proportion problems in this chapter. The extension to area (where the area ratio equals the **square** of the linear scale factor) connects to the Scaling Laws discussed in the Geometry chapter.

### Data Interpretation: Reading Tables for Ratios and Proportions

Some ACT problems present data in a table and ask you to compute ratios, proportions, or percentages from the data. These are not conceptually harder — they test whether you can identify the correct values to use in the computation. The most common trap is **reading the wrong cell** — using the total when you need a subtotal, or vice versa.

**Example:** A table of company sales by quarter and product. The question asks: *"Product A's sales in Q3 were approximately what percent of total Q3 sales?"*

You proceed by:
1. Locating Q3 sales for Product A (say, $45,000)
2. Locating total Q3 sales across all products (say, $180,000)
3. Computing $(45{,}000 \div 180{,}000) \times 100 = 25\%$

The arithmetic is trivial. The challenge is navigation — quickly and correctly identifying the relevant numbers from a dense data display. This is why the ability to scan tables efficiently is a trainable skill worth developing through practice.

### Proportion with Percentages Embedded

Occasionally, the ACT sets up a proportion where one or both sides involves percentage increases or decreases. The setup is still a standard proportion, but the values are computed from percent changes:

**Example:** *"Last year, a town's population was 50,000. It increased by 8% this year. The ratio of children to adults this year is 3:7. How many children are there?"*

Step 1: Find this year's population: $50{,}000 \times 1.08 = 54{,}000$
Step 2: Apply the ratio (3 parts children out of 10 total parts): $\frac{3}{10} \times 54{,}000 = 16{,}200$ children

This is a compound problem: percent change first, then ratio. The ACT frequently combines concepts from different chapters, and this is a small preview of that tendency. As you progress through this study guide, the connections between topics will become increasingly important.

---

## 7.8 — Strategic Synthesis: When to Use Which Tool

### Decision Framework for Rate/Ratio/Percent Problems

When you encounter a word problem, the following decision sequence will guide you to the right approach:

1. **Is the problem about comparing two quantities of the same type (both distances, both volumes, both dollars)?**
   - **Yes, and the comparison is "how many times greater" or a fraction:** You have a **ratio** problem. Use the ratio multiplier.
   - **Yes, and the comparison involves a rate per unit:** You have a **rate** problem. Identify the rate formula and plug in.

2. **Is the problem about a part of a whole expressed as a number out of 100?**
   - **Yes:** You have a **percent** problem. Use the Part = Percent × Whole framework.

3. **Does the problem involve buying, selling, cost, or money made?**
   - **Yes:** You have a **profit/revenue** problem. Identify cost per unit, selling price, fixed costs, and compute profit as revenue minus total cost.

4. **Does the problem involve combining two solutions or groups?**
   - **Yes:** You have a **mixture** problem. Use the concentration master equation or the weighted average formula.

5. **Does the problem involve equal ratios?**
   - **Yes:** You have a **proportion** problem. Set up the fraction equation, cross-multiply, and solve.

6. **Does the problem involve converting units?**
   - **Yes:** Use **dimensional analysis** — write conversion factors as fractions equal to 1 and cancel units.

### The Consistency Principle

Every ratio, proportion, rate, percent, and mixture problem ultimately comes down to **consistency of units and relationships.** The ACT tests your ability to:
- Track which number represents the part, which the whole, and which the rate.
- Set up equations where the units on both sides cancel correctly.
- Verify that the answer is numerically reasonable (e.g., a mixture concentration between the original concentrations; a percent between 0 and 100% for realistic scenarios; a speed that makes sense in context).

Internalizing these consistency checks is more valuable than memorizing additional formulas, because formula recall fails under pressure while structural reasoning endures.

---

## 7.9 — Common ACT Traps in Percents, Ratios, Rates, Profit, and Mixtures

Understanding the traps is as important as understanding the content. The ACT writes trap answers for specific, predictable errors. Below is a catalog of the traps you will encounter, organized by the type of error that produces them.

### Trap 1: The "Average of Speeds" Error
- **Problem type:** Average speed over a trip with different speeds for different segments.
- **Wrong approach:** $\frac{S_1 + S_2}{2}$
- **Correct approach:** Total distance ÷ Total time
- **Why it matters:** The harmonic mean, not the arithmetic mean, governs average speed when segments are defined by distance. Only when the time spent at each speed is equal do the two methods give the same result.

### Trap 2: The "Addition of Percentages" Error
- **Problem type:** Successive percent changes (discount then tax, increase then decrease).
- **Wrong approach:** Combining percentages by addition: -20% + 10% = -10% net, then applying -10%.
- **Correct approach:** Multiplying factors sequentially: $0.80 \times 1.10 = 0.88$.
- **Why it matters:** Percent changes are applied to different base values, so they cannot be algebraically combined.

### Trap 3: The "Revenue Equals Profit" Error
- **Problem type:** Profit problems with both fixed and variable costs.
- **Wrong approach:** Computing revenue (units × price) and stopping there, or computing profit without fixed costs.
- **Correct approach:** Profit = Total Revenue - Total Cost, where Total Cost includes both fixed and variable components.
- **Why it matters:** The ACT includes revenue as one answer and profit-missing-fixed-costs as another, catching students at different levels of incomplete work.

### Trap 4: The "Forgot to Update the Total Volume" Error
- **Problem type:** Mixture problems where a substance is added.
- **Wrong approach:** Using the original total volume instead of the new total volume.
- **Correct approach:** The total volume changes by exactly the amount added or removed.
- **Why it matters:** The concentration denominator must always match the current total volume.

### Trap 5: The "Ratio Inversion" Error
- **Problem type:** Any ratio or proportion problem where the order of comparison matters.
- **Wrong approach:** Writing the ratio as 2:3 when the problem states 3:2, or placing the wrong quantity on top in your proportion fraction.
- **Correct approach:** Read the comparison literally — "ratio of A to B" means $A:B$, with A on top of the fraction and B on the bottom.
- **Why it matters:** The ACT includes both versions as answers to catch inattentive readers.

### Trap 6: The "Concentration Equals Amount" Error
- **Problem type:** Mixture problems where the question asks for concentration but you compute total amount of substance.
- **Wrong approach:** Computing the total acid in the mixture (e.g., 4.1 liters) and selecting that value.
- **Correct approach:** Dividing total acid by total volume to get the percentage: $4.1 / 8 = 51.25\%$.
- **Why it matters:** The raw amount and the percentage are different quantities. The problem specifically asks for concentration (percentage), not the amount of solute.

### Trap 7: The "Percent Increase When Given Percent of" Error
- **Problem type:** "40% of the students are boys. What percent more girls than boys are there?"
- **Wrong approach:** Saying 20% more (60% - 40% = 20%).
- **Correct approach:** The difference between girls and boys as a percent of the number of boys: $(60 - 40)/40 \times 100 = 50\%$.
- **Why it matters:** "Percent more than" is always relative to the reference value (the thing after "than"). If boys = 40, girls = 60, then there are 20 more boys-worth-of-girls, and $20/40 = 50\%$ more girls than boys.

### Trap 8: The "Break-Even with Partial Units" Error
- **Problem type:** Problems asking "how many units must be sold" where the answer is not a whole number.
- **Wrong approach:** Rounding down (you can't sell a partial unit, but you can't break even on a partial unit either — you need to sell the next whole unit).
- **Correct approach:** Always round **up** to the next whole number, since you cannot sell a fraction of a unit and breaking even requires meeting or exceeding the target.
- **Why it matters:** If the formula gives 199.2, selling 199 units doesn't quite reach break-even. You need 200. The ACT includes 199 as a trap.

---

## 7.10 — The English-to-Math Translation Dictionary for This Chapter

Building on the general translation strategies from earlier chapters, here are the specific verbal cues and their mathematical translations for this chapter's problem types:

| Verbal Cue | Mathematical Translation |
|---|---|
| "is" | $=$ |
| "of" (after a percent or fraction) | $\times$ |
| "more than" | Addition, or comparison (depends on context) |
| "less than" | Subtraction, or comparison |
| "ratio of A to B" | $A:B$ or $\frac{A}{B}$ |
| "per" | $\div$ (rate: quantity per one unit) |
| "for every" | Ratio setup — establishes proportional relationship |
| "percent" | $\div 100$ (convert to decimal for calculation) |
| "what percent" | The unknown is a percentage — solve for it, then convert to percent form |
| "concentration of" | Amount of solute $\div$ total volume of solution |
| "profit" | Revenue $-$ Cost |
| "markup" | Increase on cost → new selling price |
| "discount" | Decrease on price → new selling price |
| "break even" | Profit $= 0$, or Revenue $=$ Cost |
| "mixture" | Two or more components combined — total amounts add |
| "replacement" | Remove some, add some — track what stays and what changes |

The ACT deliberately writes long, elaborate word problems in which these cues are embedded in dense paragraphs. The strategy is to **underline every numerical fact and every verbal cue**, then translate piece by piece into the mathematical framework. This systematic approach converts even intimidating problems into a series of small, manageable translation steps.

---

This completes Chapter 7. The concepts covered here — percentages, ratios, rates, proportions, profit, and mixtures — collectively account for a substantial fraction of the ACT Math section, both as standalone questions and as embedded steps within problems from other content areas. Mastery of the unit-tracking mindset (dimensional analysis), the multiplier method for ratios, the Part = Percent × Whole framework, the mixture concentration equation, and the recognition of common traps will serve you throughout the remaining chapters of this study guide and on test day itself.

---


# Chapter 8: Data Analysis, Statistics, and Probability — Graph Interpretation, Measures of Center and Spread, and Counting Principles

---

## 8.1 — The Role of Data Analysis on the ACT Mathematics Section

Data Analysis, Statistics, and Probability collectively represent approximately 8–12% of the ACT Mathematics test — which, on the current 60-question test, translates to roughly 5–7 questions, and on the Enhanced ACT (45 questions), approximately 4–5 questions. While this may seem like a modest slice of the exam, these questions are among the most *strategically exploitable* on the entire test. Why? Because they rely heavily on conceptual understanding and logical reasoning rather than complex computation. A student who deeply understands the *behavior* of statistical measures, the *logic* of data displays, and the *principles* of counting can answer these questions with remarkable speed — often in 20–30 seconds each — thereby "banking" precious time for the more computationally intensive algebra and geometry problems elsewhere.

The ACT tests data analysis across three broad domains:

1. **Graphical Interpretation and Data Display** — Reading, interpreting, and extracting information from various types of graphs and charts.
2. **Measures of Center and Spread** — Understanding mean, median, mode, range, interquartile range, and standard deviation, including how these measures behave under transformations and in different distributional contexts.
3. **Probability and Counting Principles** — Computing probabilities of simple and compound events, understanding independent versus dependent events, and applying the fundamental counting principle, permutations, and combinations.

Each of these domains will be explored in exhaustive detail in the sections that follow.

---

## 8.2 — Types of Data: Quantitative vs. Categorical

Before discussing any graph or statistical measure, you must understand the fundamental distinction between the two types of data the ACT recognizes, because the type of data *dictates which displays and which measures of center are appropriate*.

### Quantitative Data (Numerical Data)

Quantitative data consists of numbers that carry mathematical meaning — you can meaningfully add, subtract, multiply, and divide these values. Examples include:

- Heights of students (e.g., 5.2 feet, 6.1 feet)
- Test scores (e.g., 87, 92, 76)
- Temperatures (e.g., 72°F, 98.6°F)
- Annual incomes (e.g., $45,000, $120,000)
- Times to complete a race (e.g., 12.4 seconds, 15.1 seconds)

Quantitative data can be further subdivided:

- **Discrete quantitative data** takes on countable, separate values (often integers). Examples: number of siblings (0, 1, 2, 3, …), number of questions correct on a test (0, 1, 2, …, 60).
- **Continuous quantitative data** can take on any value within a range, including fractions and decimals. Examples: height, weight, time, temperature.

### Categorical Data (Qualitative Data)

Categorical data consists of labels, names, or categories. You cannot meaningfully perform arithmetic on these values. Examples:

- Eye color (blue, brown, green, hazel)
- Favorite genre of music (rock, pop, classical, jazz)
- Type of vehicle (sedan, SUV, truck, van)
- Zip code (note: even though zip codes are *written* as numbers, they are *labels* — it makes no sense to compute the "average zip code")

### Why This Distinction Matters on the ACT

The ACT frequently tests whether you know *which graph type is appropriate for which data type*. This is not a trivial or pedantic distinction — it is a recurring question type. The pairing is as follows:

| **Quantitative Data** | **Categorical Data** |
|---|---|
| Histogram | Bar Graph |
| Dot Plot | Pie Chart |
| Box and Whisker Plot | |
| Stem and Leaf Plot | |

A histogram looks similar to a bar graph, but there is a critical difference: in a histogram, the bars are *adjacent* (touching) because they represent intervals of a continuous or discrete quantitative variable. In a bar graph, the bars are *separated* because they represent distinct categories.

**ACT Trap Alert:** The ACT may present a bar graph and ask you to compute the mean of the data. This is generally *not* possible from a standard bar graph unless the graph explicitly labels each individual data point's value. A bar graph for categorical data shows frequencies of categories — you cannot compute a mean of "blue, red, green." Even for a bar graph showing numerical values on the vertical axis, if the horizontal axis represents categories (like "Year 2019, Year 2020, Year 2021"), you cannot compute a meaningful mean of the categories themselves.

---

## 8.3 — Graphical Displays: Deep Dive

### 8.3.1 — Histograms

A **histogram** displays the distribution of quantitative data using adjacent bars. The horizontal axis represents *bins* (intervals) of equal or unequal width, and the vertical axis represents *frequency* (count) or *relative frequency* (percentage).

**Key features to notice:**
- Bars touch each other (no gaps between adjacent bars).
- The width of each bin determines how many data points fall in that range.
- If bins are of unequal width, the vertical axis should represent *density* (frequency ÷ bin width) rather than raw frequency — though the ACT typically uses equal-width bins with raw frequency on the vertical axis.

**How to read a histogram on the ACT:**
1. Identify the bin labels on the horizontal axis.
2. Read the height of each bar to determine the frequency of that bin.
3. To find the total number of data points, sum the frequencies of all bins.
4. To find how many data points fall *above* or *below* a certain value, sum the relevant bins.

**Critical limitation:** A histogram shows you *how many* data points fall in each interval, but it does NOT show the *individual values*. Therefore, you **cannot** determine the exact mean, median, or mode from a histogram alone (unless each bin represents a single value, which would make it functionally a dot plot). The ACT loves to test this limitation by asking "Can the mean be determined from the histogram?" — the answer is typically no.

### 8.3.2 — Dot Plots

A **dot plot** is one of the most transparent data displays. Each individual data point is represented as a dot stacked above its value on a number line. This means you can literally *read off every individual data value* from a dot plot.

**Key features:**
- Each dot represents one data point (unless a key specifies otherwise).
- The horizontal axis is a number line showing the quantitative variable.
- Clusters, gaps, and outliers are immediately visible.

**Why dot plots are powerful on the ACT:** Because every individual data value is visible, you *can* compute the exact mean, median, mode, and range from a dot plot. The ACT frequently uses dot plots as the basis for questions about measures of center and spread.

**Example of reasoning:** If a dot plot shows values of 2, 3, 3, 5, 7, 8, 10, you can count that there are 7 data points, identify the mode as 3 (most frequent), find the median as the 4th value (5), compute the mean as $(2+3+3+5+7+8+10)/7 \approx 5.43$, and determine the range as $10 - 2 = 8$.

### 8.3.3 — Box and Whisker Plots (Box Plots)

A **box and whisker plot** (or simply "box plot") is a compact display of the five-number summary of a dataset. It is one of the most information-dense displays you will encounter on the ACT, and understanding every component is essential.

**The Five-Number Summary:**

1. **Minimum** — the smallest data value (end of the lower whisker)
2. **First Quartile ($Q_1$)** — the 25th percentile (bottom edge of the box)
3. **Median ($Q_2$)** — the 50th percentile (line inside the box)
4. **Third Quartile ($Q_3$)** — the 75th percentile (top edge of the box)
5. **Maximum** — the largest data value (end of the upper whisker)

**Visual structure:**

```
    |--------[   |   ]---------|
   Min       Q₁  Q₂  Q₃       Max
         lower     |    upper
         quartile  |   quartile
                   median
```

The box spans from $Q_1$ to $Q_3$ (the **interquartile range**, or IQR). The whiskers extend from the minimum to $Q_1$ and from $Q_3$ to the maximum.

**What a box plot tells you:**
- **The median** is the line inside the box. It tells you the "center" of the data in terms of position.
- **The IQR** ($Q_3 - Q_1$) tells you the spread of the middle 50% of the data. A larger IQR means more variability in the middle half; a smaller IQR means the middle half is tightly clustered.
- **The whiskers** show the spread of the lower 25% and upper 25% of the data.
- **Outliers** are sometimes shown as individual points beyond the whiskers. The ACT may or may not display outliers — if they are not shown, the whiskers extend to the actual minimum and maximum.

**What a box plot does NOT tell you:**
- **The mean.** A box plot shows the median, not the mean. You cannot compute or determine the mean from a box plot alone. This is one of the most frequently tested facts on the ACT.
- **The number of data points.** A box plot does not indicate how many values are in the dataset. A box plot could represent 20 data points or 2,000 data points.
- **The shape of the distribution in fine detail.** While you can get a rough sense of symmetry versus skew from the relative lengths of the whiskers and the position of the median within the box, you cannot see the precise shape as you would in a histogram or dot plot.

**Comparing box plots:** The ACT frequently presents two side-by-side box plots and asks you to compare their medians, IQRs, ranges, or overall distributions. When comparing:
- A higher median line means the second dataset has a higher "typical" value.
- A taller box (larger IQR) means the second dataset has more variability in its middle 50%.
- A longer overall whisker-to-whisker span (larger range) means the second dataset has more overall spread.

### 8.3.4 — Stem and Leaf Plots

A **stem and leaf plot** is another display that preserves individual data values. The "stem" represents the leading digit(s), and the "leaf" represents the trailing digit.

**Example:**

```
Stem | Leaf
  2  | 3 5 7
  3  | 1 4 4 8
  4  | 0 2 6
  5  | 1
```

Key: 2 | 3 = 23

This represents the data set: {23, 25, 27, 31, 34, 34, 38, 40, 42, 46, 51}

**Key features:**
- You can read every individual data value.
- The shape of the distribution is visible (rotate 90° counterclockwise and it resembles a histogram).
- The median, mode, range, and mean can all be computed from a stem and leaf plot.

**ACT tip:** Always check the key! The key tells you what the stem and leaf represent. A stem of 4 with a leaf of 2 could mean 42, 4.2, 400, or 402 depending on the key.

### 8.3.5 — Bar Graphs

A **bar graph** (or bar chart) is used for **categorical data**. Each bar represents a category, and the height of the bar represents the frequency or count of that category.

**Key features:**
- Bars are *separated* by gaps (unlike a histogram where bars touch).
- The horizontal axis shows categories (labels), not a numerical scale.
- The vertical axis shows frequency or count.

**What you can do with a bar graph:** Read frequencies, compare categories, identify the mode (the category with the tallest bar).

**What you cannot do with a bar graph:** Compute the mean or median of the data (unless the categories themselves are numerical and you have the raw data, which a standard bar graph does not provide).

### 8.3.6 — Pie Charts

A **pie chart** (or circle graph) shows how a whole is divided into parts. Each "slice" represents a proportion of the total.

**Key features:**
- The entire circle represents 100% of the data.
- The size of each slice corresponds to its proportion of the whole.
- Pie charts are used for categorical data.

**Reading a pie chart on the ACT:**
1. Identify the total quantity represented (sometimes given in the problem text, not on the chart itself).
2. If a slice is labeled with a percentage, multiply the total by that percentage to find the count for that category.
3. If a slice is labeled with an angle measure, the proportion of the total equals the angle divided by $360°$.
4. If two or more slices are combined, add their percentages or angles.

**Example:** A pie chart shows that 30% of a company's 500 employees work in sales. The number of sales employees = $0.30 \times 500 = 150$.

**ACT trap:** Pie charts do not directly show raw counts — they show proportions. If the total is not given, you cannot determine actual counts from percentages alone.

### 8.3.7 — Scatterplots and Trend Lines
