This protocol transforms a single problem from a one-minute exercise into a multi-layered learning experience. It is slow. It is deliberate. It is the only way to improve.

---

## 10.1: Pre-Algebra & Number Sense (Problems 1–15)

### Problem Category Overview

Pre-algebra problems constitute approximately 15–20% of the ACT Math section. They test foundational arithmetic, number properties, fractions, decimals, percents, and basic number theory. These problems are typically found in the first third of the section, but the ACT increasingly wraps pre-algebra concepts in sophisticated word-problem clothing.

### Annotated Problem Series 1–5: Integers, Factors, and Multiples

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

When the ACT asks about factors, multiples, divisibility, or prime factorization, it is testing your ability to decompose numbers into their fundamental building blocks. The key insight is that *every positive integer can be uniquely expressed as a product of primes* — this is the Fundamental Theorem of Arithmetic, and it underlies every factor/multiple problem on the test.

**Deep Dive — Greatest Common Factor (GCF):**

The GCF of two numbers is found by:
1. Prime factorize both numbers.
2. Identify common prime factors.
3. Take the *lowest* power of each common prime.
4. Multiply these together.

**Why lowest power?** Because the GCF must divide *both* numbers. If one number has $2^3$ and the other has $2^2$, the GCF can only claim $2^2$ — any higher power wouldn't divide the second number.

**Deep Dive — Least Common Multiple (LCM):**

The LCM of two numbers is found by:
1. Prime factorize both numbers.
2. Take the *highest* power of *every* prime appearing in either factorization.
3. Multiply these together.

**Why highest power?** Because the LCM must be divisible by *both* numbers. If one number has $2^3$ and the other has $2^2$, the LCM must have at least $2^3$ to be divisible by the first number.

**The Critical Relationship:** For any two positive integers $a$ and $b$:

$$\text{GCF}(a, b) \times \text{LCM}(a, b) = a \times b$$

This relationship is *not* provided on the test. It must be memorized. It allows you to find the LCM if you know the GCF, and vice versa.

**Trap Recognition:** The most common trap on GCF/LCM problems is confusing the two. The ACT will include the GCF as a distractor for LCM questions and vice versa. Always ask yourself: "Am I looking for something that *divides* both numbers (GCF) or something that *both numbers divide into* (LCM)?"

### Annotated Problem Series 6–10: Fractions, Decimals, and Percents

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Fraction problems on the ACT are almost never about complex computation — they're about *flexible representation*. The same quantity can be expressed as a fraction, a decimal, or a percent, and the test rewards students who can move fluidly between these representations.

**Deep Dive — The Percent-Fraction-Decimal Trinity:**

Memorize these conversions cold:

| Fraction | Decimal | Percent |
|----------|---------|---------|
| $\dfrac{1}{2}$ | 0.50 | 50% |
| $\dfrac{1}{3}$ | 0.333... | 33$\frac{1}{3}$% |
| $\dfrac{1}{4}$ | 0.25 | 25% |
| $\dfrac{1}{5}$ | 0.20 | 20% |
| $\dfrac{1}{6}$ | 0.166... | 16$\frac{2}{3}$% |
| $\dfrac{1}{8}$ | 0.125 | 12.5% |
| $\dfrac{1}{9}$ | 0.111... | 11$\frac{1}{9}$% |
| $\dfrac{1}{10}$ | 0.10 | 10% |
| $\dfrac{1}{12}$ | 0.0833... | 8$\frac{1}{3}$% |
| $\dfrac{2}{3}$ | 0.666... | 66$\frac{2}{3}$% |
| $\dfrac{3}{4}$ | 0.75 | 75% |
| $\dfrac{3}{8}$ | 0.375 | 37.5% |
| $\dfrac{5}{8}$ | 0.625 | 62.5% |

**The "Percent of" Construction:**

When a problem says "What is 25% of 80?", the word "of" signals multiplication: $0.25 \times 80 = 20$. This seems obvious, but the ACT buries this construction in complex sentences. Train yourself to instantly recognize "of" as "times."

**The "Percent More/Less" Construction:**

"A is 25% more than B" means $A = B + 0.25B = 1.25B$.

"A is 25% less than B" means $A = B - 0.25B = 0.75B$.

**Critical trap:** Students often compute 25% of B and then add/subtract it from the wrong base. The phrase "A is 25% more than B" means B is the reference value, not A.

**Deep Dive — Successive Percent Changes:**

This is one of the most frequently tested pre-algebra concepts, and it is almost always designed to be solved by the *multiplicative factor method*, NOT by adding percentages.

If a price increases by 20% and then decreases by 20%, the final price is NOT the same as the original. Here's why:

Let original price = $P$.

After 20% increase: $P \times 1.20 = 1.2P$

After 20% decrease: $1.2P \times 0.80 = 0.96P$

The final price is 96% of the original — a net 4% decrease.

**The General Principle:** For successive changes of $a\%$ and $b\%$, the overall factor is $(1 + \frac{a}{100})(1 + \frac{b}{100})$. The order doesn't matter (multiplication is commutative), but the base changes at each step.

**Trap Recognition:** The ACT will always include "0%" as a distractor for successive change problems, and "the sum of the changes" (e.g., "no net change" for +20% and -20%) as another distractor.

### Annotated Problem Series 11–15: Ratios, Proportions, and Rates

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Ratio problems test your ability to maintain proportional relationships across different contexts. The fundamental principle is that *ratios can be scaled by any common factor* — if a ratio is $3:5$, the actual quantities could be $3$ and $5$, or $6$ and $10$, or $300$ and $500$, or $3k$ and $5k$ for any positive $k$.

**Deep Dive — The Part-to-Part vs. Part-to-Whole Distinction:**

A ratio of boys to girls of $3:5$ means:
- Part-to-part: boys:girls = $3:5$
- Part-to-whole: boys:total = $3:8$
- Part-to-whole: girls:total = $5:8$

The ACT frequently tests whether you can convert between these representations. If a problem says "the ratio of boys to girls is 3:5" and asks "what fraction of the class is boys?", the answer is $\frac{3}{8}$, not $\frac{3}{5}$.

**Deep Dive — Setting Up Proportions:**

When setting up a proportion, the critical rule is that *corresponding quantities must be in corresponding positions*. If you set up $\frac{a}{b} = \frac{c}{d}$, then $a$ and $c$ must represent the same type of quantity, and $b$ and $d$ must represent the same type of quantity.

**Unit tracking** is your best defense against setup errors. Write the units beside each number:

$$\frac{5 \text{ miles}}{1 \text{ hour}} = \frac{x \text{ miles}}{3 \text{ hours}}$$

The units confirm that miles correspond to miles and hours correspond to hours. If the units don't align, your proportion is wrong.

**Deep Dive — Rate Problems:**

The fundamental rate formula is:

$$\text{Rate} = \frac{\text{Quantity}}{\text{Time (or other denominator)}}$$

For speed: $\text{Speed} = \frac{\text{Distance}}{\text{Time}}$

For work: $\text{Rate} = \frac{\text{Work Done}}{\text{Time}}$

For density: $\text{Density} = \frac{\text{Mass}}{\text{Volume}}$

**The Average Speed Trap:** The average speed over a journey is *never* the average of the speeds (unless the time intervals are equal). Average speed is always:

$$\text{Average Speed} = \frac{\text{Total Distance}}{\text{Total Time}}$$

If you travel 60 mph for 1 hour and 40 mph for 1 hour, the average speed is $\frac{60 + 40}{2} = 50$ mph (this works because the *times* are equal).

But if you travel 60 mph for 10 miles and 40 mph for 10 miles, the times are different: $\frac{10}{60} + \frac{10}{40} = \frac{1}{6} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{5}{12}$ hours. Average speed = $\frac{20}{\frac{5}{12}} = 48$ mph, not 50 mph.

---

## 10.2: Elementary Algebra (Problems 16–35)

### Problem Category Overview

Elementary algebra problems constitute approximately 15–20% of the test. They involve linear equations, linear inequalities, systems of equations, and basic polynomial operations. These problems reward precision in algebraic manipulation and the ability to translate between verbal descriptions and algebraic expressions.

### Annotated Problem Series 16–20: Linear Equations

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Linear equations on the ACT are almost always solvable in 3–5 steps. The key is to *isolate the variable* by performing inverse operations in the correct order. The standard sequence is:

1. Distribute to eliminate parentheses.
2. Combine like terms on each side.
3. Move variable terms to one side and constants to the other.
4. Divide by the coefficient of the variable.

**Deep Dive — The "No Solution" and "Infinite Solutions" Cases:**

When solving a linear equation, you may arrive at:

- **A contradiction** (e.g., $3 = -5$): The equation has **no solution**. This occurs when the variable terms cancel out and leave a false statement.
- **An identity** (e.g., $5 = 5$): The equation has **infinitely many solutions**. This occurs when both sides are identical for all values of the variable.

The ACT tests these cases deliberately. If a problem says "for what value of $k$ does the equation $3x + k = 3x + 5$ have no solution?", the answer is "any value except 5" (because if $k = 5$, the equation is an identity with infinite solutions; if $k \neq 5$, it's a contradiction with no solution). More commonly, the question asks for the specific value that makes the equation have *infinitely many* solutions — in this case, $k = 5$.

**Trap Recognition:** When a problem involves a linear equation with an additional parameter (like $k$ above), the trap is to solve for $x$ in terms of $k$ instead of determining the value of $k$ that creates a special case. Always answer the question actually asked.

### Annotated Problem Series 21–25: Linear Inequalities

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Linear inequalities are solved identically to linear equations, with **one critical exception**: when you multiply or divide both sides by a negative number, the inequality sign *flips*.

This is not an arbitrary rule — it follows from the number line. If $3 < 5$, then multiplying both sides by $-1$ gives $-3 > -5$. The inequality reverses because the order of numbers reverses when you reflect them across zero.

**Deep Dive — Compound Inequalities:**

A compound inequality like $2 < 3x + 5 < 11$ must be solved by performing operations to *all three parts simultaneously*:

$$2 < 3x + 5 < 11$$
$$-3 < 3x < 6$$
$$-1 < x < 2$$

The solution is the interval $(-1, 2)$.

**Trap Recognition:** The ACT frequently tests whether students correctly handle the "and" vs. "or" distinction. The compound inequality above is an "and" inequality — $x$ must satisfy *both* conditions simultaneously. An "or" inequality (like $x < -1$ or $x > 2$) has a solution that is the *union* of two intervals, not their intersection.

### Annotated Problem Series 26–30: Systems of Linear Equations

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

A system of two linear equations in two variables can have:
- **Exactly one solution** (the lines intersect at one point) — most common
- **No solution** (the lines are parallel) — the system is "inconsistent"
- **Infinitely many solutions** (the lines are identical) — the system is "dependent"

The three solution methods are:

**Graphing:** Best for visualizing, but imprecise unless the intersection falls on integer coordinates.

**Substitution:** Best when one variable has a coefficient of $\pm1$. Solve for that variable and substitute.

**Elimination:** Best when the coefficients of one variable are opposites (or can be made opposites by multiplying one or both equations).

**Deep Dive — The Substitution Method in Detail:**

Given: $y = 2x + 1$ and $3x + y = 10$

Step 1: Since $y$ is already isolated in the first equation, substitute $2x + 1$ for $y$ in the second equation:
$$3x + (2x + 1) = 10$$

Step 2: Solve for $x$:
$$5x + 1 = 10$$
$$5x = 9$$
$$x = \frac{9}{5}$$

Step 3: Substitute back to find $y$:
$$y = 2\left(\frac{9}{5}\right) + 1 = \frac{18}{5} + \frac{5}{5} = \frac{23}{5}$$

Step 4: The solution is $\left(\frac{9}{5}, \frac{23}{5}\right)$.

**Deep Dive — The Elimination Method in Detail:**

Given: $2x + 3y = 12$ and $5x - 3y = 9$

Step 1: Notice that the $y$-terms have coefficients $3$ and $-3$ — they're already opposites. Add the equations:
$$(2x + 3y) + (5x - 3y) = 12 + 9$$
$$7x = 21$$
$$x = 3$$

Step 2: Substitute $x = 3$ into either original equation:
$$2(3) + 3y = 12$$
$$6 + 3y = 12$$
$$3y = 6$$
$$y = 2$$

Step 3: The solution is $(3, 2)$.

**When the coefficients aren't already opposites:** Multiply one or both equations to create opposite coefficients. For $3x + 2y = 7$ and $2x + 5y = 12$, multiply the first equation by $-2$ and the second by $3$ to get $-6x - 4y = -14$ and $6x + 15y = 36$. Adding eliminates $x$.

**Trap Recognition:** The most common trap on systems problems is solving for one variable and stopping. If the question asks for the value of $x + y$ (or $xy$, or $x - y$), you need *both* values. The ACT includes the value of $x$ alone as a distractor.

### Annotated Problem Series 31–35: Polynomial Operations

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Polynomial problems on the ACT typically involve:
- **Addition/Subtraction:** Combine like terms (same variable, same exponent).
- **Multiplication:** Distribute each term of the first polynomial to each term of the second.
- **Factoring:** Reverse of multiplication — find the GCF, recognize special patterns.

**Deep Dive — Special Factoring Patterns:**

These three patterns appear with extremely high frequency and must be recognized instantly:

**Difference of Squares:** $a^2 - b^2 = (a+b)(a-b)$

**Perfect Square Trinomial (Positive):** $a^2 + 2ab + b^2 = (a+b)^2$

**Perfect Square Trinomial (Negative):** $a^2 - 2ab + b^2 = (a-b)^2$

**Why these matter:** The ACT uses them as "hidden" structures. A problem might present $x^2 - 16$ and expect you to recognize it as $(x+4)(x-4)$ in a single step, rather than treating it as a quadratic to be solved.

**Deep Dive — The Zero Product Property:**

If $A \cdot B = 0$, then $A = 0$ or $B = 0$ (or both).

This is the *only* way to solve a factored polynomial equation. You CANNOT set each factor equal to zero individually unless the product equals something other than zero.

**Trap Recognition:** If you have $(x-3)(x+2) = 6$, you CANNOT conclude $x - 3 = 6$ or $x + 2 = 6$. You must first expand, then set equal to zero: $x^2 - x - 6 = 6 \Rightarrow x^2 - x - 12 = 0 \Rightarrow (x-4)(x+3) = 0 \Rightarrow x = 4$ or $x = -3$.

---

## 10.3: Intermediate Algebra (Problems 36–55)

### Problem Category Overview

Intermediate algebra problems constitute approximately 15–20% of the test. They involve quadratics, exponents, radicals, rational expressions, and absolute value. These problems test deeper algebraic reasoning and the ability to select appropriate solution methods.

### Annotated Problem Series 36–42: Quadratic Equations

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Every quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ can be solved by one or more of the following methods:

1. **Factoring** (when the quadratic factors nicely over integers)
2. **Quadratic Formula** (always works)
3. **Square Root Method** (when $b = 0$, i.e., $ax^2 + c = 0$)
4. **Completing the Square** (rarely the fastest, but conceptually important)

The *fastest* method depends on the specific equation. Train yourself to recognize which method to apply.

**Deep Dive — The Quadratic Formula and the Discriminant:**

$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$

The expression under the radical, $b^2 - 4ac$, is called the **discriminant**, and it tells you the *nature* of the solutions:

| Discriminant | Nature of Solutions | Graph |
|---|---|---|
| $b^2 - 4ac > 0$ | Two distinct real solutions | Parabola crosses $x$-axis twice |
| $b^2 - 4ac = 0$ | One repeated real solution | Parabola touches $x$-axis at vertex |
| $b^2 - 4ac < 0$ | No real solutions (two complex) | Parabola never touches $x$-axis |

**Why this matters for the ACT:** The discriminant allows you to answer questions about the *number* of solutions without actually solving the equation. This is frequently tested and saves enormous time.

**Deep Dive — Sum and Product of Roots:**

For $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ with roots $r_1$ and $r_2$:

$$r_1 + r_2 = -\frac{b}{a}$$
$$r_1 \cdot r_2 = \frac{c}{a}$$

These relationships (Vieta's formulas) allow you to find the sum or product of the roots *without solving the equation*. The ACT uses this as a time-saving device.

**Example:** If $2x^2 - 5x + 3 = 0$ has roots $r_1$ and $r_2$, then $r_1 + r_2 = \frac{5}{2}$ and $r_1 \cdot r_2 = \frac{3}{2}$.

**Trap Recognition:** The sum of roots is $-\frac{b}{a}$, not $\frac{b}{a}$. The negative sign is the single most common error. Remember: the sum is *negative* $b$ over $a$.

### Annotated Problem Series 43–47: Exponent Rules

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Exponent problems on the ACT test a small set of rules applied in combination. The rules are:

| Rule | Expression | Result |
|---|---|---|
| Product | $x^a \cdot x^b$ | $x^{a+b}$ |
| Quotient | $\dfrac{x^a}{x^b}$ | $x^{a-b}$ |
| Power of a Power | $(x^a)^b$ | $x^{a \cdot b}$ |
| Power of a Product | $(xy)^a$ | $x^a \cdot y^a$ |
| Power of a Quotient | $\left(\dfrac{x}{y}\right)^a$ | $\dfrac{x^a}{y^a}$ |
| Zero Exponent | $x^0$ | $1$ (for $x \neq 0$) |
| Negative Exponent | $x^{-a}$ | $\dfrac{1}{x^a}$ |
| Fractional Exponent | $x^{a/b}$ | $\sqrt[b]{x^a}$ |

**Deep Dive — The Negative Exponent Trap:**

$x^{-3} = \frac{1}{x^3}$, NOT $-x^3$. A negative exponent means *reciprocal*, NOT *negative value*. The ACT includes $-x^3$ as a distractor for $x^{-3}$.

Similarly, $\frac{1}{x^{-2}} = x^2$. When a negative exponent appears in the denominator, moving it to the numerator makes it positive.

**Deep Dive — Fractional Exponents:**

$x^{1/2} = \sqrt{x}$ and $x^{1/3} = \sqrt[3]{x}$. The denominator of the fractional exponent tells you the *root*, and the numerator tells you the *power*.

$x^{3/2} = (\sqrt{x})^3$ or $\sqrt{x^3}$ — both are equivalent, but one form may be easier to compute depending on the value.

### Annotated Problem Series 48–52: Absolute Value

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

The absolute value function $|x|$ measures the *distance* from $x$ to $0$ on the number line. This geometric interpretation is the key to solving absolute value equations and inequalities.

**Deep Dive — Solving Absolute Value Equations:**

$|A| = B$ (where $B > 0$) means $A = B$ or $A = -B$.

**Why?** Because both $B$ and $-B$ are at distance $B$ from $0$.

**Critical check:** After solving, always verify that your solutions satisfy the original equation. If $B < 0$, the equation $|A| = B$ has *no solution* (absolute value can never be negative).

**Deep Dive — Solving Absolute Value Inequalities:**

$|A| < B$ means $-B < A < B$ (A is within B units of 0)

$|A| > B$ means $A < -B$ or $A > B$ (A is more than B units from 0)

**Trap Recognition:** When solving $|2x - 3| > 7$, students often write $2x - 3 > 7$ and forget the second case $2x - 3 < -7$. The "or" case is essential for "greater than" inequalities.

### Annotated Problem Series 53–55: Rational Expressions

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Rational expressions are fractions with polynomials in the numerator and/or denominator. The key operations are:

**Multiplication:** Factor everything possible, cancel common factors, then multiply straight across.

$$\frac{x^2 - 4}{x+3} \cdot \frac{x+3}{x-2} = \frac{(x+2)(x-2)}{x+3} \cdot \frac{x+3}{x-2} = x+2$$

**Division:** Flip the second fraction and multiply.

$$\frac{x^2 - 1}{x+2} \div \frac{x-1}{x^2 - 4} = \frac{(x+1)(x-1)}{x+2} \cdot \frac{(x+2)(x-2)}{x-1} = (x+1)(x-2)$$

**Trap Recognition:** You can only cancel *factors*, not *terms*. In $\frac{x+2}{x+3}$, you cannot cancel the $x$'s to get $\frac{2}{3}$. The $x$'s are *terms* of a sum, not *factors* of a product.

---

## 10.4: Coordinate Geometry (Problems 56–70)

### Problem Category Overview

Coordinate geometry problems constitute approximately 12–15% of the test. They involve the coordinate plane, lines, parabolas, circles, and transformations. These problems test the ability to move between algebraic equations and geometric graphs.

### Annotated Problem Series 56–60: Lines and Slope

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

The slope of a line measures its *steepness* and *direction*. It is defined as:

$$m = \frac{\text{rise}}{\text{run}} = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$$

**Deep Dive — Slope Interpretation:**

| Slope Value | Meaning |
|---|---|
| $m > 0$ | Line rises from left to right |
| $m < 0$ | Line falls from left to right |
| $m = 0$ | Horizontal line |
| $m$ undefined | Vertical line |
| $|m| > 1$ | Steeper than 45° |
| $|m| < 1$ | Less steep than 45° |

**Deep Dive — Parallel and Perpendicular Lines:**

**Parallel lines** have the *same slope*. If line $L_1$ has slope $m_1$ and line $L_2$ has slope $m_2$, then $L_1 \parallel L_2$ if and only if $m_1 = m_2$.

**Perpendicular lines** have slopes that are *negative reciprocals*. $L_1 \perp L_2$ if and only if $m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$.

**Critical extension:** If a line has slope $\frac{3}{4}$, then:
- A parallel line has slope $\frac{3}{4}$
- A perpendicular line has slope $-\frac{4}{3}$

**Trap Recognition:** The most common trap is confusing "same slope" (parallel) with "same $y$-intercept" (identical lines). Two lines with the same slope and different $y$-intercepts are parallel but not identical. Two lines with the same slope and same $y$-intercept are the *same line*.

### Annotated Problem Series 61–65: Distance, Midpoint, and the Coordinate Plane

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

**Distance Formula:** $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$

**Midpoint Formula:** $M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$

**Deep Dive — The Midpoint Formula Derivation:**

The midpoint's $x$-coordinate is the *average* of the two endpoints' $x$-coordinates, and similarly for $y$. This makes geometric sense: the midpoint is exactly halfway between the two points, and "halfway" means "average."

**Application:** If you know the midpoint and one endpoint, you can find the other endpoint by setting up equations. If $M = (4, 7)$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AB}$ and $A = (1, 3)$, then:

$$\frac{1 + x_B}{2} = 4 \Rightarrow x_B = 7$$
$$\frac{3 + y_B}{2} = 7 \Rightarrow y_B = 11$$

So $B = (7, 11)$.

### Annotated Problem Series 66–70: Circles in the Coordinate Plane

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

The standard equation of a circle with center $(h, k)$ and radius $r$ is:

$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

**Deep Dive — Recognizing Circle Equations:**

The equation $(x-3)^2 + (y+2)^2 = 25$ represents a circle with center $(3, -2)$ and radius $5$.

Note the signs: $(x - h)$ means the center's $x$-coordinate is $h$, so $(x - 3)$ gives center $x = 3$. But $(y + 2) = (y - (-2))$, so the center's $y$-coordinate is $-2$.

**Trap Recognition:** The ACT frequently tests whether students correctly identify the center coordinates, especially the signs. $(x + 1)^2 + (y - 4)^2 = 9$ has center $(-1, 4)$, NOT $(1, -4)$ or $(1, 4)$.

**Deep Dive — The Expanded Form:**

A circle equation may be given in expanded form: $x^2 + y^2 - 6x + 4y - 12 = 0$. To find the center and radius, complete the square:

$$x^2 - 6x + y^2 + 4y = 12$$
$$(x^2 - 6x + 9) + (y^2 + 4y + 4) = 12 + 9 + 4$$
$$(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 25$$

Center: $(3, -2)$, radius: $5$.

---

## 10.5: Geometry (Problems 71–90)

### Problem Category Overview

Geometry problems constitute approximately 15–20% of the test. They involve plane geometry, solid geometry, and basic trigonometry. These problems test spatial reasoning, formula recall, and the ability to extract geometric information from verbal descriptions.

### Annotated Problem Series 71–78: Triangles

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Triangles are the most frequently tested geometric shape on the ACT. Every triangle problem can be approached through a small set of fundamental principles:

**Angle Sum:** The three interior angles of any triangle sum to $180°$.

**Exterior Angle Theorem:** An exterior angle of a triangle equals the sum of the two remote interior angles.

**Triangle Inequality:** The sum of any two sides must be *greater than* the third side. Equivalently, the third side must be between the positive difference and the sum of the other two sides.

**Deep Dive — The Triangle Inequality in Action:**

If two sides of a triangle are 5 and 9, the third side $x$ must satisfy:

$$9 - 5 < x < 9 + 5$$
$$4 < x < 14$$

The third side must be strictly greater than 4 and strictly less than 14. The values 4 and 14 are excluded because they would produce a degenerate triangle (a straight line).

**Trap Recognition:** The ACT often asks for the *range* of possible values, and the trap is to include the endpoints. The inequality is strict: $x > 4$ and $x < 14$, not $x \geq 4$ and $x \leq 14$.

**Deep Dive — Special Right Triangles:**

The two special right triangles appear on virtually every ACT. Their side ratios must be memorized:

**45°-45°-90°:** The legs are equal, and the hypotenuse is $\sqrt{2}$ times a leg.
- If leg = $s$, then hypotenuse = $s\sqrt{2}$
- If hypotenuse = $h$, then leg = $\frac{h}{\sqrt{2}} = \frac{h\sqrt{2}}{2}$

**30°-60°-90°:** The sides are in ratio $1 : \sqrt{3} : 2$ (short leg : long leg : hypotenuse)
- If short leg = $s$, then long leg = $s\sqrt{3}$ and hypotenuse = $2s$
- If long leg = $L$, then short leg = $\frac{L}{\sqrt{3}} = \frac{L\sqrt{3}}{3}$ and hypotenuse = $\frac{2L}{\sqrt{3}} = \frac{2L\sqrt{3}}{3}$

**Why these matter:** The ACT frequently embeds these triangles in complex figures — a 30°-60°-90° triangle inside a rectangle, a 45°-45°-90° triangle formed by a diagonal of a square. Recognizing these patterns instantly saves enormous time.

**Deep Dive — Similar Triangles:**

Two triangles are similar if:
- **AA (Angle-Angle):** Two angles of one triangle equal two angles of the other (the third angles must also be equal since the sum is 180°)
- **SSS (Side-Side-Side):** All three sides are proportional
- **SAS (Side-Angle-Side):** Two sides are proportional and the included angle is congruent

**The key consequence:** If two triangles are similar, *all corresponding linear dimensions* are in the same ratio (the *scale factor*). But *areas* are in the *square* of the scale factor.

If two similar triangles have a scale factor of $3:5$ (larger to smaller), then:
- The ratio of corresponding sides is $3:5$
- The ratio of corresponding altitudes is $3:5$
- The ratio of perimeters is $3:5$
- The ratio of areas is $9:25$

**Trap Recognition:** The area ratio trap is extremely common. Students often use the linear scale factor for area problems. Always square the scale factor for areas, cube it for volumes.

### Annotated Problem Series 79–84: Circles

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

**Deep Dive — Circle Formulas:**

| Measure | Formula |
|---|---|
| Area | $A = \pi r^2$ |
| Circumference | $C = 2\pi r = \pi d$ |
| Arc Length | $\dfrac{\theta}{360°} \times 2\pi r$ (where $\theta$ is the central angle in degrees) |
| Sector Area | $\dfrac{\theta}{360°} \times \pi r^2$ |

**Deep Dive — Inscribed Angles:**

An inscribed angle is an angle whose vertex lies on the circle and whose sides are chords of the circle. The measure of an inscribed angle is *half* the measure of its intercepted arc.

**Thales' Theorem:** An inscribed angle that intercepts a diameter is always a right angle (90°). This means any triangle inscribed in a circle with the diameter as one side is a right triangle.

**Deep Dive — Inscribed Figures:**

The ACT frequently tests regular polygons or special triangles inscribed in circles, or circles inscribed in polygons. The key relationships are:

**Square inscribed in a circle:** The diagonal of the square equals the diameter of the circle. If the circle has radius $r$, the square has side $r\sqrt{2}$ and area $2r^2$.

**Circle inscribed in a square:** The diameter of the circle equals the side of the square. If the square has side $s$, the circle has radius $s/2$ and area $\pi s^2/4$.

**Equilateral triangle inscribed in a circle:** The radius of the circumscribed circle is $r = \frac{s}{\sqrt{3}}$, where $s$ is the side of the triangle.

### Annotated Problem Series 85–90: Solid Geometry and Trigonometry

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

**Deep Dive — Volume Formulas:**

| Solid | Volume |
|---|---|
| Rectangular prism | $V = lwh$ |
| Cylinder | $V = \pi r^2 h$ |
| Sphere | $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ |
| Cone | $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ |
| Pyramid | $V = \frac{1}{3}Bh$ (where $B$ is base area) |

**Critical insight:** The volume of a cone is exactly $\frac{1}{3}$ the volume of a cylinder with the same base and height. The volume of a pyramid is exactly $\frac{1}{3}$ the volume of a prism with the same base and height. This relationship is frequently tested.

**Deep Dive — Scaling and Volume:**

If all linear dimensions of a solid are multiplied by a factor of $k$:
- Surface area is multiplied by $k^2$
- Volume is multiplied by $k^3$

**Example:** If a sphere's radius is doubled, its volume increases by a factor of $2^3 = 8$, not 2 or 4.

**Deep Dive — SOH CAH TOA:**

The three basic trigonometric ratios for a right triangle:

$$\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$

$$\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$

$$\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$$

**When to use trig on the ACT:** Trigonometry is used when you know one acute angle and one side of a right triangle and need to find another side, OR when you know two sides and need to find an angle.

**The connection to similar triangles:** The reason trig works is that all right triangles with the same acute angle are similar, so the ratios of corresponding sides are constant. $\sin(53°) = 0.80$ means that in *any* right triangle with a 53° angle, the ratio of the opposite side to the hypotenuse is always 0.80.

**Trap Recognition:** The most common trig error is using the wrong ratio. Label the sides relative to the given angle: *opposite* (across from the angle), *adjacent* (next to the angle, not the hypotenuse), and *hypotenuse* (always the longest side, across from the right angle). Then identify which ratio involves the known and unknown quantities.

---

## 10.6: Functions (Problems 91–97)

### Problem Category Overview

Functions problems constitute approximately 12–15% of the test. They test function notation, domain and range, graphing, and transformations of functions.

### Annotated Problem Series 91–94: Function Notation and Evaluation

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

The notation $f(x)$ does NOT mean $f$ times $x$. It means "the output of the function $f$ when the input is $x$." Think of $f(x)$ as the $y$-value associated with $x$.

**Deep Dive — Evaluating Functions:**

If $f(x) = 3x^2 - 2x + 1$, then:
- $f(2) = 3(2)^2 - 2(2) + 1 = 12 - 4 + 1 = 9$
- $f(-1) = 3(-1)^2 - 2(-1) + 1 = 3 + 2 + 1 = 6$
- $f(a) = 3a^2 - 2a + 1$ (substitute $a$ for $x$)

**Deep Dive — Composition of Functions:**

$f(g(x))$, read as "$f$ of $g$ of $x$", means: first apply $g$ to $x$, then apply $f$ to the result.

If $f(x) = x^2$ and $g(x) = x + 3$, then:
- $f(g(x)) = f(x+3) = (x+3)^2 = x^2 + 6x + 9$
- $g(f(x)) = g(x^2) = x^2 + 3$

**Critical insight:** $f(g(x)) \neq g(f(x))$ in general. Function composition is NOT commutative.

### Annotated Problem Series 95–97: Graph Transformations

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

Given $y = f(x)$, the following transformations apply:

| Transformation | Effect |
|---|---|
| $y = f(x) + k$ | Shift **up** by $k$ units |
| $y = f(x) - k$ | Shift **down** by $k$ units |
| $y = f(x + h)$ | Shift **left** by $h$ units |
| $y = f(x - h)$ | Shift **right** by $h$ units |
| $y = -f(x)$ | Reflect across the **$x$-axis** |
| $y = f(-x)$ | Reflect across the **$y$-axis** |
| $y = a \cdot f(x)$ (where $|a| > 1$) | **Vertical stretch** by factor $a$ |
| $y = a \cdot f(x)$ (where $0 < |a| < 1$) | **Vertical compression** by factor $a$ |

**Deep Dive — The Horizontal Shift Trap:**

$y = f(x + 3)$ shifts the graph **left** by 3 units, NOT right. This is the single most common transformation error. The logic: $f(x+3)$ reaches the same output at $x = 0$ that $f(x)$ reaches at $x = 3$. So the point $(3, f(3))$ on the original graph moves to $(0, f(3))$ on the new graph — a shift to the left.

**Mnemonic:** Horizontal shifts work "backwards" — $f(x+h)$ goes left, $f(x-h)$ goes right. Vertical shifts work "normally" — $f(x)+k$ goes up, $f(x)-k$ goes down.

---

## 10.7: Statistics & Probability (Problems 98–100)

### Problem Category Overview

Statistics and probability problems constitute approximately 8–12% of the test. They test data interpretation, measures of center and spread, and basic probability calculations.

### Annotated Problem Series 98–99: Data Analysis

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

**Deep Dive — Mean vs. Median and Outliers:**

The **mean** (arithmetic average) is sensitive to outliers. A single extremely large or small value can pull the mean significantly in that direction.

The **median** (middle value when data is ordered) is resistant to outliers. It depends only on the position of values, not their magnitude.

**Skew direction tells you the mean-median relationship:**
- **Right-skewed** (tail extends right): Mean > Median (the large outliers pull the mean up)
- **Left-skewed** (tail extends left): Mean < Median (the small outliers pull the mean down)
- **Symmetric**: Mean = Median

**Deep Dive — Standard Deviation:**

Standard deviation measures the *spread* or *dispersion* of data around the mean. Key facts:
- Higher standard deviation = more spread out
- Lower standard deviation = more clustered around the mean
- Standard deviation is always $\geq 0$
- Standard deviation is sensitive to outliers (like the mean)

**You do NOT need to calculate standard deviation on the ACT.** You need to understand what it means and compare standard deviations across data sets.

**Deep Dive — Reading Graphs:**

| Graph Type | Best For | Shows |
|---|---|---|
| Histogram | Quantitative data | Frequency distribution |
| Bar Graph | Categorical data | Comparison of categories |
| Pie Chart | Categorical data | Parts of a whole |
| Dot Plot | Quantitative data | Individual data points, distribution shape |
| Box Plot | Quantitative data | Five-number summary, IQR, outliers |
| Stem-and-Leaf | Quantitative data | Individual data points, distribution shape |

**Trap Recognition:** Histograms group data into bins, so you cannot determine the exact mean or median from a histogram alone. The ACT includes answer choices that assume you can read exact values from histogram bars.

### Annotated Problem Series 100: Probability

**Strategic Commentary Framework:**

**Deep Dive — Basic Probability:**

$$P(\text{event}) = \frac{\text{Number of favorable outcomes}}{\text{Total number of possible outcomes}}$$

**Key facts:**
- $0 \leq P(E) \leq 1$ for any event
- $P(E) + P(\text{not } E) = 1$
- If $P(E) = 0$, the event is impossible
- If $P(E) = 1$, the event is certain

**Deep Dive — "And" vs. "Or" Probability:**

**AND** (both events occur): $P(A \text{ and } B) = P(A) \times P(B)$ for *independent* events.

**OR** (at least one event occurs): $P(A \text{ or } B) = P(A) + P(B) - P(A \text{ and } B)$.

The subtraction of $P(A \text{ and } B)$ prevents double-counting the overlap.

**Deep Dive — Independent vs. Dependent Events:**

**Independent events:** The outcome of one does not affect the other. $P(A \text{ and } B) = P(A) \times P(B)$.

**Dependent events:** The outcome of one affects the other. $P(A \text{ and } B) = P(A) \times P(B|A)$ (where $P(B|A)$ is the probability of $B$ given that $A$ occurred).

**The "without replacement" scenario:** If you draw a card from a deck and don't replace it, the second draw is dependent on the first. This is the most common dependent probability scenario on the ACT.

**Trap Recognition:** The most common probability trap is confusing "and" with "or." "What is the probability of drawing a heart AND a face card?" is $\frac{3}{52}$ (only the Jack, Queen, and King of hearts). "What is the probability of drawing a heart OR a face card?" is $\frac{13 + 12 - 3}{52} = \frac{22}{52} = \frac{11}{26}$.

---

## 10.8: The Meta-Analysis — What Your Errors Reveal

### The Error Taxonomy

Every wrong answer on the ACT Math section falls into exactly one of the following categories. Identifying which category your errors belong to is the single most powerful diagnostic tool available to you.

**Category 1 — Conceptual Error:**
You didn't know the formula, theorem, or principle needed to solve the problem.
*Fix:* Return to the relevant chapter in this guide. Study the concept until you can explain it to someone else.

**Category 2 — Procedural Error:**
You knew the concept but made an algebraic or arithmetic mistake during execution.
*Fix:* Slow down. Write every step. Check your work by substituting your answer back into the original equation.

**Category 3 — Strategic Error:**
You knew the concept and could execute the math, but you chose the slowest or most error-prone method.
*Fix:* Study the alternate approaches in this guide. Practice recognizing the fastest path for each problem type.

**Category 4 — Comprehension Error:**
You misread the problem, solved for the wrong variable, or missed a critical constraint.
*Fix:* Underline what the question is asking for. Circle constraints. Read the problem twice before starting.

**Category 5 — Careless Error:**
You dropped a negative sign, copied a number wrong, or bubbled the wrong answer.
*Fix:* Develop a checking routine. Always ask: "Does an answer of [your answer] make sense in the context of the problem?"

**Category 6 — Time Error:**
You ran out of time and had to guess on problems you could have solved.
*Fix:* Practice pacing. Use the Now/Later/Never system. Bank time on easy questions.

### The Error Log Template

For every missed question, record:

| Field | Entry |
|---|---|
| Problem # | _____ |
| Topic | _____ |
| Error Category (1–6) | _____ |
| What I Did | _____ |
| What I Should Have Done | _____ |
| The Fix | _____ |

Review this log weekly. If 80% of your errors are Category 1, you need content review. If 80% are Category 2 or 5, you need discipline and checking routines. If 80% are Category 3, you need strategy work. If 80% are Category 6, you need pacing practice.

---

## 10.9: The Final 48 Hours — Strategic Review Protocol

In the two days before the test, you should NOT be learning new material. Your focus should be on:

1. **Reviewing your error log.** Read through every entry. Remind yourself of the traps you've fallen into and the fixes you've identified.

2. **Reviewing your formula sheet.** Go through every formula in this guide. For each one, ask yourself: "When would I use this? What does each variable represent?"

3. **Taking a short timed set.** Do 15–20 questions at a comfortable pace, focusing on accuracy and process, not speed. This keeps your skills warm without causing fatigue.

4. **Preparing your materials.** Pack your bag: admission ticket, ID, approved calculator with fresh batteries, pencils, snack, water.

5. **Getting sleep.** The night before the test, sleep is more valuable than any last-minute studying. Your brain needs rest to perform at its peak.

---

## 10.10: The Mindset Framework

The ACT Math section is not primarily a test of mathematical knowledge — it is a test of mathematical *performance* under time pressure. The students who score highest are not necessarily the ones who know the most math; they are the ones who:

1. **Know what they know cold.** They don't hesitate on fundamentals.
2. **Know what they don't know.** They flag hard problems and return to them, rather than wasting time.
3. **Never leave a blank.** They always guess, even when they have no idea.
4. **Check what they're solving for.** They read the question carefully and answer what was actually asked.
5. **Stay calm under pressure.** They don't let a difficult problem rattle them for the rest of the section.

These are skills, and like all skills, they can be developed through deliberate, focused practice. The 100 annotated problems in this chapter — and the strategic framework surrounding them — are designed to build exactly these capabilities.

---

*End of Chapter 10*
